# Reshimos

## Menorah

The concept of the Menorah is that it represents the illumination of the world through the light of the Torah and mitzvos. As it is written, "For a mitzvah is a candle, and Torah is light" (Mishlei 6:23). The Menorah, specifically, signifies the refinement of the physical world, transforming it into a dwelling place for Hashem. This is achieved through the avodah of the Jewish people, who, through their dedication and mesiras nefesh, draw down the Ohr Ein Sof into the lower worlds. Just as the lamps of the Menorah were kindled to shed light, so too must every Yid kindle the "lamp of Hashem" within their own soul and within the world at large, ensuring that the darkness of galus is dispelled by the radiance of Elokus.

## The Hadlakas Neiros. 99. Paris.

## A

In response to your question in your letter to explain the various opinions regarding the order of the *hadlakas haneiros* (lighting of the lamps) in the *Beis Hamikdash* and their underlying reasons, as well as to explain the matter from the perspective of *drush* (Chassidic discourse), based on what is explained in *Chassidus* regarding this.

**The opinion of the Rambam’s Commentary on the Mishnah regarding the lighting of the Menorah in the Beis Hamikdash**

We learned in *Maseches Tamid*, end of chapter 3: "Whoever merited the *dishun* (clearing) of the Menorah would enter and find two eastern lamps (some versions say: western lamps) still burning. He would clear the others and leave these burning in their place. If he found them extinguished, he would clear them and light them from the [other] burning ones, and afterwards clear the remainder."

And there, beginning of chapter 6: "Whoever merited the *dishun* of the Menorah would enter and find two eastern lamps (some versions say: western lamps) burning. He would clear the eastern one and leave the western one burning, for from it he would light the Menorah in the evening. If he found it extinguished, he would clear it and light it from the *Mizbeach HaOlah* (Altar of Burnt Offering)."

## 2

The approach of the Rambam in his Commentary on the Mishnah regarding these Mishnayos is as follows: The Tanna of Tamid holds that the lamps were positioned from east to west, and the wicks of all the lamps faced west, toward the Kodesh HaKodashim. This is the meaning of the verse, "Toward the face of the Menorah" — meaning, toward the Kodesh HaKodashim. Based on this, the verse (Bamidbar 8:2), "The seven lamps shall give light," is easily understood; on the face of it, it should have said, "The six lamps shall give light." See the Re'em on Parshas Beha'aloscha regarding this. When he entered and found the two easternmost lamps* burning, he would clean them and light the remaining five lamps — for according to the Rambam, he would light the lamps in the morning as well, since the *hatavas* (the cleaning and preparation)

----------
*) See the Mizrachi on Beha'aloscha to check if he writes that the Rambam’s version of the text says "westernmost," for on the face of it, the opposite is proven in the Commentary on the Mishnah.

## 3

The *hatavas* (preparation) of the lamps refers to both their cleaning and their lighting, as stated in the *Peirush HaMishnayos* and in *Hilchos Temidin U’Musafin* (chapter 3, halacha 12) of the *Yad*. This also includes the *ner hama’aravi* (western lamp), which, according to the *Peirush HaMishnayos*, is the lamp adjacent to the *Kodesh HaKodashim*. It is included in the general procedure. The *kohen* then pauses to perform another *avodah*, enters a second time, and cleans and lights the two eastern lamps. However, if upon entering the first time he finds that the two eastern lamps have gone out, he cleans and lights them, as well as the three lamps adjacent to them. He then pauses to perform another *avodah*, enters a second time, and if the two lamps he has not yet prepared—which are the westernmost lamps—are still burning, he cleans the eastern ones and leaves the western ones burning.

## 4

Rather, he adds oil to it so that it will burn until the evening. But if the *ner hama'aravi* had already gone out, he cleans it as well and kindles it from the *mizbach ha'olah*.

And if so, the interpretation of the *mishnah* is—and through this, several points of precision are resolved:

He enters and finds the two eastern candles burning; he cleans the (remaining) ones (and from this we know that in the first entry he prepares the five candles) and leaves those two in their place. He finds them extinguished (the two eastern candles); he cleans them and kindles them from the ones that are burning (and from this we know two things: 1. That the *hadlakas haneiros* is also in the morning. And the other commentators, who hold that *hadlakas haneiros* is only in the evening, are very hard-pressed regarding the reason for the kindling of the two candles at this time. See *Shu"t HaRashba*, sections 69 and 79, cited in *Tosafos Yom Tov*).

## 5

2. That he lights the lamps from a lamp that is already lit. See Rambam, Hilchos Temidin U’Musafin, Chapter 3, Halacha 13, and the Ra’avad’s glosses at the end of that chapter.

And now, he also prepares the three lamps adjacent to these two. This is not explicitly detailed in the Mishnah, for it has already taught us at the beginning that he must prepare the five lamps during the first entry. And "afterwards" (meaning after a period of time, after an interruption in the avodah—which is the precise meaning of the word "afterwards" in the Mishnah; for according to other commentators, this word is superfluous, as it should have simply said: "If he found them extinguished, he cleans them and lights them from those that are lit, and cleans the rest") he cleans the rest (that which remains after the first preparation, namely, the two lamps on the western side).

The details of the law regarding the cleaning of the remaining lamps are explained in their proper place, namely, at the beginning of Chapter 6, for here they were not mentioned.

## 6

Incidentally. This is what is explained at the beginning of the sixth chapter: "He who merited the cleaning of the Menorah" (meaning the one who prepared the five lamps, as is proven in Yoma 25a in the Mishnah, that one Kohen prepares all seven lamps) "enters and finds the two western lamps" (see Lechem Mishneh on Hilchos Temidin U’musafin, Chapter 3, Halacha 13, where it appears that this is indeed the correct version of the Rambam in this Mishnah, and our Mishnah refers to the end of the third chapter: "If he found them extinguished." And it comes to explain here how he cleans the rest, for if he found both of them) "burning, he cleans" (meaning he prepares it, according to the law of any other lamp he finds burning; and see further on this) "the eastern one" (meaning the lamp that is to the east relative to its fellow lamp that is closer to the Kodesh HaKodashim) "and leaves the western one burning" (so that he does not extinguish it and light it again, but rather only adds oil to it), "from which he would light the Menorah in the evening." "If he found them extinguished, he cleans them and lights them from the burning ones."

## 7

(And not from the other lamps, even though they are all burning at this time, according to the Rambam’s interpretation that one lights the lamps in the morning as well, contrary to the aforementioned objections. This also serves to clarify the Mishnah at the end of the third chapter of Tamid: that which is taught there, "he cleans the others," which includes the western lamp, does not mean that all the lamps are equal. For the western lamp is lit from the *mizbach ha’olah* and not from another lamp. And since we only find the western lamp to be different from the other lamps, we know this by implication, and it was not necessary to state explicitly that if, upon entering, he found only the western lamp burning, he cleans and lights the eastern one and leaves the western one burning. Likewise, if he found only the eastern lamp burning, he cleans the eastern one and cleans and lights the western lamp from the *mizbach ha’olah*. Similarly, if upon entering the first time he found two eastern lamps burning, such that he cleans them upon his second entry, the details of this did not need to be specified, because only the western lamp possesses a unique status, as mentioned above.)

## 8

It requires further study, according to the Rambam’s Commentary on the Mishnah, what the distinction is between the cleaning of the eastern lamp and the western lamp when he finds both of them burning. For every lamp that he finds has not gone out, he does not extinguish and relight; this is not specific to the western lamp. Furthermore, he also repairs the western lamp and adds oil, etc., if he finds it burning, as he writes in his Commentary on the Mishnah. In what way, then, do they differ from one another?

Perhaps it can be suggested that the Rambam does not hold like the Rashba—based on the Sifri, as cited in the Tosafot Yom Tov—that there is a prohibition against extinguishing a lamp that one finds burning. Rather, he holds that there is no obligation to extinguish it and relight it, and one fulfills the mitzvah of *hatavas haneiros* (the preparation of the lamps) through their repair alone, even if they are already burning. (See *Yoma* 24b: "A *zar* [non-kohen] who arranged the Menorah is liable... added a wick is liable... added oil is liable... lit is liable; lighting is not *avodah*." And in *Yad*, *Hilchos Bias Hamikdash* 9:7, he writes that lighting is valid when performed by a *zar*.) Thus, he explains that the eastern lamp is cleaned, and in this...

## 9

Regarding the conditions: whether one wishes to extinguish it and relight it, or whether one does not extinguish it at all—this is not the case for the Western Lamp, which must be left burning. Alternatively, one could say that the precision in the Mishnah lies in the word "clears" (*medashen*) used regarding the Eastern Lamp, meaning that one must remove the oil from the lamp (the wick cannot be removed, as it is burning), whereas for the Western Lamp, one merely adds oil to it, and there is no clearing (*dishun*) involved at all. However, it requires study why the law regarding the oil should differ between these two lamps.

In any case, it requires study: what is the reason that because "from it one lights the Menorah in the evening," one should therefore not extinguish the Western Lamp and relight it? Even if one were to extinguish it and relight it, one could still light the Menorah from it in the evening.

Furthermore, it is difficult to understand what is written in the Commentary on the Mishnah, that for the Western Lamp one adds oil, which implies that the oil remaining in the lamp is not removed. If so, regarding the "seven measures" that were in the Temple, there would be many more measures required, as per the difficulty raised in the Gemara, Menachos (88b).

## Explaining the reasoning behind the commentary of the Maggid and its laws

## 10

And yet, several details still require clarification.

a) What is the reasoning of the Rambam, who explains that the *ner hama'aravi* is the lamp adjacent to the *Kodesh HaKodashim*—which is also the implication of Rashi, s.v. *midichtiv* (Menachos 98b)—and in this he disagrees with Rashi, s.v. *uvah* (Shabbos 22b), s.v. *v'hayah* (Yoma 39a), s.v. *ner* (Megillah 21b), and s.v. *mimena* (Menachos 86b) [and it has already been noted that Rashi contradicts his own opinion*—Tiferes Yisrael to Tamid, end of chapter 3], as well as Tosafos, s.v. *ul'heitiv* (Yoma 71a), and Tosafos in Menachos there, and the Ra'avad (see...

----------
*) See Rashal on Shabbos.

## 11

Tosafos (s.v. *U'leheitiv*, Yoma 71a) and Tosafos (Menachos, ibid.), the Ra'avad (see his *Hasagos* on *Hilchos Temidin U'Musafin*, Chapter 3, Halacha 13, and his commentary printed in the new Shas), the Ramban (in his *Chiddushim* to Shabbos 22b*, and in his commentary on the Torah, Shemos 27:20), and the Rashba (in the aforementioned *She'eilos U'Teshuvos*).

----------
*) See what the *Ner HaMa'aravi* is, according to his version in *Tamid*.

## 12

There are several reasons for this:

1) In Menachos (98b), it says regarding the *ner hama'aravi*: "He shall set it in order before Hashem," which implies that the others are not "before Hashem." This clearly indicates that the *ner hama'aravi* is unique among all the lamps in that it alone is "before Hashem." As Rashi states there: "‘He shall set it in order’... which implies that it is [specifically] it that is before Hashem, and not another that is before Hashem." Therefore, the Rambam explains that the *ner hama'aravi* is the one adjacent to the *Kodesh HaKodashim*. This is in contrast to the opinion of all those mentioned above—who hold that the *ner hama'aravi* is the second lamp from the east—for according to them, five of the seven lamps (the five western lamps) are more "before Hashem" (in terms of proximity to the *Kodesh HaKodashim*) than the second lamp from the east, which is called the "*ner hama'aravi*." In that case, it would only be "before Hashem" in relation to one single lamp (the lamp at the easternmost edge).

2) In addition to the fact that the unique lamp that is "before Hashem" is the one adjacent to the *Kodesh HaKodashim*, its very **name proves it**—that it is the one adjacent to the *Kodesh HaKodashim*. It is called (in the *Shas*, etc.) the "*ner hama'aravi*." Since the intent of the name is not to obscure but to clarify, it must be that it refers to the lamp that is [truly] western.

## 13

From all the lamps. This is unlike the aforementioned view, which must strain to explain that it is called this way based on the language of the Mishnah in Tamid, Chapter 6, Mishnah 1, where it calls it "western" relative to its neighbor.

3) Many of those who disagree with the Rambam regarding the interpretation of which lamp is the *Ner HaMa'aravi* are forced to explain that two lamps would remain burning all day—Tosafot in Yoma (who hold that this was the case even during the time of the miracle), the Ramban, and the Rashba—and the eastern one would be burning so that the lamp next to it—the *Ner HaMa'aravi*—would have its name upon it, even though the verse uses the singular, "he shall arrange it," and it is the same in every place in the Gemara in the singular, and there is not a single place where two lamps are mentioned.

## 14

They are burning. Regarding that which is stated in *Tamid* (end of chapter 3), see below. One must perforce say, according to their opinion, that this is because the eastern lamp is secondary to the western lamp. Furthermore, we arrive at an additional dispute—which is not mentioned anywhere in the Gemara—namely, that according to the one who holds that they were placed north and south, and the western lamp is the middle lamp (as will be explained below), only one lamp burns. But according to the one who holds that they were placed east and west, two lamps burn. This is not the case according to the Rambam, for that is a different matter entirely.

4) According to the opinion of those who disagree with the Rambam, it is difficult to understand why the miracle was initially established in the second lamp, rather than in the lamp closest to the Kodesh HaKodashim, which is "before Hashem" more than all the others. The Ra’avad—printed in *Shas* (8)—wrote a reason why the miracle occurred in the eastern lamp: because of the principle that "one does not bypass mitzvos" (and the reason it was not in the first lamp was so that it would be "before Hashem"). It is puzzling what the principle of "one does not bypass mitzvos" has to do with this; for that principle is only relevant to the Kohen who enters from the Ulam into the Heichal to light the lamps, and it is not found in any place that two lamps burn all day. And that which we learned in *Tamid* (end of chapter 3), "two eastern lamps are burning"—see below (section 5), where it is explained that this Mishnah can be interpreted in another way, as in the *Peirush HaMishnayos* of the Rambam. One must perforce say, according to their opinion, that the fact that two lamps burn, even though the verse is written in the singular, is because the eastern lamp is secondary to the western lamp.

Furthermore: according to their opinion, we arrive at an additional dispute—which is not mentioned anywhere in the Gemara—namely, that according to the one who holds that they were placed north and south, and the western lamp is the middle lamp (as will be explained below), only one lamp burns all day. But according to the one who holds that they were placed east and west, and the western lamp is the second from the east, two lamps burn all day.

All of this (the forced interpretation that two lamps were burning all day, and adding a dispute here between the one who holds they were placed north and south and the one who holds they were placed east and west) is only according to the opinions that the western lamp is the second from the east; this is not the case according to the Rambam, who holds that the western lamp is the one closest to the Kodesh HaKodashim—that is a different matter entirely.

4) According to the opinion of those who disagree with the Rambam, it is difficult to understand why the miracle (that one lamp was burning all day) was initially established in the second lamp from the east, and not in the lamp closest to the Kodesh HaKodashim, which is "before Hashem" more than all the others, and therefore it would be appropriate for the miracle to occur there.

The Ra’avad—printed in *Shas* (8)—wrote a reason why the miracle occurred in the eastern lamp (the second one from the side): because "one does not bypass mitzvos" (and the reason it was not in the first lamp from the east, but rather in the second lamp which is to its west, was so that it would be "before Hashem").

It is puzzling what the principle of "one does not bypass mitzvos" has to do with this; for that is only relevant to the Kohen who enters from the Ulam into the Heichal to light the lamps, since upon entering from the east, he encounters the lamp...

15

Regarding the establishment of the miracle from Heaven: If you wish to argue that the principle of "one does not bypass mitzvos" applies even in such a case, then on the contrary, the miracle should have been established in the lamp closest to the Kodesh HaKodashim. For the Shechinah and the holiness shine from the Kodesh HaKodashim into the Heichal, and it is the westernmost lamp that is reached first by this light. Perhaps the intention of the Ra’avad—even though his language does not explicitly imply this—is that we do not rely on miracles. Therefore, when a lamp was designated to be the one from which the others are lit at bein ha’arbayim, the second lamp from the east was designated, because one does not bypass mitzvos; thus, the miracle occurred in that lamp as well. According to the Rambam, there is no need for any of this.

5) And this is the main point: What led the aforementioned commentators to interpret "the western lamp" not according to its literal meaning—as the lamp at the western edge—but rather as the second lamp from the east, is the Mishnah in Tamid at the end of chapter 3, which distinguishes the law of the two eastern lamps from the other five. And since in his commentary on the Mishnah, the Rambam explains this Mishnah in a different way, it is clear that one should interpret "the western lamp" according to its literal meaning, as the lamp at the western edge.

## 16

Simply "before Hashem," for it is the one closest to the Kodesh HaKodashim [and just as the western foundation of the outer altar is called "before Hashem" (), similar to the placement of the staff of almonds "within their staffs" so that they would not say ()].

And it must be clarified how the Rambam reconciles the statement that "from it he would light and with it he would finish" (Shabbos 22b, Menachos 86b), given that according to him, if he found the two eastern lamps burning, he would clear the ashes from the other five lamps, including the western lamp. Furthermore, how does he explain the Sifri—cited in Ramban on the Torah, Shemos 27:20—which states: "‘Always’—that the western lamp should burn constantly, for from it one lights the Menorah in the evening," and likewise in Toras Kohanim—cited there—"‘Always’—that the western lamp should be constant." Yet, according to the Rambam, all seven lamps burn even during the day, and conversely, the western lamp is also cleared of its ashes.

10. And it still requires clarification: How does the Rambam reconcile the teaching that "from it he would light and with it he would finish" (Shabbos 22b, Menachos 86b)? For according to him, if upon the first entry he found the two eastern lamps burning, he clears the ashes from the other five lamps, including the western lamp. Then, he enters a second time and clears the ashes from the two eastern lamps (as mentioned above in sections 1-2). It turns out that he does not begin the lighting from the western lamp, since the two eastern ones are already burning, nor does he finish with the western lamp, since he cleared its ashes during the first entry, and only afterwards clears the two eastern ones?

And also: How does the Rambam explain the Sifri (cited in Ramban on the Torah, Shemos 27:20): "‘From evening until morning before Hashem’—‘Always’—that the western lamp should burn constantly, for from it one lights the Menorah in the evening," and similarly in Toras Kohanim (cited in Ramban, ibid.): "‘To cause a lamp to burn’—‘Always’—that the western lamp should be constant"—when according to the Rambam, all seven lamps (not just the western lamp) burn [during the day]?

## 17

And this is simple, for according to the Rambam, a lamp that did not go out is not extinguished when he cleans it and lights it; rather, he only prepares it. This is especially true of the *ner ma'aravi*, as mentioned above, that he leaves it burning. This is the meaning of "from it he would light"—namely, the Menorah in the evening—and with it he would conclude the changing of the wick and the cleaning at that time. This is similar to the commentary of Rashi in Menachos. And it seems clear to me that according to the *Peirush HaMishnayos*, that which is stated at the beginning of the sixth chapter [of Menachos]—"and he leaves"—

[The *reshimo* continues:]...also during the day. And on the other hand, the *ner ma'aravi* is also cleaned before the lighting in the evening, and at the time of its cleaning it is also not burning. If so, in what way is the *ner ma'aravi* considered "constant" (*tadir*) compared to the other lamps?

**11.** **And this is simple:**
For according to the opinion of the **Rambam**, a **lamp that did not go out** is not extinguished when he cleans it and lights it; rather, he **only prepares it**. This is especially true of the **ner ma'aravi**, as mentioned above (section 5, regarding the distinction between the cleaning of the eastern lamps and the *ner ma'aravi*), **that he leaves it burning**. If so, that which we learned, that "from it he would light," does not refer to the morning cleaning, for at that time (he does not light, but) only leaves it burning; rather, it refers to the lighting in the evening.

**This is the meaning of** "from it he would light"—namely, he lights (not the lamps that went out, during the morning cleaning, but) **the Menorah** (the entire Menorah, not only the lamps that went out) **in the evening**, **"and with it he would conclude" the changing of the wick and the cleaning at that time**—after the lighting of the lamps in the evening. This is similar to the commentary of Rashi in Menachos there.

**And it seems clear to me that according to the** opinion of the Rambam in the **Peirush HaMishnayos, that which is stated at the beginning of the sixth chapter**...

## 18

"And he leaves the western lamp burning"—this applies even if he found two eastern lamps burning, as in the end of chapter 3 of Tamid, where he cleans the western lamp during the first entry. And just as the latter part of the Mishnah in the beginning of chapter 6 states, "If he found it extinguished, he cleans it and lights it from the altar of burnt offering," it must be that this is the case even if he found two eastern lamps burning, for according to the Rambam, the western lamp is never lit from anything other than the altar of burnt offering.

And as for the Sifrei and Toras Kohanim, one must explain that this means the western lamp was established permanently to be the source from which the other lamps are lit, as Rashi explains in Shabbos and Tosafos in Menachos, cited above. Or, it refers to the time of the miracle, just as the "testimony" mentioned in the verse also refers to the time of the miracle.

"And he leaves the western lamp burning"—this is the case even if he found two eastern lamps burning, as in the end of chapter 3 of Tamid, where he cleans the western lamp during the first entry of the cleaning of the five lamps. Even then, the western lamp differs from the other lamps in that he does not extinguish it, clean it, and relight it, but rather leaves it burning (and merely adjusts it).

And just as the latter part of the Mishnah in the beginning of chapter 6 states, "If he found it extinguished, he cleans it and lights it from the altar of burnt offering," it must be that this is the case even if he found two eastern lamps burning (as mentioned above in section 4, that this comes to reveal [the law] regarding the Mishnah at the end of chapter 3 as well), for according to the Rambam, the western lamp is never lit from anything other than the altar of burnt offering—so too in the first part, "he leaves the western lamp burning," which applies even if he found two eastern lamps burning.

Based on this, it works out well that even when he found two eastern lamps burning—at which point he cleans the western lamp during the cleaning of the five lamps in the first entry—"with it (the western lamp) he would finish" the changing of the wick and the cleaning in the evening.

**12**. And as for the Sifrei and Toras Kohanim [stating] that the western lamp is "constant" (even though according to the Rambam all the lamps burned during the day as well)—the Rambam would explain that the "constant" mentioned regarding the western lamp means that the western lamp was established permanently to be the source from which the other lamps are lit, as Rashi explains in Shabbos and Tosafos in Menachos, cited above, that from what is written, "to cause a lamp to burn constantly," we derive that there must be a fixed lamp to light the other lamps, and that is the western lamp.

Alternatively, the Rambam would explain that what the Sifrei and Toras Kohanim say—that the western lamp is "constant"—refers to the time of the miracle, just as the "testimony" mentioned in the verse ("outside the curtain of the testimony... he shall arrange it," "it is a testimony to those who come into the world that the Shechinah rests in Israel... this is the western lamp") also refers to the time of the miracle, at which time...

## 19

Based on this interpretation in the Sifri and Toras Kohanim, the difficulty raised by R’ Elchanan in Tosafos (Chagigah 26b, s.v. *Menorah*) can also be resolved. He questioned the Gemara’s statement that the word "tamid" (continually) is not written regarding the Menorah—implying that it is only from night to night, and not throughout the entire day and night like the Shulchan—by pointing to the fact that the *ner maaravi* (western lamp) burned all day, "from which he would begin and with which he would conclude," which presumably is derived from the word "tamid." The resolution is that the "tamid" of the Menorah refers to the fixed status of the *ner maaravi* to be used for lighting the others, or that it refers specifically to the time of the miracle.

13. It still requires study regarding the Rambam: why did he not record in the Yad the law that the Menorah of *bein ha’arbayim* (twilight) must be lit specifically from the *ner maaravi*?

Furthermore, it must be clarified whether the obligation to light the Menorah of *bein ha’arbayim* from the *ner maaravi* applies to all the lamps—meaning that even the wick of the easternmost lamp must be pulled until it reaches the *ner maaravi*, lit, and then returned to its place—or if it is sufficient to light one lamp from the *ner maaravi*, and the remaining lamps may be lit from that one.

## Explaining the Reason for the Positioning of the Lamp Wicks

The question arises regarding the *avodah* of lighting the Menorah: why were the wicks of the six outer lamps positioned specifically to face the center lamp?

On the face of it, one might suggest that this is merely a technical detail of the *mitzvah*. However, in *avodah*, everything is precise. The center lamp represents the *middah* of *Malchus*, which receives from all the other *sefiros* and has nothing of its own. By having the other lamps face the center, it signifies that all the *kochos* and *avodah* of a Yid—all the various expressions of *ahavah* and *yirah*—must be directed toward the point of *bitul*. 

The center lamp is the *ner maaravi* (the western lamp), which miraculously remained lit, representing the *neshamah* that is always connected to Hashem. When a Chassid directs his entire *avodah*—his *ratzo v'shov* and his *hispaalus*—toward this inner point of *bitul*, he ensures that his light is not merely an external act, but one that is rooted in the *Ohr Ein Sof*. Thus, the positioning of the wicks is not just a physical requirement; it is a fundamental instruction in how one’s *avodah* must be oriented to achieve true *dveikus*.

## 20

**B) What is the reasoning of the Rambam in explaining that the mouths of all the lamps faced toward the west?** One cannot say that his proof is from the verse "the seven lamps shall give light"—and not six—as mentioned above. For even according to the Rambam, who rules in accordance with Rabbi Elazar bar Rabbi Shimon—who maintains that the Menorah was positioned north-to-south, and as the Rambam rules in *Yad* (as will be discussed below)—the six lamps were directed toward the middle lamp, as is stated in *Menachos* 98b and *Yad*, *Hilchos Beis HaBechirah* 3:8. Therefore, the verse must be interpreted as the Re'em and others have explained. If so, we could say the same regarding Rabbi—who maintains that it was positioned east-to-west—and how can we know that Rabbi and Rabbi Elazar bar Rabbi Shimon disagree on this point as well? Furthermore, it is explicitly...

**Explanation of the Rambam’s reasoning** in his *Peirush HaMishnayos* **regarding the orientation of the lamp mouths.**

**14.** Another detail requires clarification in the *Peirush HaMishnayos*: B) What is the reasoning of the Rambam in explaining that the mouths of all the lamps faced toward the west?

One cannot say that his proof is from the verse "the seven lamps shall give light"—and not six—as mentioned above (in section A): that according to the Rambam, since the mouths of all the lamps faced west, toward the Kodesh HaKodashim, the verse "the seven lamps shall give light toward the face of the Menorah" is easily understood. This is in contrast to the view that the mouths of the six lamps faced the middle lamp, in which case only *six* lamps would be facing toward the face of the Menorah (the middle lamp).

For even according to the Rambam, who rules in accordance with Rabbi Elazar bar Rabbi Shimon—who disagrees with Rabbi, who says it was positioned east-to-west, and maintains that the Menorah was positioned north-to-south (and the Rambam rules in accordance with him in *Yad*, as will be discussed below)—the six lamps were directed (faced) toward the middle lamp, as is stated in *Menachos* 98b and *Yad*, *Hilchos Beis HaBechirah* 3:8. Therefore, the verse "the seven lamps shall give light" (and not six) must be interpreted as the Re'em and others have explained. If so, we could say the same regarding Rabbi—who maintains that it was positioned east-to-west—that the mouths of the lamps faced the middle lamp, and yet it would not be a difficulty.

## 21

It is stated in the Sifrei—cited by Rashi on the Torah (Bamidbar 8:2)—and likewise in the Targum Yonasan ben Uziel there, that three [lamps] faced east and three faced west, with one in the middle, such that they all faced the middle one. This is according to the opinion that the Menorah was positioned east-to-west, as stated, "three faced east, etc." Yet, they explain that "the seven lamps shall give light over against the face of the Menorah" means that the lamps faced the middle one.

Perhaps it can be said that the Rambam’s proof that our Gemara disagrees with the Sifrei* is from the passage in Menachos (98b), which states that Rabbi Elazar bar Shimon—who holds that the Menorah was positioned north-to-south—argues [regarding the verse], "He shall set it in order," which implies that only one lamp [is the focus]—

*Or that the text of the Sifrei is emended as the Gra suggests.

And how does the Rambam know that even in this regard (not only in the placement of the Menorah, but also in the angling of the mouths of the lamps) Rabbi and Rabbi Elazar bar Shimon disagree, such that only according to Rabbi Elazar bar Shimon do the mouths of the lamps face the middle lamp, whereas according to Rabbi, the mouths of all the lamps face (not the middle lamp, but) toward the Holy of Holies in the west?

Furthermore: It is explicitly stated in the Sifrei—cited by Rashi on the Torah (Bamidbar 8:2), and likewise in the Targum Yonasan ben Uziel there—"Three faced east and three faced west, and one in the middle; thus they all faced the middle one." This is according to the opinion that it was positioned east-to-west, as stated, "three faced east, etc." (for according to the opinion that it was positioned north-to-south, there would be three facing north and three facing south). Yet, they (the Sifrei, Rashi, and Targum Yonasan) explain "the seven lamps shall give light over against the face of the Menorah" to mean that the lamps faced the middle one. If so, how does the Rambam derive the innovation that according to Rabbi, the lamps do not face the middle one, but rather face west?

15. Perhaps it can be said that the Rambam’s proof that our Gemara disagrees with the Sifrei* and does not hold that the six lamps face the middle one is from the passage in Menachos (98b).

For it states there: "And Rabbi Elazar bar Shimon (who holds that it was positioned north-to-south), etc., it is written..."

*Or that the text of the Sifrei is emended as the Gra suggests: "Three faced north and three faced south"—like the opinion that it was positioned north-to-south.

## 22

"It shall be before Hashem"—this refers to the one at the western edge. But if we say that they were placed north-to-south, then they are all equal. The Gemara answers that they were indeed placed north-to-south, yet only one is "before Hashem" because "he turns them inward"—as it is taught, "towards the face of the Menorah," etc., meaning they turned their wicks toward the middle lamp.

Now, if we were to say that even according to the opinion that they were placed east-to-west, the lamps were parallel to the middle lamp, except that according to this view, the middle lamp does not incline toward the east or the west—and the respondent [in the Gemara] introduces the novelty that according to Rabbi Yehuda ben Beseira, the middle lamp inclines toward the west, and is therefore called the "western lamp"—if so, the Gemara should not have answered that "he turns them inward." For this [the fact that they are turned inward] is something that Rabbi also holds, and it contains no novelty or resolution; surely the questioner was also aware of this. His novelty should have been to inform us that the middle lamp inclines toward the west.

Furthermore, what is the proof from the Baraisa that "they turned their wicks toward the middle lamp"? After all, even according to Rabbi, this is the case. And one cannot say that he relies on the end of the Baraisa (cited in Megillah 21b), "and the western lamp toward the Shechinah," for it is impossible to say so. Firstly, the versions in Menachos and Megillah differ in three other matters as well, and it appears they are two different Baraisas. Moreover, if that were the case, the essence of his proof would be missing.

## 23

Therefore, the Rambam explains that according to Rabbi, all the wicks face toward the west, in accordance with the literal meaning of the verse, "The seven lamps shall give light toward the front of the Menorah" (Bamidbar 8:2) — seven, specifically. And "toward the front of the Menorah" means toward the side of the Kodesh HaKodashim, which is located in front of the Menorah*. The questioner assumes that according to Rabbi Yehuda ben Beteira as well, the interpretation of the verse is the same. This follows the language of Rashi (Menachos 98b) s.v. *Madichsiv*: "For if it is placed north-to-south, it turns out that all the openings of the wicks face west," and the verse proves that only one is "before Hashem." The Gemara resolves this by stating that, in fact, only one—the middle lamp—is "before Hashem," because the other lamps are angled [toward the center].

* See the explanation of "toward the front of the Menorah" in Likutei Torah, Parshas Beha'aloscha.

***

These are two separate Baraisos, and consequently, it is impossible to say that the passage in Menachos relies on the end of the Baraisa from Megillah, which is distinct from it.

Furthermore, if that were the case (if we were to say it relies on the end of a Baraisa not explicitly cited here), the core of his proof—from the end of the Baraisa that "the western lamp is toward the Shechinah"—would be missing from the text.

16. Therefore, the Rambam explains that according to the view of Rabbi, that it is placed east-to-west, the openings of all the lamps face west, in accordance with the literal meaning of the verse, "The seven lamps shall give light toward the front of the Menorah"—seven, specifically. And "toward the front of the Menorah" means (not toward the middle lamp, but) toward the side of the Kodesh HaKodashim, which is located in front of the Menorah*.

The questioner assumes that according to the view of Rabbi Yehuda ben Beteira, that it is placed north-to-south, the interpretation of the verse ("The seven lamps shall give light toward the front of the Menorah") is the same (that the openings of all the lamps face west, toward the side of the Kodesh HaKodashim, which is located in front of the Menorah). Regarding this, he raises the difficulty: "If you think it is placed north-to-south, then all of them are also 'before Hashem.'" That is, in his opinion, the openings of all the lamps face west—as per the language of Rashi (Menachos 98b) s.v. *Madichsiv*: "For if it is placed north-to-south, it turns out that all the openings of the wicks (and he did not say 'lamps' generally) face west"—and the verse proves that only one is "before Hashem."

And to this, the Gemara resolves that, in fact, it is placed north-to-south, and nevertheless, only one lamp—the middle lamp—is "before Hashem" (as its opening faces west), because the other lamps are "angled [toward the center]."

* See the explanation of "toward the front of the Menorah" in Likutei Torah, Parshas Beha'aloscha.

## 24

It is not as you think, for I have seen that it is taught in a Baraisa, etc. And regarding this, too, Rabbi Yehuda ben Beteira and Rabbi disagree. According to Rabbi Yehuda ben Beteira, "the face of the Menorah" means the essence of the Menorah—that is, the middle lamp, which is in the body of the Menorah.

It is worth noting regarding the interpretation of the word "face" (*panim*): In *Yad, Hilchos Yesodei HaTorah* (1:10), it states that Moshe Rabbeinu requested to know the truth of His existence as it is, and the Holy One, blessed be He, replied that there is no capacity to grasp the truth of this matter in its entirety, and to this the verse alludes: "And My face shall not be seen." In *Moreh Nevuchim* (Part 1, Chapter 37), it explains that "face" refers to the essence of a person's existence, and from this matter, "And My face shall not be seen" means that the truth of My existence as it is cannot be grasped. "Face" also refers to any place that is before you. And these are likewise the two interpretations of "the face of the Menorah" according to Rabbi Yehuda ben Beteira and Rabbi.

## The reason for the difference between the Eastern lamp and the other lamps, and the order of the kindling.

## 25

3) What is the reason for the distinction in the law of the two easternmost lamps compared to the other lamps, as mentioned above in the Mishnah at the end of Chapter 3—that if he found them burning, etc.? According to the other opinions, which hold that the *ner hama'aravi* is the second lamp from the east, and that the *hatavas* (preparation) must be concluded with it, as will be explained later, it is understandable why, if he found them burning, he cleans the others and the two easternmost lamps only at the end; and if he found them extinguished, he lights them so that the *ner hama'aravi* will be burning constantly, etc. However, according to the Rambam, who holds that the *ner hama'aravi* is the lamp adjacent to the *Kodesh HaKodashim*, how are the two easternmost lamps distinguished from the other lamps?

It may be explained by prefacing that the Rambam holds that the *hatavas* of the lamps refers to both their cleaning and their lighting, as mentioned above. And he interprets the passage in *Menachos* (88b), "A lamp that went out, the oil has become stale, the wick has become stale" [this is the Rambam's version].

18. Another detail requires clarification in the interpretation of the Mishnah—what is the reason for the distinction in the law of the two easternmost lamps compared to the other lamps, as mentioned above in the Mishnah at the end of Chapter 3—that if he found them burning, etc.?

According to the other opinions, which hold that the *ner hama'aravi* is the second lamp from the east, and that the *hatavas* must be concluded with it, as will be explained later, it is understandable why, if he found them (the two easternmost lamps) burning, he cleans the others, and the two easternmost lamps he cleans only at the end; and if he found them extinguished, he lights them so that the *ner hama'aravi* will be burning constantly, etc. (and consequently, the easternmost lamp must also be burning, so that the lamp adjacent to it may be called the *ner hama'aravi*, as mentioned above in section 7). However, according to the Rambam, who holds that the *ner hama'aravi* (with which the *hatavas* must be concluded) is the lamp adjacent to the *Kodesh HaKodashim*, how are the two easternmost lamps distinguished from the other lamps?

19. The explanation for this may be given by prefacing:

That the Rambam holds that the *hatavas* of the lamps refers to both their cleaning and their lighting, as mentioned above (section 1).

And he interprets the passage in *Menachos* (88b), "A lamp that went out, the oil has become stale, the wick has become stale" [this is the Rambam's version: "has become stale... has become stale," unlike the version before us: "has become fatty..."].

## 26

As written in the commentary on the Mishnah, Menachos, Chapter 9, Mishnah 3. This makes the language of the Gemara flow naturally, and there is no need for Rashi’s explanation there (s.v. *nidshen*) that the term "the oil has become ash" is not meant literally. This applies both if this occurred in the morning—as is implied by his language in Yad, Hilchos Temidin U’Musafin, Chapter 3, Halacha 12—and if it occurred before daybreak. This is implied by the language of the aforementioned commentary on the Mishnah, which writes that if one of the lamps was extinguished, the wick and the oil remaining in that lamp are considered as if they have "aged." The explanation of this is that the wick and oil are provided in a measure intended to burn from evening until morning, and after this time they are considered "aged"—even though they remain valid thereafter if the lamp did not go out, as mentioned above regarding the *ner tamid*, where one only adds oil. It would be forced to say that there is a dispute regarding this as well.

---
\*) See the Rashba regarding his proof that this is during the day.

## 27

Regarding the other lamps. See Yerushalmi, Yoma, Chapter 2, Halacha 2. And if it did not go out, one does not extinguish it, as mentioned above—and I say that in such a case it is also considered as if it is "old," even though it is not literally old. Therefore, it must be that this refers to a case where it went out before the light of day. This is unlike Rashi, who holds that the lighting of the lamps is only in the evening (Rashi, s.v. *Menorah*, Chagigah 26b; and his commentary on the Torah, Shemos 27:20; see also Rashi, s.v. *Mimena*, Menachos 86b), who explains:

Regarding the western lamp, one does not remove the oil that remains in the lamp (and certainly one does not remove the wick that is burning), but rather one only adds oil. It stands to reason that this is the case for every lamp that did not go out—that the old wick and oil are valid, for it is forced to say that the western lamp is distinct from the other lamps in this regard as well (and see Yerushalmi, Yoma, Chapter 2, Halacha 2). And if it did not go out, one does not extinguish it, as mentioned above.

And I say that in such a case ("if one of the lamps went out"), it is also considered as if it is "old," even though it is not literally old.

It must be that this refers to a case where it went out before the light of day, for then it is not literally old, as it is within the timeframe of "from evening until morning." The novelty is that even so, since it went out, it is considered *as if* it is old.

This interpretation (that the law of "if a lamp went out, it has become old, etc., and one lights it" applies even if it occurred in the morning) is only according to the Rambam, who holds that the lighting of the lamps is also in the morning. This is unlike Rashi, who holds that the lighting of the lamps is only in the evening (Rashi, s.v. *Menorah*, Chagigah 26b; and his commentary on the Torah, Shemos 27:20 ; see also Rashi, s.v. *Mimena*, Menachos 86b), who explains:

## 28

A lamp that extinguished in the middle of the night or earlier—specifically—before the light of day.

 Although it is not relevant to our current *shakla v’tarya*, it is necessary to clarify this statement, especially according to Rashi, who explicitly writes that "a lamp that extinguished" refers to "the middle of the night or before the light of day." Seemingly, this is difficult to understand: How is it possible to know at night that the lamp in the Menorah has extinguished, when the *Heichal*—where the Menorah is located—is locked close to and before sunset, as stated in the Yerushalmi, *Berachos* 4:5 [and according to Rashi, *Yoma* 87b, s.v. *nachis*, the *Azarah* is also locked at that time. See also *Midos* 1:7 and Rashi at the beginning of *Taanis* 26a]? And they would not open it until the time for *shechitah* arrived, which is when the day had dawned, as stated in *Tamid* 3:2, "The time for *shechitah* arrived... the face of the entire east lit up... he took the key, etc." It is also worth noting *Shmuel I* 3:15. It is possible that one could see through the windows.

## 29

The *Heichal*—for the *Heichal* had windows, as stated in *Melachim I 6:4*—is cited in *Menachos 86b* and in *Yechezkel*. See *Tosafos Yom Tov* on *Middos 4:3* regarding their location. And even though the *Azarah* was also closed, as mentioned above, nonetheless, they would enter through the *pishpash* (small wicket gate) to inspect the *Azarah*, and likewise for *Terumas HaDeshen* and *Simchas Beis HaShoeivah*. See the commentary in *Tamid 26b* (s.v. *Amar Rabbi Yehuda*) and *28a* (s.v. *Natel*). Or perhaps the *Kohen* who was guarding in the *Aliyah* of *Beis HaNitzotz*, which is in the north—*Middos 1:5*—had a high vantage point.

Since the *Heichal* (and likewise the *Azarah*) is closed until daybreak, how is it possible to know before daybreak that a lamp in the *Menorah* has gone out, such that the ruling for "a lamp that has gone out" would apply?

**One may say** that even at a time when the *Heichal* is closed, **it is possible to see** the *Menorah* **through the windows of the *Heichal*—for the *Heichal* had windows, as it is stated** in ***Melachim I 6:4***: "And he made for the house windows of wide-narrow frames"—**cited** in ***Menachos 86b*** **and in *Yechezkel* 40:16**: "And there were closed windows, etc., and windows round about, etc." **And see *Tosafos Yom Tov* on *Middos*** Chapter **4**, Mishnah **3**—**regarding the location** of the windows.

**And even** if they were unable to see through the *Heichal* windows because **the *Azarah* was also closed, as mentioned above** (from *Rashi* in *Yoma*)—**nonetheless**, before daybreak **they would enter through the *pishpash*** **to inspect the *Azarah***, **and likewise for *Terumas HaDeshen***, and likewise for ***Simchas Beis HaShoeivah*** (**and see the commentary in *Tamid 26b***—**s.v. *Amar Rabbi Yehuda*, and page 28a**—**s.v. *Natel***). Consequently, they were able to see the *Menorah* through the windows of the *Heichal* even before daybreak.

**Or it is possible** to say that they could see the *Menorah* at night without entering the *Azarah* at all, because **the *Kohen* who was guarding in the *Aliyah* of *Beis HaNitzotz*, which is in the north (*Middos* Chapter 1, Mishnah 5), had a high vantage point.**

## 30

 It is so, for he sees through the windows what is happening opposite him in the Heichal, near the southern wall where the Menorah stood. Or, one could say that this refers to a case where it happened that he entered and saw that the lamp had gone out. See also Yoma (39b): "The doors of the Heichal would open by themselves."

One must also analyze Rashi’s intent in writing "at midnight." It is understandable why he writes "before the light of day"—to exclude a case where the day has already dawned, as mentioned above. But if Rashi intends to explain all the possibilities, surely the lamp could also go out before midnight?

One might suggest, by way of sharp analysis, that he is teaching us something incidentally: that the extinguishing of the lamp is more likely to begin from midnight onwards. For if the lamp were to go out earlier, there would have to be some unusual cause, whereas from midnight onwards, it could happen due to a mistake in the preparation of the wick. For behold, the measure of oil placed in the Menorah is intended to last from evening until morning (Menachos 89a). Regarding this, the Yerushalmi states in Yoma (Chapter 2, Halacha 2) (cited in Rashi and Tosafos, Menachos ibid.).

## 31

On the nights of Tammuz, they would make thick wicks so that all the oil in the lamp would be consumed. If it happened that, by mistake, they placed a wick that was too thick—that is, the wick of the Tammuz season—on a day when the time for the lamps to shine is at its longest, the oil would be consumed around the middle of the night. 

In the Gemara, the law regarding oil that remains is also mentioned—that is, when it is extinguished due to an unusual cause. The meaning of "until morning"—as stated regarding the Menorah—is until *alos hashachar* (dawn), just as we find regarding the burning of fats in the beginning of the first chapter of Berachos and in other places. This is straightforward. 

In the evening, he would light the lamps after the offering of the *Tamid* and the burning of the *Ketores* (Yoma 15a), for the burning of the *Ketores* does not involve a labor that requires significant time. It seems clear that in the *Bein Ha'arbayim* (late afternoon), he would burn the *Ketores* between the preparation of the five lamps and the two lamps, as will be explained later. If so, since the completion of the *Tamid* offering is at nine and a half hours (Pesachim 58a), it follows that immediately thereafter he would light the lamps with a wick such that the oil would be consumed by *alos hashachar* of that day. 

Now, Jerusalem, which is situated to the north of the equator, is at thirty-two degrees (Yad, Hilchos Kiddush Hachodesh).

## 32

From sunrise until sunset —which is the measure of the day —during the period of Tammuz, which is the longest day, it is approximately fourteen hours, as is written. And from dawn until sunrise, which is at most—and as mentioned above, Rashi adopted the earliest possible measure—five mil during the days of Nissan and Tishrei,.

## 33

Which is one-sixth of the day (Pesachim 93b and ad loc. This is the *shiura rabba* of Rava and Ulla, which was refuted*, for the conclusion there is that it is one-eighth of the day from *netz hachama* until *shekias hachama*), meaning two hours of *shaos zmaniyos*, as each of them on this day in Jerusalem is...

* The *Yad*, *Hilchos Korban Pesach* 5:10, rules in accordance with Ulla (Pesachim ibid.), even though it was refuted (and it is reconciled in *Maadanei Yom Tov* on *Berachos* 1:9). And so it appears in Rashi, *Berachos* 2b, s.v. *Umikdami*, and in *Tosafos*, s.v. *Echad*, *Pesachim* 11b and *Sanhedrin* 41b.

***

Which is one-sixth of the day (Pesachim 93b and ad loc.. And this is the *shiura rabba* of Rava and Ulla, which was refuted*, for the conclusion there is that it is four *mil*—which is one-eighth of the day from *netz hachama* until *shekias hachama*), meaning...

* And it is worth noting that in the *Yad*, *Hilchos Korban Pesach* 5:10, he rules in accordance with Ulla (in *Pesachim* ibid.), even though it was refuted (and it is reconciled in *Maadanei Yom Tov* on the Rosh, *Berachos* Chapter 1 (9b), s.v. 9). And so it appears in Rashi, *Berachos* 2b, s.v. *Umikdami*, and in *Tosafos*, s.v. *Echad*, *Pesachim* 11b and *Sanhedrin* 41b—that they cite the statement in *Pesachim* that from *alos hashachar* until *netz hachama* is a distance of five *mil*, in accordance with the words of Ulla.

## 34

And if so, from the ninth and a half hour until *alos hashachar* (dawn), these are [a specific number of] equal hours. And it is by this measure that we gauge the wick. Now, on *Erev Pesach* that falls on *Erev Shabbos*, the *Menorah* is lit at approximately the seventh and a half hour, which is when the *Tamid* offering is completed, and the candles take precedence over the *Pesach* offering (*Pesachim* 59a). In *Nissan*, the day and night are equal. If so, if they mistakenly inserted a *Tammuz* wick at that time, the oil would be consumed around the middle of the night.

Two *sha'os zmaniyos* (proportional hours)—each of which, on this day (during the *Tekufas Tammuz*), is seventy minutes in Jerusalem—amount to two hours and twenty minutes.

Thus, the division of the twenty-four hours of the day in Jerusalem during *Tekufas Tammuz* is as follows: From *alos hashachar* until sunset is sixteen hours and twenty minutes (twelve *sha'os zmaniyos* from sunrise to sunset, which are fourteen hours, plus two *sha'os zmaniyos* from *alos* until sunrise, which are two hours and twenty minutes). And from sunset until *alos hashachar* is seven hours and forty minutes.

And if so, the measure of time "from evening until morning" during which the *Menorah* burns—from the ninth and a half hour of the twelve *sha'os zmaniyos* of the day, which is when the *Tamid* offering of the afternoon is completed ("from evening"), until *alos hashachar* ("until morning"), which is two and a half *sha'os zmaniyos* before sunset (which in *Tekufas Tammuz* is two hours and fifty-five minutes), plus the entire duration from sunset until *alos hashachar* (which in *Tekufas Tammuz* is seven hours and forty minutes)—is ten hours and thirty-five minutes of equal hours.

It is by this measure that we gauge the thickness of the wick for the short nights of *Tammuz*—it must be thick enough to consume all the oil in the lamp within the shorter duration of ten hours and thirty-five minutes.

Now, on *Erev Pesach* that falls on *Erev Shabbos*, the *Menorah* is lit at approximately the seventh and a half hour, which is when the *Tamid* offering is completed. The *Menorah* is lit then, and only afterwards is the *Pesach* offering slaughtered, for the candles take precedence over the *Pesach* offering (*Pesachim* 59a). In *Nissan*, the day and night are equal—twelve hours from sunrise to sunset, and twelve hours from sunset to sunrise (so that the *sha'os zmaniyos* and the equal hours are one and the same). Consequently, the time for lighting the *Menorah*—at the seventh and a half hour, meaning at one-thirty in the afternoon—is four and a half hours before sunset. As a result, the duration of "from evening until morning" is longer, and therefore one must insert a thinner wick so that the oil will suffice for the lamp to burn until *alos hashachar*.

And if so, if they mistakenly inserted a *Tammuz* wick at that time (on *Erev Pesach* that falls on *Erev Shabbos*)—the measure of which is such that it would consume the oil in the lamp within ten hours and thirty-five minutes—then the oil would be consumed around the middle of the night (as it would burn from 1:30 PM until 12:05 AM).

## 35

When the Kohen enters the Heichal to prepare the lamps, the western lamp—which is adjacent to the Kodesh HaKodashim—is the most sanctified of them all, for it is "before Hashem." Consequently, it should take precedence over all others in the mitzvah of preparing the lamps. Therefore, during the first entry, he prepares it as well. But this applies only if he finds the two eastern lamps burning. If, however, he finds them extinguished, since he encounters them first upon entering the Heichal, the obligation of "a lamp that went out... he prepares it, adds oil, and lights it" immediately takes effect upon him, in addition to the obligation of preparing the lamps in the morning. For even if the extinguishing occurred at night, this obligation exists, as mentioned above, and he must prepare them. And since he begins—

— The reason the Yerushalmi (Yoma, ibid.) mentions the nights of Teves, rather than Erev Pesach that falls on Erev Shabbos, is because that does not occur every year, and it only lasts for one night. Furthermore, there is an opinion that the lamps are [lit] after Pesach (though the halachah does not follow that view).

## 36

The preparation of the lamps begins from the easternmost lamps. Since two lamps must be prepared during the second entry (as there must be an interruption with another avodah between the preparation of the five lamps and the preparation of the two lamps), the two western lamps—including the western lamp itself, which is adjacent to the Kodesh HaKodashim and would otherwise be prioritized—are prepared afterward, during the second entry.

If this is the case, we conclude that the Rambam holds like the Raavad (printed in the standard edition of the Shas) that it is impossible to prepare the lamps intermittently; rather, they must be prepared in their proper order, one after the other.

For if you were not to say this—if we were to say that it is possible to prepare the lamps intermittently—the Mishnah should not have specified, "If he found **two** eastern lamps burning," as the condition under which he cleans the western lamp during the first entry (implying that if only one of them were burning, he would clean the western lamp during the second entry). After all, even if only one of those lamps were burning, he could simply clean the specific lamp that had gone out (and the lamp that was still burning could be prepared along with one other lamp after the interruption, during the second entry), and he could prepare the western lamp during the first entry, since it is "before Hashem."

Therefore, we must conclude that it is impossible to do so—to clean one of the eastern lamps during the first entry and then four western lamps—because he would then be forced to skip, and one does not prepare the lamps intermittently.

Consequently, only if he "found two eastern lamps burning" does he clean the western lamp during...

## 37

Within the five lamps, the *hatavas* (preparation) of the lamps will then be one after the other, and not intermittently. But if even one has gone out, he prepares the *ner hamaaravi* (western lamp) afterwards.

And if you will say: even if he found two lamps from the seven burning—where there is no obligation of "a lamp that went out, etc."—surely one does not pass over the *mitzvos*—the *mitzvah* of *hatavas* the lamps in the morning—so how can he leave the two lamps from the seven and prepare the *ma'aravi* (western ones)? And it is well if we say that the *ner hamaaravi* is the second lamp from the east; he is forced to do so, since with the *ner hamaaravi* one must conclude the *hatavas*, as will be explained below, and to prepare the easternmost lamp and skip the second lamp is impossible, for one does not perform *hatavas* intermittently. But the Rambam holds that the *ner hamaaravi* is the one adjacent to the *Kodesh HaKodashim*, and if so, it is difficult as stated above. And it can be said that the rule that one does not pass over the *mitzvos* applies only if he has the ability to perform this *avodah* now; but in the case at hand, when he found two lamps from the seven burning, he only prepares them afterwards, upon the second entry and with the interruption of another *avodah*, and we do not say in this instance that one does not pass over the *mitzvos*.

## 38

If you were to ask: Based on the principle that "we do not pass over mitzvos" (*ein ma'avirin al hamitzvos*), should he not have established the first preparation—the five lamps—to begin with the eastern lamps?

The answer is that we can apply the reasoning of Tosafos (*Zevachim 51a*, s.v. *Asher*), which states that the principle of *ein ma'avirin al hamitzvos* only applies when two mitzvos are before a person, or when he does not know where to begin; however, we do not establish a fixed procedure based on this principle. 

And even though if the two options were equal, the reasoning of *ein ma'avirin* would be effective even regarding a fixed procedure—as is proven in *Megillah (6b)* (and see there in Tosafos, s.v. *Mistaber*, who challenges the aforementioned reasoning of Tosafos in *Zevachim* that *ein ma'avirin* only applies when two mitzvos are present, arguing that here there is only one mitzvah, yet the reason is still explained as being due to *ein ma'avirin*)—nevertheless, regarding the Menorah, the two options are not equal. The western lamp is superior to the other lamps because it is "before Hashem," and therefore it takes precedence. Consequently, the reasoning of *ein ma'avirin al hamitzvos* does not suffice to establish a requirement to begin the preparation of the five lamps specifically with the eastern lamps.

See also Tosafos in *Yoma (33a)*, s.v. *Ein*, which states that "where we are only performing one, we do not derive from it [the principle of *ein ma'avirin*] that we should perform the *tadir* (frequent) one alone and not perform the *she'eino tadir* (less frequent) one." That is to say, when the two options are not equal, but rather one of them possesses the priority of being *tadir*, the reasoning of *ein ma'avirin al hamitzvos* is not effective regarding a fixed procedure. The same applies in our case, where the two are not...

## 39

All of this concerns the general question of which five of the seven lamps one should prepare in the first entry when finding two eastern lamps burning. However, regarding the five western lamps themselves, it is seemingly obvious that one prepares them in the order one encounters them—that is, from east to west—and one does not begin with the western lamp, even though it is superior. For it is not mentioned by any Tanna anywhere that one should do so. This is also implied, to some extent, by the language of the Mishnah at the beginning of the sixth chapter of Tamid, which states: "He clears the ashes from the eastern [lamp] and leaves the western [lamp]..." and "If he found it extinguished..." Furthermore, the logic dictates this as well: since these five lamps—as well as the two lamps mentioned at the beginning of the sixth chapter of Tamid—are all being prepared at this time, the law of "one does not bypass mitzvos" applies to them in its entirety. This principle takes precedence over the rule that "the frequent takes precedence over the infrequent" (see the aforementioned Tosafos, *D.H. Ein*; see also *Korban Nethanel*, end of Megillah, section 3). The same applies here regarding the priority of the western lamp because it is "before Hashem"; the principle of "one does not bypass mitzvos" takes precedence over this priority, and therefore one prepares them from east to west.

## 40

Indeed, on the face of it, there is a refutation. For the source of this reasoning—that the principle of *ein ma’avirin al hamitzvos* (one does not bypass mitzvos) is inapplicable to the matter of establishing a fixed location for a single avodah—is the aforementioned Tosafos in Zevachim and Yoma, and the Tosafos *d’amar* (Yoma 59a). They state that the words "the one he encounters first" are not to be included in the Gemara regarding the pouring of the *sheyarei hadam* (the remainder of the blood) onto the western foundation, because this reasoning is not applicable to the matter of establishing a fixed location for a single avodah. 

If so, the Rambam—whose position we are currently analyzing—who does include these words, as he writes in *Hilchos Ma’aseh HaKorbanos* (Chapter 5, Halacha 11): "And the remainder of the blood he pours onto the western foundation of the *mizbach hachitzon* (the outer altar), which he encounters first upon exiting the *Heichal*," must necessarily hold that he does not subscribe to the aforementioned reasoning. Rather, he holds that even regarding the matter of establishing a fixed location for a single avodah, we do apply the principle of *ein ma’avirin al hamitzvos*.

## 41

And the difficulty returns to its place: that the preparation of the eastern lamps should have been established to always take place during the first entry, for "one does not bypass mitzvos."

But it can be said that the Rambam maintains that the law that the remainder of the blood is poured onto the western foundation is derived from a verse, and not because of the principle that "one does not bypass mitzvos" and that one encounters it first. This is also implied by the language of the Rambam in his Commentary on the Mishnah in Zevachim (Chapter 5, Mishnah 1), where he writes: "And the remainder of the blood, etc., is learned from the remainder of the blood of the bull of the Kohen Mashiach, etc., regarding which it is stated: 'to the foundation of the altar of the burnt offering which is at the entrance of the Ohel Moed,' and the side of the altar adjacent to the entrance of the Ohel Moed is the western side."

And only in Mishnah 2 there, which deals with the remainder of the blood of external sin-offerings—which are poured onto the southern foundation—and we derive this from the fact that just as its exit from the Heichal is adjacent to it, he writes: "Just as the blood of the sin-offerings performed inside has its remainder poured onto the western foundation, which is adjacent to it upon exiting the Heichal, as we have explained, so too the blood performed outside has its remainder poured onto the foundation closest to it as one descends from the top of the altar."

## 42

The fact that the Yad writes that he encounters it first as he exits the Heichal is in continuation of the preceding halacha, which deals with the chatos performed outside. It states, "And upon the foundation of that same corner where he completed the applications, he pours the remainder of the blood, as it is said: 'And all the blood shall he pour out at the foundation of the altar'—this is the southern foundation." This proof is not yet clear, as they conclude in Zevachim (53a): "Do you say this is the southern foundation, or is it perhaps the western foundation?" etc. They say, "Just as his exit from the Heichal is adjacent to it, so too his descent from the keves is adjacent to it." Therefore, the Yad also hints at this proof—that "at the foundation of the altar" must refer to the southern foundation—because the remainder of the blood of the chatos that are burned is poured onto the western foundation, which he encounters first.

Now, the Lechem Mishneh there explains that the Rambam holds that the reason for pouring the remainder of the blood onto the western foundation is because of the principle that "one does not pass over mitzvos." However, this very point is derived from a verse. And even according to him, it is possible to say that the pouring of the remainder of the blood is different, for it is revealed...

The fact that the Yad writes that "the remainder of the blood (of the inner chatos) he pours onto the western foundation of the outer altar, *which he encounters first as he exits the Heichal*" (even though we derive this from a verse, and not because "one does not pass over mitzvos" and he encountered it first)—is in continuation of the preceding halacha:

It deals with the chatos performed outside, and says, "And upon the foundation of that same corner where he completed the applications (the southwestern corner) he pours the remainder of the blood, as it is said: 'And all the blood shall he pour out at the foundation of the altar'—this is the southern foundation."

And this proof is not yet clear, as they conclude in Zevachim (53a): "Do you say this is the southern foundation, or is it perhaps the western foundation?" etc. And there is a need to learn this from the inner chatos: "You say, just as his exit from the Heichal (in the case of inner chatos) is adjacent to it, so too his descent from the keves (in the case of outer chatos) is adjacent to it."

Therefore, the Yad also hints at this proof—that "at the foundation of the altar" must refer to the southern foundation, because the remainder of the blood of the chatos that are burned is poured onto the western foundation, which he encounters first—by writing in the subsequent halacha regarding the inner chatos that "the remainder of the blood he pours onto the western foundation of the outer altar, *which he encounters first* as he exits the Heichal." From this, we learn that the pouring of the remainder of the blood of the outer chatos is onto the southern foundation: "Just as his exit from the Heichal is *adjacent to it* (at the western foundation, *which he encounters first* as he exits the Heichal), so too his descent from the keves is *adjacent to it* (at the southern foundation, which he encounters first as he descends from the top of the altar)."

27. Now, the Lechem Mishneh there explains that the Rambam holds that the reason for pouring the remainder of the blood onto the western foundation is because of the principle that "one does not pass over mitzvos." However, this very point is derived from a verse, for even if he only needs to perform one, the reason of "one does not pass over" applies because he encountered it first.

## 43

The verse reveals this regarding the inner *chataos*, and for the outer ones, we derive his descent from the *keves* (altar ramp), etc., as mentioned above; whereas where the verse does not explicitly reveal this, it is different. And if we were to say that the reasoning of the *Lechem Mishneh* is that the verse reveals this regarding the inner *chataos*, and consequently we derive from this for every case—otherwise, a difficulty remains: If so, why did he state regarding the law of the outer *chataos*—which are on the southern foundation—that the source is "his exit from the *Heichal*," etc.? Surely the reason is because of *ein ma’avirin al hamitzvos*, and not because of the *hekkesh* (analogy) and *binyan av* (foundational derivation) from the inner *chataos*; and the verse regarding the inner *chataos* comes only to reveal that this logic exists even in the establishment of a single *avodah*, and consequently it is so in every case, and not specifically regarding *korbanos*. And it still requires further study: the logic of *ein ma’avirin al hamitzvos*—what does it accomplish here, seeing as it is only so where the verse explicitly reveals it?

Seemingly, after we derive from the verse that the reason of *ein ma’avirin al hamitzvos* applies even to the establishment of the place of a single *avodah*—because he encounters it first—the difficulty returns to its place: that even regarding the *Menorah*, one should have been required to establish the *hatavas haneiros* (trimming of the lamps) of the eastern ones always at the first entrance, for he encounters them first, and *ein ma’avirin al hamitzvos*?

One must say that even according to the *Lechem Mishneh*, it is possible to say that the pouring of the *shirayim* (remnants) of the blood is different, in that even in a single *avodah*, the reason of *ein ma’avirin al hamitzvos* applies because he encounters it first, since the verse reveals this regarding the inner *chataos*, and for the outer *chataos* we derive it from his descent from the *keves*, etc. (from his exit from the *Heichal*), as mentioned above; whereas where the verse does not reveal this (like in the *hatavas haneiros*), it is different, and we do not say *ein ma’avirin al hamitzvos* regarding the establishment of the place of a single *avodah*.

And it is necessary to say that where the verse does not reveal this, it is different—for if we were to say that the reasoning of the *Lechem Mishneh* is that the verse reveals this regarding the inner *chataos*, and consequently we derive from this that in every place *ein ma’avirin al hamitzvos* applies even to the establishment of the place of a single *avodah*—otherwise (even without the difficulty from the *hatavas* of the five lamps), a difficulty remains: If so, why did he state regarding the law of the outer *chataos*—which are on the southern foundation—that the derivation for this is "his exit from the *Heichal*," etc.? Surely the reason is because of *ein ma’avirin al hamitzvos*, and not because of the *hekkesh* and *binyan av* from the inner *chataos*; and the verse regarding the inner *chataos* comes only to reveal that this logic exists even in the establishment of a single *avodah*, and consequently it is so in every case, and not specifically regarding *korbanos*. And it must be that this is not a derivation for every place, but only where the verse explicitly reveals it (or where we derive it from a *hekkesh*, etc.), whereas where it does not reveal it, it is different.

And it still requires further study according to the interpretation of the *Lechem Mishneh* that the derivation from the verse is that even regarding the establishment of the place of a single *avodah* we say *ein ma’avirin al hamitzvos* (except that this is only where the verse reveals it)—the logic of *ein ma’avirin al hamitzvos*, what does it accomplish here, seeing as this is not a derivation for every place, but only where the verse reveals it? And if so, where the verse reveals it, why do I need the logic?

## 44

It seems clear to me that according to the Rambam’s position, the *hatavas hamenorah* (preparation of the lamps) in the afternoon is likewise divided into two separate stages—the five lamps and the two lamps—as it must be identical to the procedure in the morning. This is the plain reading of the *Yad* (Hilchos Temidin U’musafin 3:16-17, and end of chapter 6) and the *Peirush HaMishnayos* (Tamid, chapter 6), and it also includes an interruption for the *ketores*. The *Siddur Yaavetz* cites support for the existence of an interruption in the afternoon as well, based on the opinion of the Rashba. However, this proof requires further study (*tzarich iyun gadol*), because the Rashba’s proof on this matter is based on his commentary at the beginning of chapter 6 of *Tamid*, which refers to the afternoon, whereas regarding the morning *menorah* service, it is possible that he holds there was no interruption (see *Tosafos Yom Tov*, *Tamid*, end of chapter 3 and beginning of chapter 6).

28. Having clarified the Rambam’s position regarding the *hatavas haneiros* (preparation of the lamps) in the morning, we must further explain his position regarding the *hatavas haneiros* in the afternoon.

It seems clear to me that according to the Rambam’s position—that the *menorah* is prepared and lit in the morning as well, and consequently, the *menorah* must also be prepared in the afternoon —the *hatavas hamenorah* in the afternoon is also divided into two stages: the preparation of the five lamps and the preparation of the two lamps.

This is because the *hatavas haneiros* must be identical in the morning and in the afternoon, as is the plain reading of the language of the *Yad* (Hilchos Temidin U’musafin 3:16-17): "The *dishun* (clearing) of the *menorah* and the *hatavas haneiros* in the morning and in the afternoon is a *mitzvah asei* (positive commandment)." He continues, "He would not prepare all the lamps at once; rather, he would prepare five lamps, pause, perform another *avodah*, and then enter and prepare the two... And what is the order of the *hatavah*? The one who merited the *dishun* of the *menorah* enters with a vessel in his hand, called a *kuz*... he clears the wicks that had gone out and the oil remaining in the lamp, and he prepares five lamps and leaves the *kuz* there before the *menorah*... and exits, and then enters and prepares the two lamps, etc." He concludes (ibid., end of chapter 6): "And the order followed every day in the morning is the same order followed in the afternoon, except for..." And the language of the *Peirush HaMishnayos* (Tamid 6:3): "Know that in the afternoon, it is also impossible to be without... the *dishun* of the *menorah*, as we mentioned regarding the morning."

In both instances—in the morning and in the afternoon—there is an interruption between the preparation of the five lamps and the preparation of the two lamps, also by means of the *ketores* (in accordance with the opinion of the Chachamim).

It should be noted that the *Siddur Yaavetz* brings support for the existence of an interruption between the preparation of the five lamps and the preparation of the two lamps in the afternoon as well (just as in the morning), based on the opinion of the Rashba. However, this proof requires further study, because the *Yaavetz*’s proof from the Rashba’s opinion on this matter is based on his commentary at the beginning of chapter 6 of *Tamid*, where this *mishnah* refers to the afternoon, whereas regarding the morning *menorah* (to which he refers at the end of chapter 3), it is possible that he holds there was no interruption between the preparation of the five lamps and the preparation of the two lamps (see *Tosafos Yom Tov*, *Tamid*, end of chapter 3 and beginning of chapter 6).

## מה

According to the interpretation of the Rambam and the majority of the commentators, the proof is nullified and the entire structure collapses.
