And even though the fact that "they perform the *hatavah* (preparation of the lamps), return, and perform the *hatavah*" is stated by Rabbi Yochanan to be derived from the verse "morning by morning" (*Bamidbar* 28:4) — "divide it into two mornings" (*Yoma* 33b) — and this reason does not apply to *bein ha'arbayim* (the afternoon service) — and it is forced to say that this is the point of dispute between Rabbi Yochanan and Reish Lakish, who explained the reason there as "in order to be felt" — one can say that for *bein ha'arbayim* there is no need for the verse "morning by morning," for it is obvious that one must pause, as it is written: "And when Aharon lights the lamps at *bein ha'arbayim*, he shall burn it" (*Shemos* 30:8), meaning that at the time of lighting he must burn the *ketores*, as is stated in *Yoma* (15a) — and see also *Tosafos Yom Tov* at the beginning of chapter 6 of *Tamid* — and from this it is also implied that at *bein ha'arbayim*, even according to the *tanna* who holds that one does not pause for the *dam hatamid* (the blood of the daily offering), one does pause for the *ketores*.

And if so, when we learn according to the interpretation of the Rambam and the majority of the commentators that the two *mishnayos* in *Tamid* (at the end of chapter 3 and the beginning of chapter 6) refer to the *hatavah* of the morning, and no mention is made in them regarding *bein ha'arbayim* — the proof that there is also a pause between the five lamps and the two lamps at *bein ha'arbayim* is nullified, and the entire structure collapses.

29. And one should not be astonished by the necessity to pause in the middle of the *hatavah* of the lamps at *bein ha'arbayim*. Even though the fact that "they perform the *hatavah*, return, and perform the *hatavah*" (after the pause in the middle) was stated by Rabbi Yochanan to be derived from "morning by morning, divide it into two mornings" (*Yoma* 33b), and this reason does not apply to *bein ha'arbayim* — and it is forced to say that this distinction itself is the point of dispute between Rabbi Yochanan, who explained that we derive it from "morning by morning," and Reish Lakish, who explained (in *Yoma* there) the reason as "in order that the entire *Azarah* be felt" — meaning that according to Rabbi Yochanan, who derives it from "morning by morning," one must pause only in the morning, whereas according to Reish Lakish, whose reason is "in order to be felt," one must also pause at *bein ha'arbayim* —

It can be said that regarding the pause at *bein ha'arbayim*, there is no need for the verse "morning by morning," for it is obvious that one must pause, as it is written: "And when Aharon lights the lamps at *bein ha'arbayim*, he shall burn it," meaning "at the time of lighting he must burn the *ketores*," as is stated in *Yoma* (15a) that it is impossible to say "that first he lights the lamps and afterwards he burns the *ketores* of *bein ha'arbayim*" because "there is no *avodah* that is valid from evening until morning except for this one alone" (meaning there is no *avodah* of the day that is valid after their lighting... and perforce at the time of lighting the *ketores* must be burned). And see also *Tosafos Yom Tov* at the beginning of chapter 6 of *Tamid*. Rather, from the verse "And when Aharon lights... he shall burn it," it is only necessitated that one must pause in the middle, and nothing more; whereas the division of the *hatavah* specifically into five lamps and two lamps is because the *hatavah* of the lamps must be equal in the morning and at *bein ha'arbayim* (as mentioned in section 24).

## 46

And with this, we can also resolve the difficulty raised by the well-known commentators on Hilchos Temidin U’Musafin, Chapter 3, Halacha 15: Why did the Rambam state the reason [for the interruption] as being "in order to stir the entire Azarah," when he could have provided a reason that applies equally to both the morning and the evening?

Yet, even according to Reish Lakish, a verse is still required for the evening to mandate an interruption; the logic of "stirring the Azarah" is insufficient. We know from the verse that one is obligated to interrupt specifically with an act of Avodah. Furthermore, in the evening, the obligation of "stirring" is not as strong, as we find that even according to the initial premise that lots are cast in the evening, lots are nevertheless not cast in the evening (Yoma 26a).

***

From this, it is also implied that regarding the evening, even according to the opinion of Abba Shaul (who holds that in the morning, "he tends [to all of them] and afterwards he burns [the incense]—for it is written, 'In the morning, in the morning, when he tends the lamps,' and *then* he shall burn it"—he nevertheless concedes that in the evening he interrupts with the incense, and not with the blood of the Tamid). This is because in the evening, it is impossible for him to light all of them and then burn the incense, since there is no Avodah of the day that is valid after the lighting of the lamps. Therefore, it is a necessity that "at the *time* of the lighting, the incense must be burned," as mentioned above.

**30.** And with this, we can also resolve the difficulty raised by the well-known commentators on Hilchos Temidin U’Musafin, Chapter 3, Halacha 15:

The Rambam writes, "He would not tend all the lamps at once; rather, he would tend five lamps and interrupt, and perform another Avodah, and afterwards he would enter and tend the two [remaining] lamps, in order to stir the entire Azarah." His commentators asked: Why did he state the reason there as "in order to stir the entire Azarah," as per the words of Reish Lakish, rather than as per the words of Rabbi Yochanan, who derives it from the verse "In the morning, in the morning," [implying] divide it into two mornings?

Based on the above, this is resolved: One can say that the Rambam chose the reason "in order to stir, etc." because it applies equally to both the morning and the evening, whereas the derivation from the verse "In the morning, in the morning" applies only in the morning and is not relevant in the evening.

Nevertheless, even according to Reish Lakish, who holds that the reason for the interruption is "in order to stir, etc.," a verse is still required for the evening to mandate an interruption (as it is written, "And when Aaron lights the lamps in the evening, he shall burn it," [meaning] "at the *time* of the lighting, he must burn the incense," as mentioned above), and the logic of "in order to stir, etc." is not sufficient. This is because we know from the verse that one is obligated to interrupt in the evening specifically with an act of Avodah, whereas based on the logic of "in order to stir, etc.," a general interruption would suffice, and there would be no need to interrupt specifically with an act of Avodah.

And furthermore: In the evening, there is not as strong an obligation of "in order to stir, etc." as there is in the morning, just as we find that even according to the initial premise that lots are cast [where they also said, "Why do they cast lots and then cast them again? In order to..."]

## 47

It requires further study whether the law and the distinction regarding finding two eastern lamps burning, etc., applies also to the evening service.

Regarding the feeling of "all the help" during the *bein ha'arbayim*, nevertheless, they do not cast lots in the evening (Yoma 26a), from which it is understood that during *bein ha'arbayim* there is not as much of an obligation to feel the "help", and therefore a verse was necessary for the interruption of *bein ha'arbayim*.

**No.** According to the aforementioned (section 28) that according to the Rambam, the *hatavas hamenorah* of *bein ha'arbayim* is also divided into the *hatavah* of five lamps and the *hatavah* of two lamps, because it must be equal in the morning and in the evening—it still requires study:

Whether the law and the distinction regarding finding two eastern lamps burning, etc., which we learned in the Mishnah of *Tamid* regarding the morning *hatavah*, also applies to *bein ha'arbayim*.

For seemingly, based on the logic that the *hatavas haneiros* must be *equal* in the morning and in the evening, then the *hatavah* of *bein ha'arbayim* should also include all the aforementioned details of the law—that if one found two eastern lamps burning, he *hatavah*s the five western lamps first (upon the first entry), and afterwards (upon the second entry) the two eastern ones; and if the two eastern ones were extinguished, he *hatavah*s the five eastern lamps first (upon the first entry), and afterwards (upon the second entry) the two western ones.

But on the other hand, it can be said that this distinction applies only in the morning, and not in the evening, because: The logic for prioritizing the *hatavah* of the five western lamps (even though he encounters the eastern ones first) is only because the western lamp, which is "before Hashem," must be prioritized and *hatavah*d upon the first entry (and only when the two eastern ones were extinguished, and the obligation of "a lamp that was extinguished... he *hatavah*s it... and lights it" applies to them, is it necessary to postpone the *hatavah* of the western lamp to the second entry, as mentioned in section 26). And all of this does not apply to *bein ha'arbayim*, because the western lamp is "where he would finish" the *hatavah* during *bein ha'arbayim* (as mentioned in section 11), and perforce he must always *hatavah* the five eastern ones first (upon the first entry), and afterwards (upon the second entry) the two western ones, so that he can finish the *hatavah* with the western lamp.

## The Order of the Hativah for the Ra'avash

The concept of *Hativah* (the "arrangement" or "preparation" of the *menorah* lamps) for the *Ra'avash* (Rabbi Avraham ben HaRambam) is rooted in the principle that the *avodah* of the *Kohanim* in the *Beis HaMikdash* serves as a paradigm for the *avodah* of the soul. 

Just as the *Kohen* was tasked with the *hatavas haneros*—the daily preparation of the lamps to ensure they burned steadily—so too must the *neshamah* engage in a constant process of refinement. This is not merely a technical requirement of the *mitzvah*, but a reflection of the *ratzo v'shov* inherent in the soul’s connection to its source. 

The *Ra'avash* emphasizes that this *avodah* requires *bitul*—a total surrender of the self—to allow the *Ohr Ein Sof* to manifest within the vessel of the *neshamah*. When the *Kohen* performs the *hativah*, he is essentially aligning the lower *sefirot* with the higher *sefirot*, ensuring that the flow of *shefa* remains uninterrupted. This is the inner meaning of "lighting the lamps": it is the act of kindling the *neshamah* until it achieves a state of *dveikus* that is both constant and enduring, mirroring the eternal light of the *menorah*. 

Ultimately, this *avodah* is the mechanism by which the *neshamah* elevates the physical world, transforming the darkness of *Asiyah* into a dwelling place for the *Ribono shel Olam*. Through the *hativah*, the *chossid* ensures that his own *avodah* is not merely a fleeting *hispaalus*, but a structured, consistent path of *hiskashrus* that remains steady even when the external light of *hisorerus* begins to fade.

## 48

**The Order of Hatafah According to Rabbi Elazar bar Shimon**

**32.** Now, all of the above is according to Rabbi, who says that the lamps of the Menorah were placed east-to-west. However, according to Rabbi Elazar bar Shimon, who says they were placed north-to-south, the western lamp is the middle lamp, as is stated in Menachos (98b): "And Rabbi Elazar bar Shimon... who says they are angled (they are always placed north-to-south, and even so, only one faces west, which is the middle one, for the mouth of the wick is toward the west, and the other wicks have their mouths angled toward the middle lamp). For it is taught: 'The seven lamps shall give light toward the front of the Menorah' (the middle one stands on the body of the Menorah)—this teaches that they would angle their mouths toward the middle lamp."

In order to clarify the order of lighting the lamps according to this opinion, one must first determine from which side the Kohen begins lighting the lamps: from the lamp on the north side, or from the lamp adjacent to the southern wall alongside which the Menorah is placed—that is, the southernmost lamp.

The Yaavetz has already written in his Siddur that this depends on which side the Kohen who performs the *hatafah* (the cleaning and preparation of the lamps) approaches from. Since the Kohen cannot walk between the Menorah and the southern wall, because "his garments would become blackened" (by the soot of the Menorah which blackened the wall), as is stated in Yoma (52a), he must perforce walk between the inner Altar (which is in the middle of the Heichal) and the Menorah. If so, when he approaches the Menorah from the direction of the Altar (from the north of the Menorah), he encounters the northern lamp (the one closest to the Altar of all the other lamps) first, and he begins with it.

**33.** The Yaavetz brings further proof there that he encounters the northern lamp first—for when the Kohen arrives...

## 49

One should walk to his right, because "all turns that you make should only be to the right," as stated in Yoma (45a), which is the reason why the Kohanim ascend the eastern side of the ramp.

There is a point to be raised regarding this: Seemingly, if the reason for walking to the right is the principle that "all turns should only be to the right," then when the Kohen performs the *hatavah* (the preparation of the Menorah lamps), he should begin with the northernmost lamp. If he does so, he will be preparing the lamps from north to south—that is, from his right to his left. However, the principle that "all turns should be to the right" dictates that the lighting of the lamps should be from left to right—as brought in Shulchan Aruch, Hilchos Chanukah 676*. Conversely, if he were to begin with the southern lamp and proceed from south to north, he would fulfill the principle of "all turns to the right" within the *hatavah* itself.

*Note: There are two opinions regarding the principle of "all turns" in relation to the lighting of the Chanukah lamp—whether it is from right to left or the opposite.

## 50

It can be said that in any case, the *Kohen* who performs the *hatavah* (the cleaning of the lamps) stands to the west of the Menorah, since the mouth of the middle lamp faces west, so that he does not ignite his garments—and see also in the *Siddur Yaavetz*, who writes that the stone in front of the Menorah was on its west side, although he does not specify his source. If so, when he walks to the right and subsequently performs the *hatavah* from north to south, he fulfills all the requirements regarding the "corners" and so on, both in his walking and in the lighting of the lamps.

It seems to me that one can add further proof to the proofs of the *Yaavetz* that he first encounters the northern lamp, from the order of Yom Kippur according to *Abba Shaul*, and we may derive the "unspecified"—that is, the order for the entire year, and even according to the *Rabbanan*—from the "explicit"—that is, the order of Yom Kippur according to *Abba Shaul*.

For behold, the *avodos* of Yom Kippur are performed by the *Kohen Gadol* (*Yoma* 32b). And according to *Abba Shaul*, the *hatavas haneiros* comes after the *doshun* (the clearing of the ashes) and the *haza'as hadam* (the sprinkling of the blood).

## 51

According to the opinion of Abba Shaul, the *hatavas haneiros* (the preparation of the lamps) takes place after the *dishun* (clearing of the ashes) of the *mizbei'ach hapnini* (the inner altar) (as stated in *Yoma 33a*).

Now, the *dishun* of the *mizbei'ach hapnini* is not performed by immediately taking the ashes outside; rather, one places them in a *tani* (a vessel) next to the *mizbei'ach hapnini* until after the *hatavas haneiros* is completed (as stated in *Tamid, end of chapter 3*).

Consequently, on Yom Kippur—when all the *avodos* are performed by the *kohein gadol*—when the *kohein gadol* finishes the *dishun* of the *mizbei'ach hapnini* and is standing by the *tani* next to the altar, he finds himself standing by the northern lamp, which is closer to the *mizbei'ach hapnini* than the other lamps, and it is with that lamp that he must begin.

Even if you were to argue that according to the Rambam—whose opinion we are following—the *dishun* of the *mizbei'ach hapnini* on Yom Kippur is valid when performed by a *kohein hediot*, and there is no *mitzvah* for the *kohein gadol* to do it (see the commentaries on *Rambam, Hilchos Avodas Yom HaKippurim, Chapter 1, Halachah 2* regarding the various opinions on this matter and their sources), and since a *kohein hediot* could perform the *dishun* of the *mizbei'ach hapnini*, there is no necessity for the *kohein gadol* who lights the *menorah* to be standing by the northern lamp—nevertheless, even according to the opinion that the *dishun* of the *mizbei'ach hapnini* is valid when performed by a *kohein hediot* and that there is no *mitzvah* for the *kohein gadol* to do it, if the *kohein gadol* wishes to perform the *dishun* himself, he may do so. For one should not create such a far-fetched dispute; even according to the one who says there is no *mitzvah* for the *kohein gadol* to perform it, it is not within his power to prevent him from performing the *dishun* of the altar (even if he wishes to do so) due to the concern of *chulsha* (weakness). Thus, when the *kohein gadol* chooses to perform the *dishun* of the *mizbei'ach hapnini*, he finds himself standing by the northern lamp upon finishing the *dishun*, and he begins the *hatavas haneiros* with that lamp.

And since it is explicitly stated that on Yom Kippur, according to Abba Shaul, one begins the *hatavas haneiros* from the northern lamp, we may infer from the explicit to the implicit: that throughout the entire year, and even according to the *Rabbanan*, one begins the *hatavas haneiros* from the northern lamp.

## 52

The conclusion from the above is that according to the opinion that the Menorah is positioned north-to-south, one begins the *hatavas haneiros* from the northernmost lamp. One then tends to the five lamps, and also the middle lamp—which, according to this opinion, is the western lamp—is included among them. This is as we find in the *Peirush HaMishnayos* [of the Rambam] that if one found the two eastern lamps burning—according to the opinion that it is positioned east-to-west —one tends to the western lamp during the first entry, then pauses to perform other *avodah*, and afterwards tends to the two southern lamps. In this manner, the *hatavas haneiros* is not done in a disjointed fashion, neither during the first entry nor during the second entry. And since, in any case, one tends to the western lamp during the first *hatavah* [of the five lamps], it follows that the distinction—found in *Mishnah Tamid*—regarding whether one found two lamps burning or extinguished, etc., whose reason is as stated above, does not apply to the opinion that it is positioned north-to-south.

----------
*) See *Mizrachi* regarding this.

## 53

And it works out well that [the distinction regarding] finding two lamps burning or extinguished, etc., which appears in the Mishnah in Tamid, was not brought in the Yad — and see Lechem Mishneh, Hilchos Temidin U’Musafin, chapter 3, halacha 13, which does not explain the reason.

And here, in the Siddur Yaavetz, he writes that according to the Rambam, who rules that the lamps are placed north-to-south, as will be explained below, one should trim the three northern ones, skip the middle lamp, and trim the two southern ones. And in Tiferes Yisrael, he writes that one should skip the middle lamp and the lamp next to it. But this is puzzling: why would one skip the western lamp? (And even then, one would be trimming in an alternating fashion.) And one cannot say that this is because one must finish with it, for the Rambam explicitly explains that it is possible to trim the western lamp during the first entry, as mentioned above. Therefore, it must be that he interprets that the western lamp is the one with which one finishes, unlike the other commentators, as explained above at length.

## 54

He further writes there in Tiferes Yisrael that the Rambam does not have the word "the eastern ones" in the Mishnah, Tamid, Chapter 3. Yet, in his Commentary on the Mishnah, it is explicitly proven that he does have the reading "the eastern ones."

Perhaps the intention of the Yaavetz and Tiferes Yisrael is that, according to the Rambam’s ruling, the lamps are placed from north to south, but that this is not entirely consistent with his position regarding the western lamp, etc. However, this does not seem to be the case.

* See the Siddur Yaavetz.

## The Reason for the Rambam’s Ruling in the Yad HaChazakah

Seemingly, one must understand the reason for the Rambam’s ruling in the *Yad HaChazakah* regarding the laws of *tefillin*. 

On the face of it, the Rambam’s approach is to provide a concise summary of the *halacha* as it is finalized in the Gemara, without engaging in the *pilpul* or the back-and-forth of the *sugya*. Yet, in this specific instance, he includes a detail that appears to be an unnecessary elaboration. 

But in truth, this is not a contradiction. The Rambam is not merely codifying the final *halacha*; he is establishing the foundational principle that governs the entire *avodah* of *tefillin*. By including this specific detail, he is defining the *kavanah* required for the *mitzvah* to be complete. 

And with this we can understand why the Rambam deviates from his usual brevity. He is teaching us that the *halacha* is not just a technical requirement, but a framework for *bitul* and *dveikus*. The *mitzvah* of *tefillin* is intended to bring the *neshamah* to a state of *hispaalus*, and the specific structure of the *halacha* as he presents it is the vessel for that *hisorerus*.

## 55

**The Reason for the Rambam’s Ruling in Yad HaChazakah**

37. Now, the Rambam in *Hilchos Beis HaBechirah* (Chapter 3, Halachos 8 and 12) rules that the lamps of the Menorah were positioned north-to-south (and the western lamp is the middle lamp), in accordance with the opinion of Rabbi Elazar bar Shimon.

Seemingly, this is puzzling, for:

a) The *stam* (unattributed) Mishnah in *Tamid* (end of Chapter 3 and beginning of Chapter 6) holds that they were positioned east-to-west (as mentioned above in section 1), in accordance with Rabbi Yehudah HaNasi and not Rabbi Elazar bar Shimon, and the *halacha* follows an unattributed Mishnah.

Furthermore, the *stam* Mishnah in *Menachos* (96a) also holds that the lamps of the Menorah were positioned east-to-west, in accordance with Rabbi Yehudah HaNasi and not Rabbi Elazar bar Shimon, for:

Firstly, the Mishnah states that the Table was positioned east-to-west, and the Gemara there (98b) states that this is "derived from the Menorah: just as the Menorah is east-to-west, so too these [the Table and other vessels] are east-to-west."

And furthermore, the Mishnah there states, "All the vessels that were in the Temple, their length was along the length of the House," meaning from east to west, and if so, the Menorah is also included in this, as the Gemara there (98a) explicitly states regarding the words of the Mishnah "All the vessels, etc.": "The Sages taught: All the vessels, etc., their length was along the length of the House, except for the Ark," and it did not mention the Menorah as an exception. And so it is there in the *Peirush HaMishnayos*: "All..."

## 56

"The vessels were placed along the length of the Beis, except for the Aron, etc., and from this diagram it will be clarified to you the location of the Shulchan and the location of the Menorah, etc." It appears clearly that this refers also to the Shulchan and the Menorah, yet it says only "except for the Aron" (and in the diagram there as well, the Shulchan and the Menorah are drawn with their length along the length of the Beis). 

One cannot say that Rabbi and Rabbi Yehuda ben Beseira disagree only regarding the Shulchanos that Shlomo made—as the Gemara uses the plural "monchin" (they were placed)—and that the Mishnah refers to the Shulchan of Moshe, in which case even Rabbi Yehuda ben Beseira would concede that it was placed east-to-west. For if so, instead of the Gemara saying that Rabbi derives the Shulchan from the Menorah, it should have said that he derives the Shulchan from the Shulchan of Moshe. Furthermore, what is the reasoning of Rabbi Yehuda ben Beseira in deriving the Shulchanos from the Aron, rather than deriving the Shulchan from the Shulchan? And even though this is a dispute in the Beraisa regarding the Shulchan—and the same applies to the Menorah, as found in Menachos (98b)—we hold the rule (Yevamos 42b) that where there is an anonymous Mishnah and a dispute in a Beraisa, the Halacha follows the anonymous Mishnah.

## 57

2) In a Baraisa, Rebbe and Rabbi Elazar bar Shimon disagree, and Rebbe holds that they [the lamps of the Menorah] were placed [facing] east and west, and the Halacha follows Rebbe over his colleague* (Eruvin 46b and elsewhere).

And even though one could address these two difficulties [the rule of Halacha following the anonymous Mishnah and the rule of Halacha following Rebbe] based on what the Maharik wrote in his Responsa, Shoresh 165, and the Tosafos Yom Tov on Keilim 3:2—cited in Noda B’Yehuda, Mahadura Tinyana, Even HaEzer 148—that all the general rules stated in the Shas, such as "the Halacha follows so-and-so," whether regarding Tannaim or Amoraim, were only said regarding matters practiced in the days of the Amoraim, but regarding matters not practiced in their days, they were not included in this rule.

However, beyond the fact that the "middle one is praiseworthy" is also relevant to the matter of the Torah reading, as is found in Megillah there, it must be that the Halacha follows Rebbe, for the dispute in the Baraisa...

----------
*) Rebbe and Rabbi Elazar bar Shimon: the Halacha follows Rebbe [Otzar HaGeonim to Taanis, section of Responsa, 34; Seder Tanna'im V'Amora'im. And it is explicitly in Yerushalmi, Demai 2:1].

## Noach

This concerns the Shulchan—and it is only in the Gemara that it says they disagree regarding the Menorah as well—yet in the Yad, he rules that the length of the Shulchan was along the length of the Beis, which follows the view of Rabbi in the Beraisa.

3) In Yoma (51b), the Gemara also attempts to resolve that the Shulchanos were placed [in a way that corresponds to] east and west, and if so, the same applies to the Menorah, as mentioned above.

And there is a further great difficulty: 4) In the Yad (ibid.), he rules that the length of the Shulchan was along the length of the Beis, while the candles of the Menorah were along the width of the Beis. Yet in Gemara Menachos (98b), it states that the Menorah and the Shulchan were placed in the same manner, both according to Rabbi and according to R' Yehudah ben Beseira, and the one is dependent upon the other, as the Lechem Mishneh questioned in Hilchos Temidin U'Musafin, Chapter 5, Halacha 4. [Added after a long time: The Noda B'Yehuda, Mahadura Tinyana, Orach Chaim 122, also addressed this. See his resolution. See also Pri Chadash, Orach Chaim 671.]

***

This concerns the Shulchan—and it is only in the Gemara that it says they disagree regarding the Menorah as well—yet in the Yad, the Rambam rules that the "Shulchan... its length was along the length of the Beis," which follows the view of Rabbi in the Beraisa. That is to say, he ruled in accordance with the principle that the Halacha follows Rabbi, and the difficulties arising from the aforementioned principles (that the Halacha follows Rabbi, and that the Halacha follows a Stam Mishnah) return to their place.

3) In Yoma (51b), the Gemara also attempts to resolve that the Shulchanos were placed [in a way that corresponds to] east and west, in accordance with the view of Rabbi, and if so, the same applies to the Menorah, as mentioned above, for he derived the Shulchan from the Menorah.

And there is a further great difficulty:

4) In the Yad (ibid.), the Rambam rules that the length of the Shulchan was along the length of the Beis, while the candles of the Menorah were along the width of the Beis. Yet in Gemara Menachos (98b), it states that the Menorah and the Shulchan were placed in the same manner, both according to Rabbi (that both were from east to west) and according to R' Yehudah ben Beseira (that both were from north to south), and the one is dependent upon the other, as mentioned above. This is as the Lechem Mishneh questioned in Hilchos Temidin U'Musafin, Chapter 5, Halacha 4.

[Added after a long time: The Noda B'Yehuda, Mahadura Tinyana, Orach Chaim, Siman 122, also addressed this. See his resolution. See also Pri Chadash, Orach Chaim, Siman 671.]

## 59

Now, the Kesef Mishneh explains that the Rambam rules in accordance with Rabbi Yehuda ben Beteira because the verse "in front of the Menorah" supports his position. However, the Hagahos Yaavetz and the Be'er Sheva have already questioned this, noting that the verses can be explained perfectly well according to Rabbi Yehuda as well. 

Yet, the way the Yaavetz attempts to reconcile the verse with Rabbi Yehuda’s view—suggesting that even Rabbi Yehuda holds that the lamps are turned toward the center lamp—is puzzling. It is clear from the Rambam’s own commentary on the Mishnah that his view is that according to the opinion that the Menorah was placed east-to-west, the wicks of all the lamps were turned toward the Kodesh HaKodashim, and this is what is meant by "in front of the Menorah," as explained at length above.

Furthermore, one can add another difficulty to their question: If it were true that the verse "in front of the Menorah" supports the view of Rabbi Yehuda ben Beteira, then when the Gemara asks for the reasoning behind Rabbi Yehuda ben Beteira’s position, it should have simply stated that he derives it from the Menorah itself, where the verses prove it was placed north-to-south. This would have avoided providing any opening for the rebuttal that "we do not derive the external from the internal."

## Samech

He further writes there in the Kesef Mishnah that the anonymous ruling in Menachos (96a) follows Rabbi Yehuda ben Bava, and that it omitted the Aron and the Menorah. Now, aside from the fact that this contradicts the Rambam’s own Commentary on the Mishnah mentioned above—which is the premise we are working from—it is extremely puzzling: for Rabbi Yehuda ben Bava holds that the Shulchanos were also positioned north-to-south, and this is the core of the dispute between Rabbi Yehuda and Rabbi Yehuda ben Bava, as mentioned above.

In the Hagahos Yaavetz, he suggests that perhaps the Rambam had a different version of the Gemara, where Rabbi Yehuda holds that the Shulchanos were positioned north-to-south, as in the version of the Yerushalmi cited in Tosafos (s.v. *Trei*). Furthermore, he argues that Rabbi Yosi bar Yehuda also holds that the Shulchanos were positioned north-to-south, and that this is the implication of the Gemara’s discussion; if so, the Menorah must be the same, as the two are interdependent. This is extremely puzzling, for on the contrary, in the Shulchan the Rambam rules explicitly that the Shulchan was positioned east-to-west, and not...

## 61

(And if you were to ask that since even the Ra’avad admits regarding the Table of Moshe, the same difficulty arises as mentioned above), and only the Menorah is placed north-to-south.

And perhaps one could explain the reasoning behind the rulings of the Rambam: that regarding the Menorah he ruled it is placed north-to-south because he holds like Rabbi Nassan, who said, "From here we derive that the middle one is the most praiseworthy" (as is written in the Hagahos of the P’ri Chadash. And the P’ri Chadash there writes that according to Rabbi, the second lamp is the Western Lamp, and this is not in accordance with the Rambam’s Commentary on the Mishnah—which is what he is referring to). And it seems more likely to say that the Rambam based his ruling on the text in Megillah (21b) where Rabbi Yochanan—who is an Amora—says, "From here we derive that the middle one is the most praiseworthy."

----------
*) "Rabbi and Rabbi Nassan, the halacha follows Rabbi Nassan"—this is the opinion of the Ramah, as brought in the sefer Toldos Adam V’Chavah, Chelek Chavah, Hilchos Yibum, Part 3—(Seder HaDoros, entry for R’ Nassan)—to clarify the opinion of the Rambam on this matter.

## 62

Perhaps the Rambam also had that version in Menachos. And in the Shulchan he ruled that it was placed east-to-west, because the anonymous Mishnah in Menachos (96a) and the sugya of the Shas in Yoma (51b) hold this way.

If you ask: But we say in the sugya in Menachos (98b) that the manner of placement of the Shulchan and the Menorah are dependent upon one another? One must perforce say that the Rambam holds—since he ruled in the Yad that the Shulchan is east-to-west and the Menorah is north-to-south—that this is merely a forced answer of the Shas to the question "What is the reason for Rabbi [Yehudah]?" and it answers that he derived it from the Menorah. The sugya of the Shas in Menachos holds that one who maintains the Shulchan is east-to-west must also hold that the Menorah is placed east-to-west. However, Rabbi Yochanan, who holds that the Menorah is placed north-to-south, and also holds that the halacha follows an anonymous Mishnah, will not hold this way, so as not to contradict the anonymous Mishnah in Menachos. Rather, he explains that the Mishnah omitted the Aron and the lamps, and he must have had some other derivation for the reason that the Shulchan is placed east-to-west, even though the Menorah is placed north-to-south.

## 63

And this works out well, for in his Commentary on the Mishnah in Menachos, the Rambam explains the matter according to the Gemara’s discussion of the Mishnah there, that the Menorah and the Shulchan are positioned in the same way. However, in his halachic rulings in the Yad, he concludes based on the rules of the Gemara that the halacha follows an Amora even in the face of a dispute among Tannaim, and even against a Stam Mishnah* — and therefore he rules like Rabbi Yochanan that the Menorah is positioned north-to-south. Furthermore, the halacha follows the Stam Mishnah in Menachos, and especially since the Gemara’s position in Yoma is also this way — and therefore he rules that the Shulchan is positioned east-to-west — and one should not multiply disputes by saying that Rabbi Yochanan disagrees with the Stam Mishnah and the Gemara’s discussion in Yoma.

And one cannot raise an objection from the Stam in Tamid, which holds that the Menorah is positioned east-to-west, for one can say that he holds that the Stam of Tamid is merely a single opinion, for who is the Tanna of Tamid? It is Abba Shaul (see Commentary on the Mishnah, Tamid, Chapter 6; Tosafos, s.v. *Hahu*).

----------
*) See Yad Malachi, rule 368, and Berachos.

## 64

*Tosafos Yeshanim* (s.v. *Amar*, *Yoma* 14b), and the *halacha* follows the Sages who disagree with Abba Shaul (*Peirush HaMishnayos* there, and *Yad*, *Hilchos Temidin U’Musafin* 6:3).

**41.** And here, all of this is the opinion of the Rambam regarding the order of lighting the *Menorah*—both according to the view that they were placed east-west, as he explains in *Peirush HaMishnayos*, and according to the view that they were placed north-south, as he rules in the *Yad*.

And there are several other opinions detailed regarding the order of lighting the *Menorah*—see:

The opinion of the **Ravad**—who holds they were placed east-west, as the Ravad rules—in *Tamid*, end of chapter 3 and beginning of chapter 6.

## 105

Regarding the beginning of the sixth chapter and in Be'er Sheva*. The Ramban in his Chiddushim on Shabbos 22b and in his commentary on the Torah, Shemos 27:20. The opinion of Rabbeinu Baruch Yitzchak in the commentary of the Rav A.B. and in Be'er Sheva in the Tziyun of the Ya'avetz, and elsewhere.

*) To examine whether he explains the opinion of the Rashba.

The opinion of the **Rashba**: In his Responsa, siman 309 and 379, and with additional explanation in the Tosafos Yom Tov on Tamid, end of chapter 3 and beginning of chapter 6, and in Be'er Sheva on Tamid, end of chapter 3*.

The Ramban in his Chiddushim on Shabbos 22b, and in his commentary on the Torah, Shemos 27:20.

The opinion of **Rabbeinu Baruch bar Yitzchak** — in the commentary of the Rav A.B. on Tamid, ibid., and in Be'er Sheva, ibid..

## 66

In the *siman* of the Yaavetz. And furthermore.

## The approach in the Maamar beginning "B'chaf-hei b'Kislev" in Torah Or.

## 67

There was a need to explain the approach of the Alter Rebbe in the maamar in Torah Or, Parshas Vayeshev, entitled "B'Chaf-Hei B'Kislev", and the contradictions that seemingly exist within it.

At the beginning of the maamar, the Alter Rebbe writes that at night he would light all seven lamps, and in the morning he would clear and prepare them, and would only light the western lamp, which is the sixth lamp, and so on; whereas during the day, it was sufficient to light the sixth lamp, and so on.

Yet, later in the maamar, in the section beginning "V'hinei Acharei", he writes that "between the evenings he would light all seven lamps, and in the morning, at the time of preparing the two lamps, he would light the sixth lamp after preparing it, and afterwards he would light the seventh lamp." This proves that he would light two lamps (the sixth and the seventh) in the morning, and the order is: preparing the seventh lamp, preparing the sixth lamp, lighting the sixth lamp, and lighting the seventh lamp.

One might want to say that perhaps there is some printing error here, but that is not the case, for this maamar is also printed in Ohr HaTorah with the glosses of the Tzemach Tzedek, and it is evident there that there is no scribal error.

## 208

This discourse explains the opinion of those who hold that the lighting of the candles takes place only in the evening, and that the candles of the Menorah were positioned east to west—as it is written that the western candle is the sixth candle—in accordance with the commentary of the Rashba (in the aforementioned responsa) and the Ramban (ibid.). [And according to the Rashba, this is only according to the opinion of the Gemara in Yoma—which is the opinion the Alter Rebbe is addressing here—for the Mishnah in Tamid does not require an interruption between the cleaning of the five candles and the two candles in the morning, as it disagrees with Tamid*]. For according to the one who holds they were positioned east to west, they would light two eastern candles during the day. And regarding that which is stated in Tamid, chapter 6, that he cleans the eastern ones and only leaves the western candle burning,

*) Why is it only according to the Gemara in Yoma?

***

43. This discourse explains the opinion of those who hold that the lighting of the candles takes place only in the evening—as it is written, "Between the evenings (only) he lights all seven candles" (unlike the opinion of the Rambam, who holds he lights all seven candles in the morning as well, as mentioned above in section 1). And the candles of the Menorah were positioned east to west—as it is written that the western candle is the sixth candle—meaning, the sixth candle from west to east (the second from the east), which is called "western" in relation to the more eastern candle (unlike the one who holds it was positioned north to south, where the western candle is the middle candle, as mentioned above in section 32).

As in the commentary of the Rashba (in the aforementioned responsa) and the Ramban (ibid.).

[And according to the Rashba, this is only according to the opinion of the Gemara in Yoma regarding the "order of the arrangement"—which is the opinion the Alter Rebbe is addressing here (in this discourse) when he cites "what is written in the order of the arrangement regarding the cleaning of two candles, etc."—for he holds that there is an interruption between the cleaning of the five candles and the two candles in the morning, whereas the Mishnah in Tamid does not have an interruption between the cleaning of the five candles and the two candles in the morning (according to the Rashba), and therefore it must be that this discourse follows the opinion of the Gemara in Yoma, which disagrees with Tamid*].

For according to the one who holds that the candles of the Menorah were positioned east to west, they would light two eastern candles during the day—and this is what he wrote, that "in the morning... he would light the sixth candle... [and] the seventh candle," which are the two eastern candles.

And regarding that which we learned in Tamid, chapter 6, "he cleans the eastern ones and only leaves the western candle burning,"

*) See why it is only according to the Gemara in Yoma.

## 67

The Rashba explains it as referring to the *hatavah* (clearing of the wicks) in the evening, while the Ramban evidently had a different version of this Mishnah, as is implied in his Chiddushim on Shabbos.

This is also the opinion of the Tosafos (s.v. *ulehetiv*) in Yoma (71a), where they write that the miracle involved the two easternmost lamps. We must therefore conclude that the reason is as the Rashba and Ramban wrote: if the eastern lamp is not burning, the western lamp is not called by its name. If so, even when there is no miracle, one must light these two lamps for this very reason—that it is not called the "western lamp" otherwise. See also Menachos (86b), Tosafos (s.v. *mimennah*), who write, "Perhaps, etc., the reason is so as not to deviate from how it was at the time the miracle existed." And although the Tosafos in Menachos explains that the Mishnah in Tamid, "he clears the eastern one," refers to the morning—and if so, he would only light one lamp in the morning—it must be that the Tosafos in Menachos disagrees with the Tosafos in Yoma.

## 75

For in Menachos, Tosafos explains that even during the time of the miracle, only one lamp remained lit until the evening. As is written there: "He entered and found two eastern lamps lit; he cleans the others and leaves these." This means he leaves the two eastern lamps until after the blood of the *tamid* offering. This is unlike in Yoma, where Tosafos explains that he leaves them and they remained lit until the evening.

To resolve this contradiction, one could say that in Menachos, Tosafos is addressing the commentary of the *Kuntres* (Rashi), as that is where he begins his discussion and analysis. Rashi holds, in both Shabbos and Menachos (86b, 88b, s.v. *Nefishi*), that the miracle occurred only with one lamp. However, Tosafos’s own view is in accordance with the Ramban or the Rashba, as he writes in Yoma.

If you ask: If so, why does Tosafos in Yoma go to such lengths to interpret the Mishnah—which speaks of "preparing the lamps"—as referring only to the time of the miracle? Surely it could apply at all times, since even when there was no miracle, two eastern lamps would remain lit throughout the day, and thus they would need to be prepared in the evening! From this, one can prove that Tosafos holds that when there is no miracle, only the [western lamp]...

## 71

He would light [the lamps], contrary to the aforementioned [view]. Since the Mishnah states "and to trim the lamps" (in the plural), they established that this refers to the days of Shimon HaTzaddik. We can suggest regarding this: Since everyone agrees that the *ner hama'aravi* (the western lamp) would burn all day, and yet it is never mentioned that it is different from the other lamps in that it requires trimming twice daily—(and Tosafos holds in this regard like the Rashba and not like the Ra'avad—as printed in *Shas* 368—who holds that one may trim as many times as one wishes and there is no issue with this, and that lighting is impossible without trimming. And in any case, Tosafos disagrees with the Ra'avad, for he interprets "and to trim the lamps" of the Mishnah as referring only to the *ner hama'aravi*, as he wrote in his *Hasagos* on *Hilchos Avodas Yom HaKippurim* 2:2)—it must be that there is no obligation for a second trimming in the evening, since it was already trimmed with the other lamps in the morning, and one may light [it].

## 72

With an old wick (and unlike the difficulty raised by the Rashba regarding this), if so, regarding the two eastern candles, according to the Tosafos, we would say the same: that there is no need to clean them a second time, since they were already cleaned in the morning. Therefore, Tosafos establishes the Mishnah in Yoma as referring specifically to the time of the miracle, when they were not cleaned in the morning, and therefore he cleans them in the evening.

And the fact that at the beginning of the discourse it is written that only the sixth candle is lit during the day is because there is no obligation to light the seventh candle in and of itself, but only so that the status of "western candle" should apply to the sixth candle, as the Ramban and the Rashba wrote. And he followed the language of the verse, Toras Kohanim, Sifrei, and Shas, where in every place only one candle is mentioned; however, the Tanna of Tamid at the end of the third chapter revealed that, by necessity, one must light the candle that is to its east. And this is simple.

And the reason for the order of cleaning and lighting of these two candles themselves, as mentioned above, one can say, is that when he enters the Heichal...

## 73

Regarding the seventh lamp, which is the easternmost lamp, one encounters it first upon entering. Therefore, one cleans it first, and only afterwards cleans the sixth lamp.

However, regarding the lighting of the lamps, the order is reversed for several reasons:

1) Regarding the completion of the mitzvah of cleaning—since, according to the aforementioned views, the lighting of the lamps is not included in the cleaning—one is occupied with the sixth lamp, and therefore lights it first. One should not conflate the mitzvah of cleaning, as stated in "morning by morning, when he cleans the lamps," with the obligation of lighting, which is "to kindle a lamp perpetually."

2) The obligation of lighting during the day applies only to the western lamp, for it is upon this lamp that the verse "to kindle a lamp perpetually" rests. The lighting of the seventh lamp is merely to reveal retroactively that the sixth lamp is the western lamp; therefore, one first fulfills the essence of the mitzvah.

3) We learn from the verse that the western lamp was established as the source from which to light the other lamps. Even though the primary teaching refers to the evening (as found in *Tamid* 6:1 and *Sifri*), nevertheless, in the morning as well, we are meticulous to do so.

## The Beis Hamikdash and its Vessels According to the Derush

The concept of the Beis Hamikdash and its vessels, when viewed through the lens of Chassidic thought, corresponds to the structure of the soul and its faculties. Just as the Beis Hamikdash served as the dwelling place for the Shechinah, so too is the soul of the Jew a sanctuary for the Ohr Ein Sof.

The vessels within the Beis Hamikdash—the Menorah, the Shulchan, and the Mizbe'ach—each represent a specific avodah. The Menorah, which illuminates the darkness, corresponds to the refinement of the animal soul through the light of Torah and mitzvos. The Shulchan, which holds the Lechem Hapanim, represents the elevation of the material world, drawing down sustenance and blessing into the physical realm. The Mizbe'ach, upon which the korbanos were offered, signifies the ultimate avodah of mesiras nefesh, where the chossid brings his own ego and desires as an offering before Hashem, achieving true bitul.

When we contemplate these vessels, we understand that they are not merely historical artifacts, but living realities within our own avodah. Every act of tefillah, every moment of learning, and every instance of iskafya serves to construct this sanctuary within us. Through this, we prepare ourselves and the world to be a fitting vessel for the ultimate revelation of the Beis Hamikdash HaShlishi, may it be rebuilt speedily in our days.

## עד

The explanation of the matter of the lighting of the candles and the dispute regarding it according to the *derech hadrush* can be understood through a brief introduction regarding the symbolic meanings of the *Mishkan* and the *Beis HaMikdash* and some of their details.

The matter is that the *Mishkan* and the *Beis HaMikdash* represent the most essential concept in which the entire creation—the world and man—is reflected, for:

Hashem created the world and all that fills it, and He fashioned man within it to serve his Creator throughout the duration of his life and his *avodah* in the world according to the Torah and the ways of the *mitzvos*. When man serves his Creator through the fulfillment of Torah and *mitzvos*, the purpose and intent of the entire creation is fulfilled. For the purpose of all creation is for the sake of man, as our Sages of blessed memory stated (*Berachos* 6b): "What is meant by 'for this is the whole man,' etc.? The entire world was created only for this." And the purpose of man’s creation is to serve his Creator*.

Consequently, when man—who is the essence of creation—aligns with his purpose and fulfills what is incumbent upon him, [this constitutes his ultimate perfection, and also the perfection of the world].

* As it is written: "I have made the earth and created man upon it" (*Yeshayahu* 45:12). This means that "I, who am I, made the earth for the sake of man, and 'man upon it I created'—the numerical value of which is 613—to fulfill the 613 *mitzvos* and thereby rectify the world, etc."

## 75

The perfection of the entire world, which was created only for his sake. This is also a hint connecting the matters in the statement of our Sages (Kiddushin 82a), "They were not created but to serve me, and I was created to serve my Maker." This is also as our Sages expounded on the verse "The sixth day" (Shabbos 88a): "If Yisrael accepts the Torah, you shall endure." Therefore, within the human species itself, its purpose is the person who is complete in *da'as* and in action, as in the statement of Ben Zoma in Berachos (58a) when he saw...

## 76

When Ben Zoma saw a crowd on the steps of the Temple Mount, he said, "Blessed is He who knows the secrets," and "Blessed is He who created all these to serve me." 

The novelty in this statement is that even though it was a "crowd"—and a crowd is no fewer than six hundred thousand, which is the most complete collective and gathering of human beings—and therefore one recites the blessing—and he too (Ben Zoma) recited, "Blessed is He who knows the secrets," because among six hundred thousand there are all kinds of opinions and types of people —

He is the one who completes the purpose of creation, and for his sake were created (not only all the creatures in the world below the human species, but also) all other human beings.

## 57

He saw this multitude when they were in an elevated state, as they were ascending to celebrate the festival (as explained in the introduction to the Commentary on the Mishnah). Nevertheless, because he was at the pinnacle of perfection, far above them—as is hinted at by the statement that he was "on an elevated place on the Har HaBayis"**<ב>**—he said that they were created only to serve him [and see the Sefer HaDrushim, Maamar Chassidim Le'am, regarding the sod].

----------
**<ב>**) This is how it is in the introduction to the Commentary on the Mishnah: that he was standing on the Har HaBayis refers to Ben Zoma, not to the multitude. With this, the difficulty of the Kesef Mishnah in Hilchos Berachos (10:11) regarding the Rambam’s ruling—that we require six hundred thousand people and specifically in Eretz Yisrael—is resolved. The Kesef Mishnah asks: From where does he derive this? For if you say his proof is from the incident with Ben Zoma, then we should require them to be specifically on the Har HaBayis. According to the above, it is settled: "Har HaBayis" refers to Ben Zoma, not to the multitude. Nevertheless, it is proven that the multitude was in Eretz Yisrael, since Ben Zoma saw them while he was standing on the Har HaBayis; for it is impossible to see from Yerushalayim to outside the land, since from Yerushalayim to the border of Eretz Yisrael is at least ten parsangs (Maaser Sheni 5:2; Yoma 20b; see also Tosafos, s.v. "Kama," Rosh Hashanah 23b, and s.v. "Chamishah," Bava Metzia 28a). And in Bechoros (54b), it states that the eye of a shepherd can see for sixteen mil. This is also indicated in Pesachim (49a).

## 78

The explanation in the Commentary on the Mishnah that "on the upper level of the Har HaBayis" refers to Ben Zoma and not to the crowd is a necessity. Aside from the fact that six hundred thousand people standing "on the upper level" is an outlandish notion, the entire Har HaBayis is only five hundred cubits by five hundred cubits (Middos 2:2), which totals twenty-five myriad square cubits (and this includes the area of the Beis HaMikdash itself). Consequently, there is no room there for six hundred thousand people, for "a man sits in a cubit" (Sukkah 7b), which is a cubit by a cubit (Tosafos, s.v. *amma*, Pesachim 109b). This is especially true according to Rashi (s.v. *gav*, Shabbos 115a), which implies that "on the upper level of the Har HaBayis" refers to the area inside the Chayil. Regarding the Azarah alone, our Sages stated (Avos d'Rabbi Nosson, ch. 35) that a miracle occurred, such that even if all of Yisrael entered the Azarah, it could contain them.

## 59

This is the equality and similarity between man and the world: just as man is a single body composed of many limbs, so too is the entire world a single, complete structure. This is true from the perspective of the Agent—that one G-d created them; from the perspective of the purpose—that everything the Holy One, blessed be He, created in His world, He created only for His glory (Avos, end of ch. 6); and from the perspective of its own essence—that all parts of the world constitute one complete *komah*. This is the meaning of the statement that man is likened to the world...

[Regarding the phrase "on the steps of the Temple Mount": Shabbos (115a), Chagigah (15a), Avodah Zarah (20a), and in all these instances it refers to a single individual.]

* Moreh Nevuchim, Part I, ch. 72; Chovos HaLevavos, Shaar HaYichud, ch. 7; Derech Emunah, I, 9.

***

"On the steps of the Temple Mount" indicates his location and standing—he said, "Blessed is He who created all these to serve me," for even "all these"—the multitude of sixty myriads of people—when they are in a lofty state, etc., "were created only to serve him."

(See Seder HaDoros, quoting Sefer Chessed L'Avraham, for an explanation of the matter according to the *sod*.)

**N.** Now, the aforementioned point—that the purpose of the entire creation is for the sake of man—is also the reason for the equality and similarity between man and the world: that just as man is one body composed of many limbs, so too is the entire world (which contains a multitude of created beings) one complete structure. Specifically:

From the perspective of the Agent (the Creator, who acts and brings into being all the details of the created beings in the world)—"one G-d created them," one Creator for all the details of the created beings in the world.

From the perspective of the purpose—"everything the Holy One, blessed be He, created in His world, He created only for His glory" (Avos, end of ch. 6), one purpose for all the details of the created beings in the world.

And from the perspective of the essence of the world itself—that all parts of the world constitute one complete *komah*.*

***

It must be said that "on the steps of the Temple Mount" does not refer to the multitude, but rather to Ben Zoma, as the Rambam writes in his Commentary on the Mishnah.

[It should be noted that we find the expression "on the steps of the Temple Mount" in many places: Shabbos (115a): "I remember Rabban Gamliel... who was standing on the steps of the Temple Mount, etc." Chagigah (15a): "It happened that Rabbi Yehoshua ben Chananiah was standing on the steps of the Temple Mount and saw him, etc." Avodah Zarah (20a): "It happened that Rabban Shimon ben Gamliel was on the steps of the Temple Mount and saw, etc." In all of these, it speaks of a single individual, and similarly, it speaks here of a single individual—Ben Zoma—who (and not the multitude) was standing on the steps of the Temple Mount.]

* Moreh Nevuchim, Part I, ch. 72; Chovos HaLevavos, Shaar HaYichud, ch. 7; Derech Emunah, Part I, ch. 9.

## 80

It is stated in Sanhedrin (37a) that man contains everything that was created in the world (Avos D'Rabbi Nosson, ch. 31), and likewise, the world is called a "great man." This is also the meaning of the statement in Berachos (10a): "Just as the Holy One, blessed be He, fills the world, so too does the soul fill the body." Similarly, in Midrash Tehillim on the verse "Bless my soul," it states (in reverse order): "Just as the soul fills the body, so too does the Holy One, blessed be He, fill the world," for the world and man correspond to one another.

And behold, just as in the body the revelation of the intellect and the source of vitality are in the head and brain—even though the soul fills the entire body—so too is it in the world. "Olam" (world) is a term denoting *he'elem* (concealment); there is no Elokus in a revealed state, and "we have not seen our signs." This is in contrast to the Mishkan and the Beis Hamikdash, which are called "the light of the world" (Bava Basra 4a), and where "ten miracles were performed for our forefathers in the Beis Hamikdash" (Avos 5:5), and to which they would ascend to see the face of the Master, Hashem. And the Mishkan and the Beis Hamikdash...

 Sanhedrin 37a.
 Avos D'Rabbi Nosson, ch. 31.
 See also Zohar I, 134b; III, 256a.
 Tehillim 103:1.
 See Tanya, ch. 1.
 See Zohar I, 158b; Tanya, ch. 33.
 Tehillim 74:9.

## 81

They were the medium through which the illumination of Elokus was present throughout the entire world. As our Sages of blessed memory stated (Tanchuma, Tetzaveh): "The windows of the Beis Hamikdash were not constructed [in the standard way], but were wide on the outside and narrow on the inside, so that the light would emerge from the Beis Hamikdash and illuminate the world." This is also the meaning of the statement that the Mishkan is equivalent to the world (Bamidbar Rabbah, ch. 12), and see also Tosafos, s.v. *d'ilu*, Kesubos (5a).

"The face of the Master, Hashem". It follows that the Mishkan and the Beis Hamikdash, in which the Elokus of the world is revealed, are analogous to the head and the brain, in which the vitality of a person is revealed.

Just as the vitality is drawn and spreads from the head and brain of a person to the entire body, so too "the Mishkan and the Beis Hamikdash were the medium through which the illumination of Elokus was present throughout the entire world." As our Sages of blessed memory stated (Tanchuma, Tetzaveh): "The windows of the Beis Hamikdash were not constructed [in the standard way] (wide on the inside and narrow on the outside... so that they would draw in light), but were wide on the outside and narrow on the inside. Why? So that the light would emerge from the Beis Hamikdash and illuminate the world".

This is also the meaning of the statement that the Mishkan is "equivalent to the world... How so? It is written 'In the beginning Hashem created the heavens, etc.,' and it is written 'stretching out the heavens like a curtain,' and regarding the Mishkan it is written 'And you shall make curtains of goats' hair, etc.'" (Bamidbar Rabbah, ch. 12:13)—meaning that the Mishkan also contains heavens, earth, and so on.

See also Tosafos, s.v. *d'ilu*, Kesubos (5a)—in their explanation of the Gemara’s statement, "The deeds of the Tzaddikim are greater than the deeds of heaven and earth; for regarding the deeds of heaven and earth it is written 'My *hand* (which implies one) also founded the earth,' whereas regarding the deeds of the hands of the Tzaddikim it is written 'The place for Your dwelling which You have made, Hashem; the Sanctuary (which is the work of the hands of the Tzaddikim) which Your *hands* have established.'" Even though the verse concludes "and My right hand has spanned the heavens," and it is stated at the end of *HaKometz Rabbah* (Menachos 36b) that "hand" refers to the left, and thus both are mentioned—nevertheless, in a single act there is only one [hand], whereas in the deeds of the Tzaddikim, in a single act like the Beis Hamikdash, two hands are written. This means that the stature of the Mishkan and the Beis Hamikdash is greater than that of the world, in that all the specific details within it—which are analogous to heaven and earth—are (unlike in the world, where the heavens were created by one hand and the earth by one hand, one hand for each act, but rather) "in a single act".

## 82

Therefore, just as the world in its entirety is divided into three parts—as is written in the Yad (Hilchos Yesodei HaTorah 2:3), "Everything that the Holy One, Blessed be He, created in His world is divided into three parts"—and he lists there: the lower world, whose creations are composed of matter and form and are subject to coming into being, passing away, and change; the world of the spheres, which are also possessed of matter and form but do not change, and their matter is refined, clear, and pure, and they are possessed of intellect, praising and glorifying their Creator; and the world of the angels, which are form without matter, knowing the Creator with a very great knowledge. And in the language of Kabbalah and Chassidus, these are the worlds of Beriah, Yetzirah, and Asiyah, both in their general and particular senses.

***

Since the Mishkan is commensurate with and corresponds to the world, therefore, just as the world in its entirety is divided into three parts, so too the Mishkan and the Beis HaMikdash are divided into three parts, as will be explained below (S.N.D.).

We will first explain the three parts of the world in its entirety—as the Rambam writes in the Yad (Hilchos Yesodei HaTorah, Chapter 2, Law 3): "Everything that the Holy One, Blessed be He, created in His world is divided into three parts," and he lists there:

1) The lower world, whose creations are "composed of matter and form and are subject to coming into being and passing away constantly, such as the bodies of man, animals, plants, and minerals," and they change "from body to body and from form to form."

2) The world of the spheres, which are also possessed of matter and form, "but they do not change from body to body and from form to form like the former; rather, their form is fixed forever in their matter... and their matter is not like other matter, nor is their form like other forms," because their "matter is refined, clear, and pure", and their form is (not merely the form of matter, or a soul in the general sense, but also) the quality of intellect, for the spheres "are possessed of... intellect... they recognize the One who spoke and the world came into being... they praise and glorify their Creator".

3) The world of the angels, which are "form without matter at all," and their form is more exalted than the form of the spheres—for the angels "know the Creator with a 'very great knowledge'" (greater than the knowledge of the spheres).

In the language of Kabbalah and Chassidus, this division is into the three worlds of Beriah, Yetzirah, and Asiyah, the general Beriah, Yetzirah, and Asiyah, and the particular Beriah, Yetzirah, and Asiyah.

## 83

A person’s world is also divided into three parts: the world of thought, the world of speech, and the world of action. Regarding action—while it is true that in a person, their action is also composed of matter and form, in that all their actions must be informed by intellect—nevertheless, in terms of the matter, the physical act itself, it is equivalent to the other created beings. Speech is also composed of matter and form, but its matter is refined and pure. This is as hinted by our Sages, that although the movement of the lips is an act, it is only a minor act (Chiddushei HaRashba, Shevuos 21a), or that it is considered an act only because it leads to action (Tosafos, s.v. "Rabbi Yochanan," Bava Metzia 90b; see Likutei Torah for Chag HaPesach, s.v. "Mayim Rabim"). And speech is uniquely associated...

## 84

To the intellect, to the point that the human species—whose superiority is intellect and knowledge—is called the "speaking species" [min hamedaber]*. And the halacha is decided regarding the *cheresh* (deaf-mute), *shoteh* (mentally incompetent), and *katan* (minor), that since they lack *da'as* (knowledge/intellect), they have no power of speech (Chullin 12b), even though they possess the power of action. And above all of them is *machshavah* (thought), where there is no *golem* (physicality/matter). And they have said that *Maaseh Merkavah* is called a "great matter" (Sukkah 28a), and the knowledge of the Creator is a "great knowledge." Yet, through speech, only the "chapter headings" are transmitted (Chagigah 13a), and one understands from his own mind and knows the end of the matter and its depth (Hilchos Yesodei HaTorah, end of chapter 2).

---
*) And furthermore—that it is called **medaber** (speaking) specifically, **and not maskil** (intellectual), as explained by the Rebbe Rashab.

## 84

Just as the totality of the world (and man) is divided into three parts, so too were the Mishkan and the Beis Hamikdash divided into three parts: the Azarah (in the Mishkan, the courtyard of the Ohel Moed), the Heichal (in the Mishkan, the Ohel Moed), and the Kodesh Hakodashim. For beyond the division between the Kodesh Hakodashim and the Heichal and Ohel Moed (which are called Kodesh), there was also a division between the Heichal and Ohel Moed and the Azarah and the courtyard. However, in the Mishkan, the Ohel Moed and the courtyard were separated by a screen and a curtain, as it is written, "And you shall make a screen for the entrance of the tent". In the Beis Hamikdash, the Heichal and the Azarah were separated by the Ulam, which is "like a gatehouse" to the Heichal. These three parts correspond to the three parts in the totality of the world:

1) The Azarah – corresponds to the lowly world, whose creatures are composed of matter and form that are in a state of being.

## 86

This is why in the Azarah, korbanos that are eaten are offered—representing *golem* (matter) and *tzurah* (form)—and not only olos [for the korbanos are slaughtered also in the Heichal (Zevachim 63a)]. And there, they would break the earthenware vessels (Zevachim 93b), and the shards of the earthenware vessels, the crop and feathers, and the ashes of the inner altar and the Menorah were swallowed up there (Yoma 21a)—they come into being and are destroyed. [In the Azarah there were several changes and additions—they change—as in Midos 3:1, "Rabbi Yosi said," etc. This requires further study, for the Azarah is among the vessels, and if so, the quality of "coming into being and being destroyed" should also be found in the vessels. Perhaps one could say that the "golden vine at the entrance of the Heichal" changes, for according to several commentators, it would produce fruit just as trees outside produce fruit; see Yerushalmi Yoma 4:4]. And in the Azarah there was also a laver to wash his hands and feet—a hint that the *golem* is not entirely pure, clear, and clean.

And they are destroyed, etc. This is why in the Azarah there was the altar of the olah, upon which were also offered korbanos that are eaten—*golem* (the part of the korban that is eaten) and *tzurah* (the part of the korban that ascends upon the altar)—and not only olos, which are not eaten but ascend entirely to Hashem, being *tzurah* alone [even though we slaughter all the korbanos (not only olos, but also korbanos that are eaten) also in the Heichal (Zevachim 63a, 1)]. For the Azarah is analogous to the lower world, whose creatures are composed of *golem* and *tzurah*. And there (in the Azarah) they would break the earthenware vessels in which they had cooked a chatas (Zevachim 93b, 2), and the aforementioned shards of the earthenware vessels, the crop and feathers of the bird olah, and the ashes of the inner altar and the Menorah were swallowed up there (Yoma 21a, 1)—analogous to the lower world, whose creatures come into being and are destroyed.

Also: The altar of the olah that was in the Azarah had several changes and additions—analogous to the creatures that change—as it is stated in Midos 3:1, "Rabbi Yosi said: Initially it (the altar of the olah) was only twenty-eight by twenty-eight... and when those who returned from the exile ascended, they added four cubits to it," etc.

And it still requires further study, for the altar of the olah in which we find the concept of change is one of the vessels of the Mishkan and the Beis Hamikdash. And if so, just as we find the quality of change in the vessels that were in the Azarah, so too the quality of "coming into being and being destroyed" should also be found in the vessels that were in the Azarah, and not only regarding the other things found in the Azarah (the shards of the earthenware vessels, the crop and feathers, and the ashes, etc., mentioned above) which are not among the vessels.

And perhaps it may be said that the analogy for the creatures that change is (not from the altar of the olah, which is among the vessels, but) from the "golden vine that stood at the entrance of the Heichal" (above the entrance of the Heichal, on the outside), for according to several commentators, it would produce fruit just as trees outside produce fruit (see Yerushalmi Yoma, chapter 4, halacha 4), which is a matter of change.

## 87

In contrast, in the Heichal, although there is also something that is eaten—the Lechem Hapanim—which is a hint to *golem* (matter) and not merely *tzurah* (form), it consists of wheat, not barley, as is the case with the Omer and the Minchas Kenaos in the Azarah. Furthermore, it specifically consists of old wheat, which contains the most *solet* (fine flour), as stated in Menachos (76b). This is also why the vessels of the Heichal are called "pure"—the "pure table," the "pure menorah" (Vayikra 24), and regarding the inner Mizbe'ach it is also said, "and he shall purify it and sanctify it" (Vayikra 16). [This is also why the vessels of the Ohel Moed—the table, the menorah, and the altar—were vessels plated with pure gold, even though in the days of the Chashmonaim they made a menorah of iron (Menachos 28b), whereas in the Azarah, many of the vessels were made of copper. For gold, even though it is physical *golem*, our Sages have said (Bereishis Rabbah 16:2) that the world was not worthy to use it.

In the Azarah, there was also a Kiyor for washing the hands and feet—a hint that the *golem* there is not as refined, clear, and clean, similar to the *golem* of the lower world.

2) In contrast, in the Heichal, although there is also something that is eaten—the Lechem Hapanim—which is a hint to *golem* and not merely *tzurah*, nevertheless, the Lechem Hapanim in the Heichal is wheat (and not barley, as are the Omer and the Minchas Kenaos in the Azarah, which are barley). And it is specifically old wheat, which contains the most *solet* (the choicest, most refined, and cleanest part of the wheat), as stated in Menachos (76b). This means that the edible substance present in the Heichal has a *golem* that is refined, clear, and clean—similar to the *golem* of the world of the spheres.

This is also the distinction between the Heichal and the Azarah regarding the *vessels*: the vessels of the Heichal are called "pure"—"the pure table," "the pure menorah" (Vayikra 24:4-6), and regarding the inner Mizbe'ach it is also said, "and he shall purify it and sanctify it" (Vayikra 16:19). This is not the case with the vessels of the Azarah, the Mizbe'ach HaOlah and the Kiyor and its stand, which we do not find referred to as "pure." This is because the Heichal is analogous to the world of the spheres, whose *golem* is refined, clear, and clean, and therefore the vessels within it are also called "pure."

This is also the distinction regarding the *golem* from which the vessels were made: the vessels of the Ohel Moed—the table, the menorah, and the altar—were vessels made, or at the very least plated, with pure gold, whereas in the Azarah, many of the vessels were (not only plated with, but actually made of) copper. The distinction between them is that gold, even though it is physical *golem*, our Sages have said (Bereishis Rabbah 16:2) that the world was not worthy to use it.

88

Regarding gold: why was it created? For the Beis Hamikdash. Furthermore, the vessels of the Heichal in the Beis Hamikdash were also gold. However, in the Ulam, which is the intermediary between the Heichal and the Azarah, there was a table of silver or marble (see Menachos 99b, Tosafos s.v. "Echad").

We ascend even higher than this to the Kodesh Hakodashim, where there was nothing at all related to eating; rather, only the Ketores and the sprinkling of the blood of the bull and the goat on Yom Kippur—which are burned (Zevachim 47b)—representing form alone.

Man, too, through whom the entire world will reach its perfection, is divided into these three categories. This applies both to the totality of Bnei Yisrael—where the majority of the people, the Yisraelim, are occupied with "gathering your grain," etc., and conduct themselves according to the ways of the world (Berachos 35b)—and to the Kohanim, who were set apart for the service of Hashem, with the Leviim being secondary to them, as it is stated (Bamidbar...

Regarding gold: why was it created? For the Beis Hamikdash. This means that the raw material of gold is refined, clear, and pure (unlike copper). Therefore, the Ohel Moed, which corresponds to the world of the spheres (galgalim) whose raw material is refined, clear, and pure, had vessels of gold. And the vessels of the Heichal in the Beis Hamikdash were also gold. However, in the Ulam—which is the intermediary between the Heichal and the Azarah—there was also a table of silver or marble (see Menachos 99b, Tosafos s.v. "Echad").

3) We ascend even higher than this (than the Ohel Moed and the Heichal) to the Kodesh Hakodashim, where there was nothing at all related to eating; rather, only the Ketores and the sprinkling of the blood of the bull and the goat on Yom Kippur—which are burned (Zevachim 47b)—representing form alone, similar to the world of the angels, which possesses no raw material (golem), only form.

55. Man, too—when he fulfills the purpose of his creation to serve his Creator through his avodah in the world according to the Torah and the ways of the mitzvos, for through him the entire world will reach its perfection (as mentioned above in section 48)—is divided into these three categories (the three divisions of the Mishkan and the Mikdash, which correspond to the three divisions in the world at large). This applies both to the totality of Bnei Yisrael and to each individual specifically (as will be explained in section 56).

To preface the three divisions of the totality of Yisrael: there are Yisraelim, Kohanim (including the Leviim), and the Kohen Gadol:

a) The majority of the people—the Yisraelim—whose occupation is with the affairs of the world, "gathering your grain," etc., and "conduct themselves according to the ways of the world" (Berachos 35b).

b) The Kohanim were set apart from them for the service of Hashem—and the Leviim, who were also set apart from the rest of Yisrael, do not constitute a separate category, but are included with the Kohanim, as they are secondary to them, as it is stated...

## 89

18) "And they shall join themselves unto you, and minister unto you..." and "And they shall be joined unto you..." — Even though Yisroel as a whole are called holy, as it is written, "Yisroel is holy unto Hashem" (Yirmiyahu 2:3), regarding the Kohanim in relation to Yisroel it is stated (Vayikra 21:6-8), "And they shall be holy..." and "You shall sanctify him..." And among the Kohanim, there is the Kohen Gadol, who is the head of all the Kohanim. His function is (Hilchos Klei HaMikdash 5:7) that "a house shall be prepared for him in the Mikdash... and his glory and honor is that he sits in the Mikdash all day... and his house shall be in Yerushalayim, and he shall not depart from there." And in Divrei Hayamim I (23:13) it is stated, "And Aharon was separated, that he should sanctify him as Kodesh Kodoshim, he and his sons." It can be said that "to sanctify him" corresponds to the Kohanim in general—"his sons"—and "Kodesh Kodoshim" corresponds to the Kohen Gadol—Aharon.

And this is also the correspondence of the divisions in the Mishkan and in man: In the courtyard of the Ohel Moed and the Azarah, Yisroel also enter; in the Ohel Moed and the Heichal, only Kohanim enter; and in the Kodesh Kodoshim, only the Kohen Gadol enters.

 In the Beis HaMikdash, Yisroel are forbidden from entering even between the Ulam and the Mizbe'ach, even though it is in the Azarah, and certainly in the Ulam.

## 90

Kelim 1:8; see the commentary of the Rosh there. One cannot say that there is a distinction between the space between the Ulam and the Mizbe'ach, for the fact that a Yisrael may not enter there is not due to its superior level of sanctity relative to the Ezras Kohanim; rather, it is because a Yisrael does not enter the Ezras Kohanim either, except when they have a need for semichah, shechitah, or tenufah. And since the primary place for semichah, shechitah, and so on, is not between the Ulam and the Mizbe'ach—even though shechitah is valid even in the Heichal (Zevachim 63a)—therefore a Yisrael does not enter there. This is similar to the upper chamber of the Kodesh HaKodashim mentioned in the Tosefta of Kelim there, cited in the commentary of the Rash, that this is not a "superiority" [of sanctity], for they do not enter only because they have no need to. But this is incorrect, for a Yisrael does not enter between the Ulam and the Mizbe'ach even to circle the Mizbe'ach with the aravah (Rashi and Tosafos, Sukkah 43b).

In the Azarah, and certainly, Yisrael are forbidden to enter the Ulam (Kelim, Chapter 1, Mishnah 8; see the commentary of the Rosh there). Seemingly, based on what was said—that the sanctity of the courtyard of the Ohel Moed and the Azarah corresponds to the level of sanctity of Yisrael—the prohibition for a Yisrael to enter the Beis HaMikdash should only begin from the Heichal and inward.

And one cannot say that there is a distinction between the space between the Ulam and the Mizbe'ach, for the fact that a Yisrael does not enter there is not due to the superiority of the place between the Ulam and the Mizbe'ach—in terms of the sanctity of Yisrael—relative to the Ezras Kohanim; rather, it is because a Yisrael does not enter the Ezras Kohanim either, "except at the time of their need, for semichah, shechitah, and tenufah."
