And since the primary place for semichah, shechitah, and so on, is not between the Ulam and the Mizbe'ach—even though shechitah is valid even in the Heichal, as mentioned above from Zevachim (63a)—this does not constitute a "need" of the Yisrael, and therefore a Yisrael does not enter there.

This is similar to the upper chamber of the Kodesh HaKodashim mentioned in the Tosefta of Kelim there (cited in the commentary of the Rash)—regarding the words of Abba Shaul that "the upper chamber of the Kodesh HaKodashim is more stringent than the Kodesh HaKodashim itself, for in the Kodesh HaKodashim the Kohen Gadol enters every year for the service of Yom Kippur four times a day... whereas to the upper chamber of the Kodesh HaKodashim they only enter once every seven years to see what it needs"—"this is not a superiority," since the fact that they do not enter the upper chamber of the Kodesh HaKodashim is not due to the superiority of its sanctity, but because they had no need to enter. Similarly in our case, the fact that a Yisrael does not enter between the Ulam and the Mizbe'ach is not due to the superiority of its sanctity, but because he has no need to enter there.

But this is not so, for a Yisrael does not enter between the Ulam and the Mizbe'ach even to circle the Mizbe'ach with the aravah (Rashi and Tosafos, Sukkah 43b), even though he has a need for this. We must therefore conclude that this is due to the superior level of sanctity.

## 91

The distinction mentioned above applies also to time: the six days of the week, which are the time for "gathering your grain," when one is occupied with worldly matters.

— This proof can be refuted. It can be said that the *Halacha L'Moshe MiSinai* regarding the *aravah* applies only to *kohanim* (see *Tosafos*, s.v. *Amar Lei*, *Sukkah* 44a).

— Alternatively, it can be said that the levels established by the Sages (the various opinions on this in *Yoma* 44b; *Sukkah* ibid.; *Sefer HaMitzvos*, Negative Commandment 69; *Yad*, *Biyas Mikdash* 1:15, and commentaries).

***

The distinction mentioned above regarding the *Mishkan* and the person applies also to time: the six days of the week, which are the time for "gathering your grain," when one is occupied with the affairs of the world.

Regarding the prohibition for a *Yisrael* to enter the area between the *Ulam* and the *Mizbe'ach*, and certainly the *Ulam* itself—even though it is within the *Azarah*—it can be said that the levels of sanctity of the *Azarah* enumerated by the Sages (in the *Mishnah* of *Keilim*), "The *Azaras Kohanim* is more sacred than it... the area between the *Ulam* and the *Mizbe'ach* is more sacred than it," are only *d'Rabbanan*. According to the Torah, it is permitted for a *Yisrael* to enter the entire *Azarah*, including the *Ulam* (the various opinions on this—see *Yoma* 44b; *Sukkah* ibid.; *Sefer HaMitzvos* by the Rambam, Negative Commandment 69; *Yad*, Laws of *Biyas Mikdash*, Chapter 1, Law 15, and commentaries).

## 92

The warning, "Take heed to yourselves, lest your heart be deceived, and you turn aside, etc." Shabbos, "And you shall keep the Shabbos, for it is holy," and then the thirty-nine forbidden labors. *Borer* (selecting) is forbidden. And even the *golem* (the raw material/physicality) is refined*, to the point that they said, "A Shabbos dish has a fragrant scent" (Shabbos 119a), and "The radiance of a person's countenance is not the same" (Bereishis Rabbah 11:2). Yet, despite this, it is a mitzvah to delight in it with eating and drinking [and from the Torah, speech is permitted on Shabbos**]. And the explanation according to the *derush* regarding Yom Kippur requires further study (see *maamar* on Yom Kippur).

----------
*) "Spread your Shabbos" is not said. *Aliyas Klippas Nogah*.
**) Shabbos 150a. To be researched in the *kuntres*.

---

This lowly world, whose *golem* is not refined and pure, therefore requires the warning, "Take heed to yourselves, lest your heart be deceived, and you turn aside, etc.".

b) Shabbos – regarding which it is said, "And you shall keep the Shabbos, for it is holy," and then the thirty-nine labors of weekday activity are forbidden, and even *borer* is forbidden. This means that one is in a state and condition of holiness that is above any connection to weekday activity (even not in the manner of *borer*), which is the concept of a "form" that is above "golem." Furthermore, even the *golem* that exists on Shabbos is refined*—to the point that Chazal said, "A Shabbos dish has a fragrant scent" (Shabbos 119a), and "The radiance of a person's countenance" on weekdays is not the same as his radiance on Shabbos (Bereishis Rabbah 11:2). However, nonetheless, there is also a *golem* on Shabbos (refined and pure, similar to the *golem* of the world of the spheres), which is why it is a mitzvah to delight in it with eating and drinking [and this is also why, from the Torah, only an act whose *golem* is not refined and pure is forbidden on Shabbos, whereas speech, whose *golem* is refined and pure (as mentioned above in section 53), is permitted on Shabbos**]. And the explanation according to the *derush* regarding Yom Kippur, whose essence is *machshava* (thought), requires further study—why is speech permitted from the Torah even on Yom Kippur?

----------
*) And this is the meaning of "**Spread your Shabbos is not said**", because on Shabbos, the **elevation of *Klippas Nogah*** occurs.
**) **Shabbos 150a**. **To be researched in the *kuntres***.

## 93

This is not the case on Yom Kippur, when we are like the angels (Shulchan Aruch Orach Chayim, end of section 610, and references cited there). There is no eating or drinking, and wearing leather shoes is forbidden, for it must be "remove your shoes," as it is a "holy day to Hashem" (Shabbos 119a).

This is the concept of the entry of the Kohen Gadol* into the Holy of Holies, which occurs on Yom Kippur. And that which is eaten from the matters performed in the Heichal specifically—the Lechem HaPanim—its mitzvah is specifically on Shabbos, both its arrangement and its removal (Menachos 99b and there), and it is eaten only by the Kohanim (Menachos 74b).

----------
*) See Likutei Torah, discourses for Yom Kippur and the end of Parshas Pinchas.

## 58.

And furthermore, this is true for every individual as well. For the majority of the day, one person turns to his vine and another turns to his fig tree, becoming so invested in these pursuits that he finds himself outside the *Azarah*, since he is occupied with mundane matters outside the *Beis Hamikdash*. But when he remembers his role in the world and takes stock of his soul—realizing that he is a *shaliach* of the *Makom* in this world—he begins to enter from the outside into his "home," into the *Mishkan* and *Mikdash* within his own soul.

And the order of progression when entering from the outside into the *Mishkan* and *Mikdash* is: the *Azarah*—the courtyard of the *Ohel Moed*—and then the *Heichal*—the *Ohel Moed*, and so on, as follows.

## 59.

First and foremost is the entry from the outside into the *Azarah*—and in the *Mishkan*, the courtyard of the *Ohel Moed*—where the *Kiyor* (laver) and the *Mizbach HaOlah* (altar of burnt offerings) are located. Their significance in a person’s *avodah* is as follows :

The *Kiyor*, from which the *Kohanim* would wash their hands and feet upon entering the *Azarah*, signifies that before anything else, a person entering the *Azarah* must wash his hands and feet of whatever adhered to them during his business in this world while he was outside the *Azarah*.

Only then can he approach the *avodah* of the *Mizbach HaOlah*, which alludes to *tefillah*, which is the *avodah* of the heart (*Taanis* 2a).

## 95

The Sages established the *tefillos* to correspond to the *temidin* (Berachos 26b), which are specifically the *olah* offering—the "continual *olah* that was made at Har Sinai"—because the heart must be entirely for Hashem, and one’s worldly affairs must be conducted without the boiling of the blood or emotional heat. As is known, the interpretation of the Sages' statement, "If you eat the labor of your hands, you are fortunate" (Avos 4:1), is that it refers specifically to your *hands*. The performance of permitted matters must be done "without the heart"—and this is the significance of the "made at Har Sinai," for it is called Sinai because "hatred descended upon the nations of the world upon it" (Shabbos 89a), meaning a hatred for all material matters. And through the heart, which is the king...

[The service of the heart is prayer (Taanis 2a), and the Sages established the *tefillos* to correspond to the *temidin* (Berachos 26b), which are specifically the *olah* offering —the "continual *olah*," and it adds "that was made at Har Sinai," as follows—which is offered entirely to Hashem. For the heart (the service of the heart is prayer) must be entirely for Hashem.

The concept here is that the heat and *hispaalus* of the heart must be entirely for Hashem, whereas one’s *affairs* in worldly matters should be without the boiling of the blood or emotional heat. As is known, the interpretation of the Sages' statement, "If you eat the labor of your hands, you are fortunate and it is good for you; you are fortunate in this world and it is good for you in the World to Come" (Avos 4:1), is "the labor of your *hands*" specifically—meaning that only the hands are weary, or the feet and other external limbs, "without the heart, etc." For the heart must be entirely for Hashem, and the performance of all *permitted matters* (in addition to one’s business affairs) must be done "without the heart"*.

[And this is the "continual *olah* that was made at Har Sinai"—that in order to be an "*olah*" ("continual *olah*") that is entirely for Hashem, there must be the remembrance of "that was made at Har Sinai," for it is called Sinai because "hatred descended upon the nations of the world upon it" (Shabbos 89a), meaning a hatred for all material matters.]

* See **Torah Or**, *maamar* **Lo Tihyeh Meshakelah**, and *maamar* **U'Mordechai Yatza**.

## Tzav

Through the heart, which is the king over the limbs, it becomes an *olah* (burnt offering) to Hashem (through the *avodah* of the heart—*tefillah*, which corresponds to the *temidin*, the daily *olah* offerings). Consequently, the person becomes "entirely as it should be," just like an *olah* offering that is entirely consumed for Hashem. As our Sages of blessed memory said regarding the trait of a "good heart," that it includes all good traits (*Avos* 2:10). From this, it is understood that the entirety of a person’s existence depends on the heart; therefore, when the heart is entirely for Hashem, the entire person becomes for Hashem.

And after he has washed his hands and feet from what adhered to them during his involvement in this world (*kiyor*), and has achieved within himself that his heart is entirely for Hashem (*mizbach ha'olah*) while he is in the *azarah* (courtyard)—then, "from the *azarah* he enters the *heichal*". He reaches a state where it is no longer possible for there to be any *pesoles* (dross) or loss, for in every matter...

## 107

Entering the Heichal—which corresponds to the world of the spheres, where there is no waste or loss, and whose substance is refined and pure—signifies that a person reaches a state where waste and loss are no longer applicable. For in everything he engages, even in physical matters, it is solely for a Divine intent (without the admixture of any other intent, thus negating waste), which is eternal (negating loss). In all his deeds, he is like a Kohen serving Hashem.

This is similar to what is written in the Yad, Hilchos Deos (3:2): "A person must direct his heart and all his deeds solely to know Hashem, blessed is He, and his sitting, rising, and speech should all be directed toward this matter." And it concludes there (3:3): "It turns out that one who walks in this path is serving Hashem constantly, all his days, even when he is engaged in business and even when he is engaged in marital relations, because his thought in all of these is to obtain his needs so that his body may be whole to serve Hashem. Even when he sleeps, if he sleeps with the intent that his mind may be at rest and his body may rest so that he does not become ill and unable to serve Hashem due to sickness, his sleep is found to be a service to the Omnipresent, blessed is He."

61. More specifically, regarding the details of the vessels in the Heichal and the service performed with them:

In the Heichal, the Menorah was in the south, the Shulchan was in the north, and the inner Mizbe'ach was in the middle, but pulled toward the outside.

Since the inner Mizbe'ach is pulled toward the outside, it turns out that one encounters it first upon entering the Heichal, as mentioned above. For the beginning of one's entry into this level—the level of the Heichal, where all his deeds are for a Divine intent like a Kohen serving Hashem—is the inner Mizbe'ach.

The explanation of this matter:

The uniqueness of the inner Mizbe'ach is that it is called "the altar upon which incense is burned" (Shemos 30:1), for one does not offer incense upon the outer Mizbe'ach (Menachos 50a)—except for the Nesi'im, who offered incense upon the outer altar, the Mizbe'ach HaOlah, because it was a temporary instruction, as is stated there in Menachos.

## 98

There was no *ma'arachah* (arrangement of wood) for the inner *mizbe'ach*; rather, they would take coals from the second *ma'arachah* of *ketores* that was arranged upon the outer *mizbe'ach* (*Tamid* 5:4; *Yoma* 45a).

It can be said that the allusion in this is that even in the level of the inner *mizbe'ach*—which is above the category of *golem hoveh v'nifsad* (matter that exists and decays) of wood (which is why it did not have a *ma'arachah*)—there are coals that come from the wood of the *ma'arachah* of the outer *mizbe'ach* in the *azarah*, which is *golem hoveh v'nifsad* (though it is burned upon the *mizbe'ach*)—in the manner and pattern of the contact with the inner *mizbe'ach* at the beginning of its arrival from the *azarah*, as will be explained later.

62. To preface the virtue of the *ketores* that is offered upon the inner *mizbe'ach*: *Korbanos* differ from *ketores*, in that *korbanos* are called "bread for My fire" and also "a pleasing aroma"—both food (bread) and aroma—whereas *ketores* is aroma alone, as it is stated, "They shall put *ketores* in Your nostrils" (*Devarim* 33:10), which refers to the *ketores*, as is found in *Yoma* (26a). See also *Moreh Nevuchim* (Part 3, Chapter 45) regarding the reason for the *ketores*.

If so, the distinction between them is similar to the distinction between aroma and eating: aroma is something that the soul enjoys, while the body does not enjoy it (*Berachos* 43b), whereas with eating, the body also enjoys it.

## 99

For the neshamah is eternal, and it is more beloved than all the korbanos (Bamidbar Rabbah, ch. 18). [And furthermore, the gematria of *ketores* is 613, by substituting the *kuf* with a *dalet* according to the *atbash* cipher (Bamidbar Rabbah, ch. 13, regarding the korban of Nachshon).] The ketores symbolizes that the person is at a level where the neshamah alone is the ruler, and he connects to Hashem without any obstruction—for *ketores* is the Aramaic translation of *v’tikshar* (and you shall bind), just as Chazal expound regarding Keturah, that she "bound" (kashrah) her opening, and she had no connection to anyone but Avraham (Rashi on Bereishis 25:1, from Bereishis Rabbah).

This is also why the ketores specifically (and not the korbanos) halts the plague (Shabbos 89a), because the ketores is a fragrance that the *neshamah* derives pleasure from, and the *neshamah is eternal*—which emphasizes the connection of the ketores specifically to the neshamah. Therefore, the greatness of the ketores is such that *it is more beloved than all the korbanos* (Bamidbar Rabbah, ch. 18, 8)—because it is merely a fragrance (without consumption, as with korbanos) which pertains to the neshamah.

[And furthermore: The gematria of *ketores* is 613, by substituting the *kuf* with a *dalet* according to the *atbash* cipher (Bamidbar Rabbah, ch. 13, regarding the korban of *Nachshon*)—which emphasizes the spiritual quality of the ketores, in which the entirety of the concept of the mitzvos is hinted.]

Since the essence of the ketores is a fragrance that the neshamah *alone* (without the involvement of the body) derives pleasure from, it follows that *the ketores symbolizes that the person* is at a level where the neshamah alone (without the involvement of the body) *is the ruler* over him, and then he *connects to Hashem without any obstruction*—as is hinted in the word "ketores," which is related to the word *kesher* (bond), as we find that "*ketores*" is the *Aramaic translation* of "*v’tikshar*", and as per the exposition of Chazal regarding Keturah, that she bound her opening and had no connection to anyone but Avraham (Rashi on Bereishis 25:1—from Bereishis Rabbah). The meaning of this in avodah is that the person connects with the Ribono shel Olam to be attached to Him alone, and this connection is without any hindrance, since the body occupies no space at all, and only the neshamah is the ruler over him.

## 100

However, in order to reach the avodah of the Heichal, where the *golem* is refined as mentioned above, the very beginning of the avodah must also be in a more elevated manner. When one contemplates how he was previously immersed in the vanities of this world, etc., and feels great distress over this, he will be able to stand firm against any obstacle from the body, etc. And with the assistance of his prior involvement in Torah and mitzvos—for Torah is "strength and wisdom" (Torah Or, *B'Chodesh HaShlishi*), a strength for the soul that weakens the power of the body (Sanhedrin 26b and Bava Metzia 84a)—and also through the mitzvos, for "when one rises, the other falls" (Pesachim 42b) and "one mitzvah leads to another" (Avos 4:2), and see also Sotah (21a)—through all this, he arrives at the level of the avodah of the Ketores.

From this it is understood that the level of the Ketores, where the soul alone rules and connects to Hashem without hindrance, is a *more elevated* level than the level of the Heichal. Nevertheless, in order to reach the avodah of the Heichal, where the *golem* is refined as mentioned above—meaning that his physical matters are for the sake of a Divine intent, etc.—the very beginning of this avodah must also be in a more elevated manner. This is why, immediately upon entering the avodah of the Heichal, he first encounters the Inner Altar, where the avodah of the Ketores is performed.

63. The explanation for this is: On the face of it, how can the initial entry into the avodah of the Heichal be in such an elevated manner—that when he contemplates how he was initially immersed in the vanities of this world, etc., and feels great distress over this, he will then be able to stand firm against any obstacle from the body, etc.?

With the assistance of his prior involvement in Torah and mitzvos—for Torah is "strength and wisdom" (Torah Or, *Maamar B'Chodesh HaShlishi* and in the Additions), a strength for the soul that weakens the power of the body (Sanhedrin 26b and Bava Metzia 84a), and also through the mitzvos, strength is added to the soul and the power of the body is weakened, for "when one rises, the other falls" (Pesachim 42b), and "one mitzvah leads to another" (Avos 4:2), and see also Sotah (21a) that "a mitzvah, at the time one is engaged in it, protects and saves" (protects from suffering and saves from the Yetzer Hara so that it does not cause him to sin)—through all of this (the strength of standing against any obstacle from the body, and the weakening of the power of the body, etc.), he arrives at this.

## 101

[He] is no longer part of the "transient physical matter" [golem hoveh v'nifsad]—like the wood and limbs from which the coals are made on the outer Mizbei'ach [Mizbei'ach HaOlah]—and the coals through which one offers the Ketores come from the outer Mizbei'ach*—and see Yad, Hilchos Temidin U'Musafin, Chapter 3, Halacha 5, that according to the Rambam, it specifically requires [coals from] edible matter, while the Ra'avad disagrees.

----------
*) Ta'arav.

[He] reaches the level of the avodah of the Ketores—where the neshamah alone governs him and connects to Hashem without hindrance—immediately upon his entry into the avodah of the Heichal.

**104.** And this is why there is no arrangement [of wood] on the inner Mizbei'ach [for, when one is at the level of the Heichal, he no longer belongs to the "transient physical matter" like the wood and limbs from which the coals are made on the Mizbei'ach HaOlah] in the Azarah, and the coals through which one offers the Ketores come from the Mizbei'ach HaOlah*:

In the offering of the Ketores on the inner Mizbei'ach (which does not belong to the transient physical matter) via coals that come from the Mizbei'ach HaOlah *in the Azarah*, where there is transient physical matter—which is even lower than the level of the "matter" in the Heichal, which is refined and pure—it is hinted that the encounter at the inner Mizbei'ach, where the most exalted avodah of the Ketores takes place—where the neshamah alone governs, etc.—is immediately *upon entering* the Heichal, just as they have *come from the Azarah*, which contains transient physical matter.

However, for this, one must first establish the strength of standing against any hindrance from the body and the breaking of the body's power (as mentioned above, that through this one reaches the level of the avodah of the Ketores), which is the concept of the "coals" specifically, which are created through the *burning of the physical matter*.

—And see Yad, Hilchos Temidin U'Musafin, Chapter 3, Halacha 5, that according to the opinion of the Rambam, we require coals...

----------
*) See the explanation of this in the Chassidic discourses—Hemshech **Ta'arav**.

## 100

And only after encountering the inner altar did he encounter the Menorah and the Table, which hint at the two types of man’s avodah: the spiritual—Torah and tefillah—which is the Menorah, and the material matters—the Table. This is as our Sages of blessed memory hinted: "One who wishes to become wise should turn south, and one who wishes to become wealthy should turn north; your mnemonic is: the Menorah [in the south] and the Table [in the north]" (Bava Basra 25b). And as will be explained further, this is similar to the prohibition against eating before tefillah (Berachos 10b) or attending to one's affairs (ibid. 14a). Furthermore, even regarding Torah study, one strives to daven first (Berachos 5b; see Rashi and Tosafos there), and in any event, the blessing over the Torah must precede it. It is the same regarding Shabbos.

...specifically "consumed coals," which indicates the complete nullification of the *golem*. The Raavad disagrees and holds that we do not specifically require consumed coals; rather, the action of weakening the *golem* is sufficient.

**65.** Let us return to our subject: that upon entering the Heichal, he encountered the inner altar first, and only after encountering the inner altar did he encounter the Menorah and the Table, which hint at the two types of man’s avodah:

The spiritual avodah—Torah and tefillah—is hinted at by the Menorah, and the avodah regarding material matters—eating and other personal affairs—is hinted at by the Table. As our Sages of blessed memory hinted: "One who wishes to become wise should turn south, and one who wishes to become wealthy should turn north; your mnemonic is: the Menorah [in the south] and the Table [in the north]" (Bava Basra 25b). This means that the Menorah pertains to spiritual matters (wisdom), and the Table pertains to material matters (wealth), as will be explained.

And one may add to this (in a slightly different manner), that the encounter with the inner altar first and only afterwards with the Menorah and Table is similar to the prohibition against eating before tefillah (Berachos 10b) or attending to one's affairs (ibid. 14a). And this applies not only regarding eating and attending to one's affairs, but also regarding Torah study, where one strives to daven first (Berachos 5b; see Rashi and Tosafos there). In any event, the blessing over the Torah (which is akin to tefillah) must necessarily precede Torah study, for it is forbidden to study before the blessing over the Torah. The parallel in our case is that the inner altar...

## 103

(Ketores, which corresponds to the hiskashrus of tefillah) precedes the Shulchan (which alludes to physical matters, such as eating and attending to one’s affairs), and it also precedes the Menorah (which alludes to spiritual matters, such as Talmud Torah).

It should be noted that the prohibition against eating before tefillah (which corresponds to the priority of the inner altar over the Shulchan) applies not only on weekdays, when the *golem* (the act of eating, etc.) is not as refined and pure, but it also applies on Shabbos—which corresponds to the *Heichal*. Even though on Shabbos the *golem* is refined and pure, and consequently the eating is more elevated (as mentioned above in section 59), one must nevertheless first prioritize tefillah—the concept of the inner altar, which one encounters first.

The point of this is that after the general hiskashrus to Hashem is strengthened—alluded to by encountering the inner altar upon which the Ketores is offered [which corresponds to the *chinuch* at the beginning of avodah, similar to "train a child", "like the analogy of a child whom one trains... through some addition, such as some form of closeness or a gift... because all beginnings are difficult"]—only then does one begin the specific details of avodah, which are the Menorah and the Shulchan.

106. Now, all of this (the entry from the outside into the Azarah, and from the Azarah into the Heichal) is analogous to the weekdays and Shabbos. On weekdays, one spends most of the day outside the Azarah, and at special times (when one remembers that he is an emissary of the Makom, etc.), one begins to enter from the outside into the Azarah, until one enters the Heichal, where even the *golem* of that which is eaten (the Lechem HaPanim upon the Shulchan) is refined and pure; this concept is primarily manifest on Shabbos (as mentioned above in sections 56-58).

However, there are times of deeper internal hisorerus—when a spirit from on high is poured upon him —where a person feels himself completely divested of his physicality. That is to say, it is not merely that his physicality becomes refined and pure (as on Shabbos, when he is at the level of the Heichal), but that he feels himself completely divested of his physicality.

And a person is especially prone to reaching this state during the Aseres Yemei Teshuvah, regarding which our Sages of blessed memory expounded, "Seek Hashem when He is to be found..." and "when He is near" (Rashi).

## 104

Rabbah bar Avuha says: These are the ten days between Rosh Hashanah and Yom Kippur (Rosh Hashanah 18a). More specifically, this applies to Yom Kippur, when one is distanced from sin. As our Sages of blessed memory said regarding the Nehardeans who sinned on holy Yom Kippur: "What did the Holy One, blessed be He, say? He said to them: 'Sin crouches at the entrance'" (the *yetzer hara* causes him to sin against his will — Rashi), Yoma 19b. At that time, *Bnei Yisrael* are like the angels, as mentioned above. This is the allusion to the world of the angels, the *Kodesh HaKodashim*, the *Kohen Gadol*, and Yom Kippur.

In the *Kodesh HaKodashim* was the Ark, in which the Tablets were placed*, and the Ark is called the "Ark of the Covenant of Hashem," because the Tablets that are in the Ark—the matter of Torah—belong to the level of *Kodesh HaKodashim*.

---
\*) Regarding what was in the Ark besides the Tablets, see *Tosafot* s.v. *Kol* (Eruvin 63b) and the references there.

## 105

The ability of a person to reach a great level of hiskashrus, as mentioned above, stems from the fact that his neshamah is a "literal part of Elokus from above" and is connected to its source. Regarding this, it is stated (*Zohar Chadash*, Vol. 3, 73a): "Three bonds are bound one to the other," meaning that Yisrael is bound to the Torah, and through this, to the Ribono shel Olam. It is also stated regarding the verse "And this was the custom in Yisrael" that the word *Yisrael* is an acronym for "There are six hundred thousand letters to the Torah," corresponding to the six hundred thousand souls of Bnei Yisrael. Each and every one of them has a letter in the Torah which is his chiyus [and this is also alluded to in the words of our Sages (Berachos 61b): "The place of our chiyus... we are occupied with Torah... for it is your life"].

The fact that a person can reach a great level of hiskashrus with the Ribono shel Olam—in a manner where he is completely abstracted from his physicality, like a form without matter, which is the level of Kodesh HaKodashim as mentioned above—is because his neshamah is a "literal part of Elokus from above" and is connected to its source.

Regarding this, it is stated (*Zohar Chadash*, Vol. 3, 73a): "Three bonds are bound one to the other," meaning that Yisrael is bound to the Torah, and through this, they are bound to the Ribono shel Olam. It is also stated regarding the verse "And this was the custom in Yisrael"* that the word *Yisrael* is an acronym for "There are six hundred thousand letters to the Torah," corresponding to the six hundred thousand souls of Bnei Yisrael. Each and every one of the six hundred thousand souls of Bnei Yisrael has a letter in the Torah which is his chiyus** [and this is also alluded to in the words of our Sages (Berachos 61b): "The place of our chiyus... we are occupied with Torah... for it is your life"].

Thus, it is found that the perfection of hiskashrus with the Ribono shel Olam, which is the level of Kodesh HaKodashim, is achieved...

----------
*) Search in *Zohar Chadash*, *Rus*. 
**) *Hakdamas Likutei Torah*, 63.

## Kav

Such a state is transcendent of physicality, similar to mesiras nefesh which is above intellect and entirely beyond a keli; it is a "form without matter," where even refined and pure matter is absent. We find another distinction between the Kodesh HaKodashim and the Heichal: the Heichal cannot exist without kelim, whereas in the Second Beis Hamikdash, the Aron was hidden (Yoma 52b and 53b), which proves that even without a keli, all the matters associated with it are present.

[Note: In the First Beis Hamikdash, the Aron was present. And we find that at its dedication, they ate—a keli—on Yom Kippur (Moed Katan 9a). And in the future, when the Aron returns, all the festivals will be nullified; seemingly, Yom Kippur is included in this, yet the days of Purim will not pass. See Torah Or, Megillas Esther, and the additions.]

Through the Torah—the "Luchos HaBris" that were in the "Aron Bris Hashem"—there is a connection in the manner of a "bris," which is above intellect.

208. And to hint at the fact that such avodah (a great connection at the level of the Kodesh HaKodashim) is transcendent of physicality, similar to mesiras nefesh which is above intellect and entirely beyond a keli—which is the concept of a "form without matter," where even refined and pure matter is absent—we find another distinction between the Kodesh HaKodashim and the Heichal:

In addition to what was stated above (section 207) that in the Heichal there is something that is eaten, whose matter is refined and pure (the Lechem HaPanim), whereas in the Kodesh HaKodashim there was nothing at all related to eating, which is the concept of a "form without matter"—we find another distinction between them: the Heichal cannot exist without kelim (the inner Mizbeiach, the Menorah, and the Shulchan), whereas in the Kodesh HaKodashim, in the Second Beis Hamikdash, the Aron was hidden (Yoma 52b and 53b), which proves that even without a keli (without the Aron), all the matters associated with it are present.

In this is hinted the superiority of the Kodesh HaKodashim—that not only is there no refined and pure matter of the world of the spheres, but furthermore, it is also entirely above the concept of a "keli" (the limitation of holiness), as it is in a state of bli gevul. Its meaning in avodah is the mesiras nefesh that is above the limitation of the intellect of holiness, the connection with the Ribono shel Olam that comes from the essence of the neshamah.

[Note: In the First Beis Hamikdash, the Aron was present, and we find that at the dedication of the Beis Hamikdash, they ate—eating which is (even if not at the level of refined and pure matter as on Shabbos, but only) at the level of a "keli"—on Yom Kippur (Moed Katan 9a). And likewise in the future, when the Aron returns—since then all the festivals will be nullified...]

## 107

The Keruvim of Shlomo and the Keruvim of Moshe [Torah Or, Terumah; Likutei Torah, Ki Al Kol Kavod]. The Kapores is [nullified], and seemingly Yom Kippur is also included in this, and only "the days of Purim will not cease" (see Torah Or, Megillas Esther and the additions), so there will be the matter of eating—the aspect of a *keli*—on Yom Kippur.

It follows that the presence of the Aron in the Kodesh HaKodashim (in the First Beis Hamikdash and in the future) indicates that there is still a connection to the matter of a "keli" (the eating of Yom Kippur). And in this regard, there is a superiority in the Second Beis Hamikdash, where the Aron was hidden—for this emphasizes that the essence of the Kodesh HaKodashim itself (which can exist even without the Aron) is entirely above a "keli," as mentioned above.

**69.** And it is appropriate to add and explain also the matter of the Kapores and the Keruvim*, and the distinction between the **Keruvim of Shlomo** and the **Keruvim of Moshe**—for regarding Moshe it is stated, "And you shall make two Keruvim of gold..."

----------
*) Regarding the **Keruvim**—see also **Likutei Torah** to **Shir HaShirim 29a, b, c**.

## 108

Chuppah. There is a Chuppah over ZON, where the Tiferes of Imma becomes a Keter for ZON—a ratzon that is below the level of intellect, which is derived from hisbonenus. It can be said that this is why the Keruvim of Moshe were made of gold—gold represents Binah, hisbonenus—and were hammered out of the Kapores. However, the Keruvim of Shlomo are a Chuppah over Abba and Imma, a ratzon that is above intellect, and were therefore made specifically of olive wood, for oil comes from the source of wisdom.

The matter is as follows: The Kapores, which is the level of makif, is the level of Chuppah. There is a Chuppah over ZON, which is the level of Tiferes of Imma that becomes a Keter for ZON. In the avodah of man, its concept is a ratzon that is below the level of intellect, which is derived from hisbonenus—this is the concept of the Kapores, which is a Chuppah over ZON, which are the Keruvim.

It can be said that this is why the Keruvim of Moshe, which are the level of ZON, were made of gold; for gold is the level of Binah, intellectual hisbonenus, from which the middos—ZON (the Keruvim)—are derived. They were made "hammered out of the Kapores," because ZON (the Keruvim) are drawn from the level of Tiferes of Imma (the Kapores), which becomes a Keter for ZON.

However, the Keruvim of Shlomo are on a higher level (not only higher than the Keruvim of Moshe, but also higher than the Kapores, which is a Chuppah over ZON)—they are a Chuppah over Abba and Imma, the concept of which is a ratzon that is above intellect. Therefore, the Keruvim of Shlomo were (not of gold, the level of Binah, but) made specifically of olive wood, for...

## 109

They were not hammered from the kapores, which is Binah; on the contrary, they overshadowed the Aron and also the Keruvim of Moshe, as is understood (*Divrei HaYamim II 5*), for the chupah of Abba and Imma is the source for the chupah of Ze'er Anpin and Nukvah. Nevertheless, they too were overlaid with gold—the ratzon that is above seichel clothes itself in the ratzon that is below seichel (*Torah Or, Vayakhel*), and this is why the gold is a garment upon the *atzei shemen*. This did not exist in the Mishkan, but only in the Beis HaMikdash, for in the desert they were in a state of *achor b'achor*, which is only *iskafya*.

*Shemen* indicates the *mocha stima'ah* —the ratzon that is above seichel. "They were not hammered from the kapores," which is nothing other than the aspect of Tiferes of Imma, [Binah].

"On the contrary": The Keruvim of Shlomo were "overshadowing the Aron and also the Keruvim of Moshe, as is understood" from what is written (*Divrei HaYamim II 5:7-8*): "The Kohanim brought the Aron of the Covenant of Hashem... under the wings of the Keruvim (these are the ones Shlomo made, which stood on the ground). And the Keruvim were spreading wings over the place of the Aron (in the place where the Aron stands, therefore), and the Keruvim covered the Aron, etc." This is because the chupah of Abba and Imma is the source for the chupah of Ze'er Anpin and Nukvah. Therefore, the Keruvim of Shlomo, which are the chupah of Abba and Imma, overshadow the (Aron and) the kapores, which is the chupah of Ze'er Anpin (from which the Keruvim of Moshe were made).

"Nevertheless, they too" — the Keruvim of Shlomo — "are overlaid with gold," because the ratzon that is above seichel (the level of the Keruvim of Shlomo, which were made of *atzei shemen*) clothes itself in the ratzon that is below seichel (*Torah Or, Vayakhel*). And this is the meaning of the gold—the aspect of Binah, the contemplation from which the ratzon that is below seichel is born—being a plating and a garment upon the ratzon that is above seichel, which is hinted at by the *atzei shemen*.

The reason this did not exist in the Mishkan, but only in the Beis HaMikdash, is because in the desert they were in the state of *achor b'achor*, which is only the aspect of *iskafya*.

## 110

In the Beis Hamikdash, there is the state of "face to face" (Likutei Torah, Bamidbar, discourse beginning *Vayedaber*, 63a), which is the level of *is'hapcha*. And this revelation of *Ratzon HaElyon* occurred during the three *Regalim* (Likutei Torah, *Vezos HaBerachah*, discourse beginning *Torah Tzivah*, 63a), and this is the meaning of the fact that at that time they would show the *Keruvim*.

(Likutei Torah, *Shir Hashirim*, discourse beginning *Tze'enah U're'enah*, Part I)

It was only in the Beis Hamikdash that the state of "face to face" existed (Likutei Torah, *Naso*, discourse beginning *Vayedaber*, 63a), which is *is'hapcha*. Therefore, specifically in the Beis Hamikdash—the state of "face to face"—the level of *Ratzon HaElyon* that transcends *sechel* is drawn down and revealed, and this is the concept of the *Keruvim* of Shlomo.

And this revelation of *Ratzon HaElyon* (the *ratzon* that transcends *sechel*, the level of the *Keruvim* of Shlomo) occurred during the three *Regalim* (Likutei Torah, *Vezos HaBerachah*, discourse beginning *Torah Tzivah*, 63a), and this is why at that time they would show the *Keruvim*.

(Likutei Torah, *Shir Hashirim*, discourse beginning *Tze'enah U're'enah*, Part I)

## The Menorah According to the Approach of Drush

The Menorah represents the illumination of the soul, which is the light of Hashem. As it is written, "The soul of man is the lamp of Hashem" (Mishlei 20:27). This illumination is not merely an external radiance, but an internal process of *avodah* that draws down the *Ohr Ein Sof* into the vessels of the *neshamah*.

The seven branches of the Menorah correspond to the seven *middos* of the heart. Through the *avodah* of *iskafya* and *is'hapcha*, the *Chassid* refines these *middos* until they become a vessel for the *Gilyon Elokus*. This is the inner meaning of the command, "And you shall kindle the lamps" (Bamidbar 8:2) — that the *avodah* must be performed in a manner of *hisorerus* from below, which then elicits a *hisorerus* from above.

When the *neshamah* is engaged in this *avodah*, it achieves a state of *dveikus* with the *Ribono shel Olam*. This is the purpose of the *mitzvah* of the Menorah: to demonstrate that even within the world of *Asiyah*, which is characterized by darkness and concealment, it is possible to reveal the *Ohr Ein Sof* through the *bitul* of the *nefesh* and the dedication of one's *kochos* to the service of Hashem.

## 111

**The Menorah according to Chassidic discourse**

Now, after having explained the general concept of the Beis Hamikdash and its vessels according to Chassidic discourse, we will proceed to explain the matter of the Menorah and its lamps in detail, along with the various opinions regarding this. This includes the manner in which the Menorah was positioned—whether it was placed along a north-south axis or an east-west axis—and the matter of the western lamp: whether it was the middle lamp (according to the opinion that it was placed north-south), the lamp closest to the Kodesh Hakodashim (according to the Rambam), or the second lamp from the east (according to the opinion that it was placed east-west), all according to Chassidic discourse.

By way of introduction: in the avodah of man, there are generally two types: the avodah of the mind and intellect, and the avodah of the heart and emotion. These are alluded to in the two opinions regarding the Menorah—that it was placed north-south, or east-west—as will be explained at length below. And their distinction is as follows:

## 112

Their distinction: The avodah of the mind is within one’s own intellect and reasoning. Consequently, not everyone is capable of this avodah in its ultimate sense, although to some extent, every person is obligated in this, inasmuch as he is a human being possessed of intellect and understanding.

Its foundation is that one must place the point of truth before his eyes, without inclining to the right or to the left, without the desire to force his own opinion, to be contentious, or the like.

In the life and actions of a person, this is the engagement in the nigleh and nistar of Torah. For the Torah is the wisdom of the Ribono shel Olam, and as Chazal have said, everything that a veteran student is destined to innovate* was already told to Moshe at Sinai; therefore, the Torah...

***

The avodah of the mind is within one’s own intellect and reasoning. And since there are differences in this—some possess a refined intellect while others do not—consequently, not everyone is capable of this avodah in its ultimate sense (in perfection). Although to some extent, every single person, each according to his measure, is obligated in this, inasmuch as he is a human being possessed of intellect and understanding (which is the essence of man and his superiority over the other creations in the world). Consequently, every person is obligated to serve Hashem also with his mind and intellect, according to the measure of his own intellect and understanding.

The foundation of the avodah of the mind is that one must place the point of truth before his eyes, without inclining to the right or to the left, without the desire to force his own opinion, and certainly not for the sake of being contentious or the like. Rather, on the contrary, he should desire to arrive at the *point of truth* and grasp it with his intellect and understanding, each according to his measure.

The content of the avodah of the mind in the life and actions of a person is the engagement in the nigleh and nistar of Torah. Every person is obligated to engage in the study of Torah, each according to his measure, according to the breadth of his intellect and mind. Its foundation is that he must place the point of truth of the Torah before his eyes without inclining, etc.—meaning, that the *truth of the Torah* should enter his mind and comprehension, each according to his measure, as follows.

71. By way of introduction: In addition to the fact that engagement in all matters of wisdom requires one to place the point of truth before his eyes, etc., this is emphasized even more strongly in the engagement with *Torah*.

For the Torah is the wisdom of the Ribono shel Olam, and even the matters of Torah that appear to be innovated by the intellect of the person engaging in Torah are the wisdom of the Ribono shel Olam, as Chazal have said, "Everything that a veteran student is destined to innovate* was already told to Moshe at Sinai." Therefore, the Torah...

## 113

It is eternal and unchanging (Rambam, Hilchos Yesodei HaTorah, beginning of chapter 9; and this is apparently one of the fundamental principles according to the Rambam in his Commentary on the Mishnah, Chelek, Principle 9). And our Sages said (Berachos 22a) that "words of Torah cannot contract tumah,"* as it is written: "Is not My word like fire?" It is above time and space, to the extent that our Sages said (Menachos 110a) that whoever occupies himself with the Torah of the chatas offering is considered as if he had offered a chatas, even if he does so at night or outside of Eretz Yisrael. And it is called "eternal life" (chayei olam), in contrast to "temporary life" (chayei sha'ah) (Shabbos 10a; though in Taanis 21a the text reads "life of the World to Come!"). And a person who studies Torah...

----------
*) It requires study to reconcile this with the fact that it is forbidden [to study Torah] in filthy alleyways.

## 114

When a person grasps a logical argument or a halachic ruling in Torah with his intellect, his intellect becomes unified at that moment with the Chochma of Hashem. This is because the Torah, as it exists Above, has descended below. As our Sages of blessed memory stated (Avodah Zarah 19a), "At first it is called by the name of Hashem, and finally (once he has studied it), it is called by his name," as it is written: "In the Torah of Hashem is his desire, and in His Torah..." (Tehillim 1:2). This means that the "Torah of Hashem" literally becomes "his Torah." Moreover, it becomes his to the same extent that it was initially Hashem's, to the point that he can forgo its honor (Kiddushin 32a). It is only that it has become enclothed in physical matters—Zeraim, Moed, etc., the Shas and Mishnah, which are rooted in the Torah Shebichtav—so that through this, man can grasp it.

## 115

Every man in Israel is obligated in the study of Torah (Hilchos Talmud Torah, Chapter 1, Halacha 8), and yet, it has no fixed measure even below (Kuntres Acharon to Hilchos Talmud Torah, Chapter 3). As mentioned above, although everyone is obligated in this by virtue of being a person endowed with intellect, there are many people whose minds are not well-suited for it; a thousand enter the study of Scripture, and only one emerges to teach (Vayikra Rabbah, Chapter 2). This is as explained at length in the Alter Rebbe’s Hilchos Talmud Torah, Chapter 3, Halacha 4, and in the Kuntres Acharon there, regarding the specific distinctions in this matter.

Torah is the wisdom of Hashem —and in order for a person to be able to grasp it, it must be through his own intellect and comprehension.

It is understood that for a person to be able to grasp the actual Torah of Hashem—the wisdom of Hashem—within his own intellect, the foundation must be that he places the point of the truth of Torah before his eyes, without inclining to the right or to the left, and without the desire to uphold his own opinion. Only then can he arrive at the comprehension of the truth of Torah.

**72.** After the foundation is established—that he places the point of truth before his eyes, etc.—he then arrives at the comprehension of Torah *within his own intellect*. This is something that must exist for every person in Israel, though there are differences in levels:

"Every man in Israel is obligated in the study of Torah" (Hilchos Talmud Torah, Chapter 1, Halacha 8—in the Rambam’s *Yad*), and yet, it has no measure (not only above, in the sense of "meditating upon it day and night" literally, but) "even below" (Kuntres Acharon to the Alter Rebbe’s Hilchos Talmud Torah, Chapter 3). It reaches the point where there are those who fulfill their obligation of Torah study with only one chapter in the morning and one chapter in the evening.

As mentioned above, although everyone is obligated in this (in the service of the mind and intellect) by virtue of being a person endowed with intellect, there are nevertheless many people whose minds are not well-suited for it; a thousand people enter the study of Scripture, and only one of them emerges to teach (Vayikra Rabbah, Chapter 2). Accordingly, there are differences in levels regarding the manner of the obligation of Torah study—each according to his own measure, according to the breadth of his intellect and mind—as is written at length in the Alter Rebbe’s Hilchos Talmud Torah, Chapter 3, Halacha 4, and in the Kuntres Acharon there, regarding the specific distinctions in this matter.

## 116

The avodah of the heart* and the emotions. Every single person is capable of this. However, people are not all the same; one serves out of ahavah, and another serves out of yirah. And within ahavah and yirah themselves, there are many distinctions (see Rambam, Hilchos Yesodei HaTorah 2:2, 4:12, and Hilchos Teshuvah, chapter 10). In general, they are divided into seven types, as brought in the sefarim—see also Likutei Torah, beginning of Parshas Beha'aloscha.

----------
*) Heart – Mishlei 17:16.

73. In addition to the avodah of the mind and the intellect, there is also the avodah of the heart and the emotions:

The avodah of the heart and the emotions is not like the avodah of the mind and the intellect, where not everyone is capable of reaching its ultimate goal. Rather, every single person is capable of this (to reach its goal as well). However, people are not the same as one another in the details of the middos through which this avodah is performed—one serves out of ahavah, and another serves out of yirah.

And within ahavah and yirah themselves, there are many distinctions (see Rambam, Hilchos Yesodei HaTorah, chapter 2, halachah 2; chapter 4, halachah 12; and Hilchos Teshuvah, chapter 10).

And in general, the modes of the avodah of the heart and the emotions are divided into seven types, corresponding to the seven middos, as brought in the sefarim (see also Likutei Torah, beginning of Parshas Beha'aloscha, in the explanation of the concept of the seven lamps of the Menorah).

----------
*) **Heart – Mishlei 17:16**.

## 117

The avodah of each person depends on their nature and character traits, as hinted at in Shabbos (156a): "One born under the sign of Mars (Maadim) will be a shedder of blood." Rav Ashi said: "Either a surgeon (who draws blood), a thief, a butcher, or a mohel." And on the following page (156b), it is related that the mother of Rav Nachman bar Yitzchak was told by astrologers, "Your son will be a thief." She did not let him go with his head uncovered, saying, "Cover your head so that the fear of Heaven may be upon you." This is also as written in the beginning of Hilchos Deos: "Every person has many different character traits, one distinct and far removed from another. There is a person who is... and there is a person who is... The straight path is the middle way in every trait... If one was far toward one extreme, he should distance himself toward the other extreme and conduct himself that way for a long time until... [see there]." This is also discussed at length in Chapter 4 of Shemonah Perakim.

The reason for these differences is that the avodah of each person depends on their nature and character traits, and the natures and traits of people differ from one another. As hinted at in Shabbos (156a): "One born under the sign of Mars will be a shedder of blood." Rav Ashi said: "Either a surgeon (who draws blood), a thief (a bandit who kills), a butcher, or a mohel."

And on the following page: "The mother of Rav Nachman bar Yitzchak was told by astrologers, 'Your son will be a thief.' She did not let him go with his head uncovered... 'Cover your head so that the fear of Heaven may be upon you.'" This means that due to his nature, he required a specific effort in the matter of fear of Heaven.

As the Rambam writes at the beginning of Hilchos Deos: "Every person has many different character traits, one distinct and far removed from another. There is a person who is hot-tempered... and there is a person whose mind is settled and he does not get angry at all... There is a person who is extremely haughty, and there is one who is extremely humble..." and he continues: "The straight path is the middle way in every trait," and "If one was far toward one extreme, he should distance himself toward the other extreme and conduct himself that way for a long time until...," see there, and at length in Chapter 4 of Shemonah Perakim—meaning that the avodah of each person must be in accordance with their nature.

Accordingly, there are also differences in the avodah of the heart and emotion—one serves out of ahavah, another serves out of yirah, and so on with other traits. However, the general avodah of the heart and emotion itself is such that everyone can reach its ultimate purpose (unlike the avodah of the mind and intellect, where some have a fine mind and others do not).

**Ad.** Now, since the heart and emotion (the middos) are the *actual existence of the person*, the avodah is to ensure that the person's existence is as it should be (unlike the avodah of the mind and intellect, which is a matter of going beyond oneself to see and understand *the truth that is above oneself* by setting the point of truth before one's eyes, etc.).

## 118

The foundation is that the awe of the King of Kings, the Holy One, Blessed be He, should always be before one’s eyes. This will guide him in the paths of righteousness, as mentioned above: "May the awe of Heaven be upon you." In general, this consists of two matters: the lowliness of one's self in one's own eyes—such that his desires, cravings, and entire essence occupy no space for him*—and the connection of his thought and consciousness to Hashem at all times, such that he does not wish to be separated from Him.

The distinction between these two matters is as follows: Lowliness is the preparation and qualification required for connection to be possible, so that one does not place falsehood within his soul. For otherwise, regarding any person who possesses arrogance, the Holy One, Blessed be He, says, "He and I cannot dwell in the world" (Sotah 5a).

Regarding the attainment of lowliness, a person can reach its truth through his own strength, for the truth is indeed that "what are we, what is our life," and so on. This is not the case regarding connection to his Creator; for it to be genuine, it is only through Divine grace, for...

*) The foundation and essential beginning of a person’s avodah is humility and lowliness—Lech Lecha 5655.

## 119

There is no comparison whatsoever, nor any similarity, between the Creator and the created. How, then, can a created being attain even a glimmer of understanding of the Creator, or connect itself to Him? The answer is that the Creator desired that there be knowledge of Elokus in the world, and in the light of His countenance, He gave us His Torah. As our Sages of blessed memory said (Shemos Rabbah, ch. 33): "I have sold you My Torah; it is as if I have been sold along with it," etc.*

This is true generally through all the mitzvos, for the word *mitzvah* implies *tzavta* (connection) and *chibur* (union). Through the fact that the Creator commands something to a created being, and the created being fulfills that command, a connection is forged between them.** This is also hinted at in the blessing over the mitzvos—and likewise in the blessing over the Torah in the morning blessings—where we say, "Who has sanctified us with His mitzvos." This is derived from the language of *kiddushin* (betrothal), as in "You are betrothed to Me." Through Torah and mitzvos, one becomes separated from the entire world and belongs to the Creator, to be unified with Him.***

And this means that it is only through the Torah and mitzvos, which were given by the Ribono shel Olam out of supreme kindness, that a person can truly connect with the Ribono shel Olam (and not through the person’s own power, such as through humility alone).

----------
*) Tanya, ch. 45. 
**) HaTamim, issue 1, p. 25. 
***) Tanya, ch. 46.

## Tzv"d Munachas

The concept of *Tzv"d munachas* (an acronym for *tzarich iyun, munach*—"requires further study, let it remain") is not merely a technical notation for an unresolved difficulty in the Gemara. Rather, it represents a specific state in the *avodah* of the *neshamah*. 

When a *chossid* encounters a challenge in his *avodah*—a point of *bitul* or *dveikus* that he cannot yet reconcile with his own *yeshus*—he must not force a premature resolution. To "settle" the matter through intellectual gymnastics or a superficial *hispaalus* is to bypass the truth of the *neshamah*. Instead, he must allow the difficulty to remain *munach*—placed, held, and suspended—within the *penimiyus* of his *nefesh*. 

This state of *tzarich iyun* is, in itself, a form of *avodah*. By holding the tension of the unresolved question without retreating into indifference or false certainty, the *chossid* creates a vessel. He acknowledges that his current *keilim* are insufficient to grasp the *Elokus* hidden within the difficulty. This very acknowledgment is a profound act of *bitul*, for it subjects the intellect to the reality of the *Ein Sof*, which transcends the limitations of human logic. Thus, the *munach* is not a state of stagnation, but a state of waiting—a *kavanah* that prepares the *neshamah* for a future *hisorerus* that will resolve the difficulty from a higher level of *Chochma*.

## 120

This is the basis for the two opinions regarding the Menorah—which generally alludes to a person’s spiritual avodah, in contrast to the Shulchan, upon which the Lechem HaPanim is placed, which alludes to a person’s avodah within their body and the materiality of the world, as spirituality is the light that illuminates physicality—specifically, how it was positioned.

The one who says it was positioned North-South means that he holds the essence of avodah to be that of the mind and intellect—Torah—which, in all manners of grasping and studying Torah, is the Torah of Hashem, and "the truth of Hashem endures forever," for "there is no truth but Torah."*

----------
*) "Truth—this is Torah" (*Berachos* 5b); *Yalkut Shimoni* on *Daniel* 1066: "There is no truth but Torah."

**Positioned North and South**

75. Now, the two general categories of spiritual avodah—the avodah of the mind and intellect, and the avodah of the heart and emotion (which were discussed at length previously)—pertain to the matter of the Menorah. This is the basis for the two opinions regarding the Menorah—which generally alludes to a person’s spiritual avodah, in contrast to the Shulchan, upon which the Lechem HaPanim is placed, which alludes to a person’s avodah within their body and the materiality of the world—because the essence of the Menorah is light, and therefore it alludes to spiritual avodah, since "spirituality is the light that illuminates physicality."

How was it positioned in the Beis HaMikdash—North-South or East-West? The two opinions regarding the positioning of the Menorah allude to the two types of spiritual avodah (the general matter of the Menorah): the avodah of the mind and intellect, and the avodah of the heart and emotion.

That is to say: the one who says it was positioned North-South means that he holds the essence of avodah to be that of the mind and intellect, and the one who says it was positioned East-West means that he holds the essence of avodah to be that of the heart and emotion, as will be explained below.

76. The explanation of the connection between "positioned North-South" and the avodah of the mind and intellect:

The essence of the avodah of the mind and intellect is engagement in "Torah, which in all manners of grasping and studying Torah" (even if the student’s mind is not sharp, and even if he is not in a state of purity, etc.), is the Torah of Hashem, and "the truth of Hashem endures forever", "there is no truth but Torah."*

----------
*) "**Truth—this is Torah**" — *Berachos* 5b. And in *Yalkut Shimoni* on *Daniel*, remez 1066: "**There is no truth but Torah.**"

## 121

This is as they said regarding this (Berachos 22a): "Just as there—at the time of the giving of the Torah, when 'Hashem spoke all these words'—it was with awe, etc., so too here—when anyone, whoever he may be, learns Torah—it must be with awe, etc." This applies even though there are differences in the understanding of Torah from one extreme to the other, as our Sages said (Eruvin 53a): "The heart of the early ones was like the opening of the Ulam, etc., and we, etc."

And this is why it was said that it is placed North and South, and not East and West; for from West to East there were differences in the holiness of the place in the Beis Hamikdash, and the further West one goes, the holier it is. This is as stated in the Rambam, Hilchos Beis HaBechirah, Chapter 4, that the Aron was in the West of the Kodesh HaKodashim itself, and in the Rashbam, Bava Basra (99a), s.v. *Aron*, that it was by the Western wall of the Kodesh HaKodashim. This is contrary to the opinion of Rashi, s.v. *Makom*, Yoma (21a), that it was placed in the center, and this is also the view in Tosafos, s.v. *Dochakin*, Menachos.

***

As our Sages said (Berachos 22a): "Just as there—at the time of the giving of the Torah, when 'Hashem spoke all these words'—it was with awe, etc., so too here—when anyone, whoever he may be, learns Torah—it must be with awe, etc." This is because the learning of every person is literally the word of Hashem that was spoken to Moshe from Sinai.

All of this applies even though there are differences in the understanding of Torah from one extreme to the other, as our Sages said (Eruvin 53a) regarding the differences in levels of Torah understanding: "The heart of the early ones was like the opening of the Ulam, and the later ones, etc., and we, etc." For the differences are only in the *understanding* of the Torah, but the *holiness* of the Torah (which is the Torah of Hashem, literally the word of Hashem) is equal across all levels of understanding.

And this is why he who focuses on the primary avodah of the mind and intellect—the study of Torah—said that it is placed North and South, and not East and West. For:

From West to East there were differences in the holiness of the place in the Beis Hamikdash, and the further West one goes—meaning, the closer one is to the Kodesh HaKodashim in the West, where the Aron is located, which is the place of the Indwelling of the Shechinah—the holier it is. Just as the Heichal, which is further West than the Azarah, is holier than the Azarah, and the Kodesh HaKodashim, which is further West, is holier still.

And it is the same within the Kodesh HaKodashim itself, where the Western side is holier, as the Rambam writes in the Yad (Hilchos Beis HaBechirah, Chapter 4) that the Aron was in the West of the Kodesh HaKodashim itself, and the Rashbam in Bava Basra (99a), s.v. *Aron*, writes that the Aron was by the Western wall of the Kodesh HaKodashim. This is contrary to the opinion of Rashi, s.v. *Makom*, Yoma (21a), that it was placed in the center of the Kodesh.

## 122

98: This is unlike the opinion of the Ritzba in Tosafos (Bava Basra 25a, s.v. *u’tzava*), who held that it was in the east of the Kodesh HaKodashim—unlike Tzafon and Darom, which constitute a single level of holiness. This distinction is also alluded to in the contrast between length and width*, as the Sages state that it was placed along the "width of the Beis [HaMikdash]." For the width of any thing represents its level and essence, merely expanding into various details and matters. Length, by contrast, refers to that which descends below—or ascends above—its own level [which is the praise given to Abba Shaul, Rabbi Tarfon, etc., that he was "long" in his generation (Niddah 24b)]. And so it is regarding the Hekdeshim, and so it is in Tosafos (Menachos 98b, s.v. *dochakin*), that it was placed in its center. This is unlike the opinion of the Ritzba in Tosafos (Bava Basra 25a, s.v. *u’tzava*), who held that it was in the east of the Kodesh HaKodashim—unlike Tzafon and Darom, where there were no distinctions in the holiness of the place, but rather it is a single holiness.

This is why he holds that the Menorah was placed along the north and south—because the essence of the spiritual avodah (the Menorah) is the avodah of the mind and intellect, Torah, which, in all modes of study and comprehension, is the Torah of Hashem, and it is a single holiness (like Tzafon and Darom in the Beis HaMikdash).

**57.** And it should be added that this distinction (that from west to east there are distinctions in the holiness of the place, unlike Tzafon and Darom, which are a single holiness) is also alluded to in the contrast between length and width*, as the Sages state that the vessels of the Mikdash were placed "along the length of the Beis" (east and west) or "along the width of the Beis" (north and south). In our case, the Menorah is placed along the width of the Beis, for:

The width of any thing is its level and essence, merely expanding into various details and matters.

By contrast, length refers to that which descends below—or ascends above—its own level [which is the praise given to Abba Shaul, Rabbi Tarfon, etc., that he was "long" in his generation (Niddah 24b)].

---
*) The **distinction between length and width** is explained in.

## 123

This is also true of a person, in whose length exist all the distinctions of head, body, and feet. This is as mentioned above, that in the avodah of Torah, although there are many details, nevertheless, all of them are the Torah of Hashem, in the likeness of "just as the former, etc." And therefore it is said that the Menorah is placed across the width of the Beis Hamikdash.

And in the Menorah there are seven branches, six of which emerge from the center branch—this means, according to this opinion, the Shas and Mishnah, whose source is the Torah Shebichtav, as they say, "From where are these words derived?" "The verse says," and the like*. And the center branch is—according to this opinion—the western lamp, the "testimony," whose mouth faces the west, where the Tablets of the Testimony are located.

----------
*) Torah Or, Vayakhel.

It indicates that it is higher in level, and therefore it has the capacity to descend and influence even those who are lower in level, etc. 

"And this is also true of a person"—"in whose length exist all the distinctions of head, body, and feet," where the head is higher in level, the body is below it, and the foot is at the lowest level, because the distinctions of "above" and "below" pertain specifically to length.

"And this is" what is meant by the statement that the Menorah is placed "across the width" of the Beis Hamikdash—"as mentioned above, that in the avodah of Torah, although there are" in this "many details," meaning, distinctions in levels of Torah comprehension—for one whose intellect is refined, the comprehension of Torah expands into many details and matters—"nevertheless, all of them are" details of that same level—"the Torah of Hashem." And for everyone, whoever they may be, the study is "in the likeness of" "just as the former, etc." And therefore it is said that the Menorah is placed across the width of the Beis Hamikdash—width specifically—meaning that the same level (the Torah of Hashem) expands into many details and matters.

78. And in addition to the connection between the avodah of the mind and intellect—Torah—and the location of the Menorah, which is placed north to south, the avodah of the mind and intellect is hinted at in the Menorah itself:

"In the Menorah"—"seven branches," "six of which emerge from the center branch." The matter here is that the six branches "mean—according to this opinion——the six orders of Mishnah," and their emergence from the center branch means "that their source is the Torah Shebichtav, as they say" in the Gemara, "From where are these words derived?" "The verse says," and the like*.

"And the center branch is—according to this opinion——the western lamp," "testimony," and it is called "western" because "its mouth faces the west" (as mentioned above in section 32)—since "there are located" the "Tablets of the Testimony."

----------
*) Torah Or, Vayakhel.

## 124

They are the essence of the Torah Shebichtav. The wicks of the other lamps are turned toward the middle lamp, to hint that in the avodah of the mind and intellect, one cannot deviate to the right or to the left; only the pursuit of truth must be before one’s eyes, as mentioned above. This is why all the lamps are lit specifically from the middle lamp. And the middle lamp is lit from the Mizbeach HaOlah, upon which the blood is sprinkled and the fat is burned, as mentioned above—for all fat and all blood—the taanug, the warmth, and the hispaalus of the heart—are only for Hashem. For the Torah is only sustained in one who "kills himself for it" (Berachos 63b), and "this is the way of Torah:...you shall live a life of hardship" (Avos 6:4). This is as explained in the Yad, Hilchos Talmud Torah, chapter 3, halachos 6–9, cited in Shulchan Aruch Admur HaZaken, Hilchos Talmud Torah, chapter 3, halacha 6, that one whose heart has moved him to fulfill this mitzvah—of Talmud Torah—properly, must remove the desires and pleasures of the time from his heart, and the Torah is acquired through deprivation.

***

The middle lamp, the western lamp, whose essence is "testimony," is the one toward which the wicks turn, as it represents the essence of the Torah Shebichtav.

Beyond the fact that the six branches extend from the middle branch, the wicks of the other lamps are turned toward the middle lamp to hint that in the avodah of the mind and intellect, it is impossible to deviate to the right or to the left; only the pursuit of truth must be before one’s eyes, as mentioned above.

This is also why all the lamps are lit specifically from the middle lamp (from which he would light the Menorah, from the western lamp, which is the middle lamp). This means that in addition to the fact that the wicks *turn* toward the middle lamp, their *kindling* also comes specifically from the middle lamp. This hints at the Torah Shebichtav, in which the negation of any possibility of the intellect deviating from the truth of Torah is emphasized more strongly.

The middle lamp is lit from the Mizbeach HaOlah—because:

The Mizbeach HaOlah is the altar upon which the blood is sprinkled and the fat is burned, as mentioned above, for all fat and all blood—the taanug, the warmth, and the hispaalus of the heart—are only for Hashem.

This concept is a prerequisite and condition for the study of Torah—for "the Torah is only sustained in one who 'kills himself for it'" (Berachos 63b), and "this is the way of Torah:...you shall live a life of hardship" (Avos 6:4).

This is as explained in the Yad, Hilchos Talmud Torah, chapter 3, halachos 6–9 (cited in Shulchan Aruch Admur HaZaken, Hilchos Talmud Torah, chapter 3, halacha 6), regarding the statement "you shall live a life of hardship": that "one whose heart has moved him to fulfill this mitzvah—of Talmud Torah—properly, must remove the desires and pleasures of the time from his heart, as the Sages said, 'This is the way of Torah:...you shall live a life of hardship,' etc."

## 125

"And Torah is acquired through a limitation of taanug" (Avos 6:5), and other similar statements of Chazal. It is well known that for the attainment of intellectual concepts in general, a prerequisite is the withdrawal from physical desires, etc., as hinted at by the words of Chazal (Taanis 7a): "If they were haters [of beauty], they would be wiser," as explained by Tosafos there: "If they hated beauty, they would be greater talmidei chachamim." This means that the increase in wisdom is connected (not with the negation of beauty, but) with the *hatred* of beauty, which is the essence of the matter of withdrawing from desires, etc.

This is why the lighting of the middle candle (from which all other candles are lit)—which represents the concept of Torah—is specifically from the Altar of Burnt Offering, upon which the blood is sprinkled and the fats are burned. This means that the warmth, the hispaalus of the heart, and the taanug should not be directed toward worldly matters (the removal of desires, etc.), but only to Hashem alone; this matter is the introduction and prerequisite for the study of Torah (the concept of the Menorah).

**79.** Based on the above—that the middle candle corresponds to the Torah Shebichtav and the six candles correspond to the Torah Shebe'al Peh—we can explain their distinction also regarding the time of lighting the candles:

By way of introduction: Within Torah itself, the Torah Shebichtav (the middle candle) and the Torah Shebe'al Peh (the six orders of Mishnah, the six candles) are like day and night. As it is stated in the Midrash (Shemos Rabbah, ch. 47; see the commentary *Yedei Moshe*; Tanchuma, end of Parshas Ki Sisa; Yalkut Shimoni, remez 406): "How did Moshe know when it was day and when it was night? Rather, when the Holy One, Blessed be He, taught him Mikra, he knew it was day, and when He taught him Mishnah, he knew it was night."

The connection and relevance of day to Torah Shebichtav and night to Torah Shebe'al Peh is understood—as is clearly felt, that holiness is more revealed and perceptible in Torah Shebichtav, and therefore Torah Shebichtav relates to day, light, and revelation.

## 126

In contrast, regarding Torah Shebe'al Peh, one might mistakenly think that his own intellect is the master of it. This is why at night all the lamps were lit—including the middle lamp—as the study of Mishnah also includes within it various verses from Torah Shebichtav (and regarding what is said specifically about Bavel, that it is "mingled with Scripture," see Sanhedrin 24a and the Chiddushei Aggados of the Maharsha there). Conversely, during the day, it was not lit—according to most commentators, except for the Yad, as mentioned above—only the western lamp, which is the middle lamp that alludes to Torah Shebichtav.

Torah Shebe'al Peh, whose essence is understanding and comprehension through human intellect, to the point that one might mistakenly think that his own intellect is the master of it, does not have its holiness revealed and felt to the same degree; therefore, Torah Shebe'al Peh corresponds to the night.

This is why at night, which corresponds to Torah Shebe'al Peh, all the lamps were lit—the six lamps corresponding to the six orders of Mishnah, and also the middle lamp, which corresponds to Torah Shebichtav, was included—just as the study of Mishnah includes within it various verses from Torah Shebichtav.

(And regarding what Chazal said specifically about the [Talmud] Bavli, that it is "mingled with Scripture, etc.," as it is found in Sanhedrin 24a—see the Chiddushei Aggados of the Maharsha there, who explains that the meaning is "that because of their pride, they learn in a mingled and mixed fashion according to the time, for at age five one learns Scripture, at age ten one learns Mishnah, and at age fifteen one learns Talmud, etc." However, the fact that there are various verses from Torah Shebichtav, etc., does not apply specifically to the Talmud Bavli, for the Talmud Yerushalmi is also "mingled in this manner," and the same applies to the Mishnah.)

Conversely, during the day, which corresponds to Torah Shebichtav, it was not lit—according to the opinion of most commentators (except for the Rambam in the Yad, who holds that even during the day they would light all the lamps, as mentioned above in section 1)—only the western lamp, which is the middle lamp that alludes to Torah Shebichtav.

## Discussion on the Concept of Munach

The term *munach* (literally "placed" or "resting") refers to a state of being that is established and settled. In the context of *avodah*, this signifies a level of *bitul* that is not merely a transient state of *hispaalus*—a temporary arousal—but rather a permanent, "placed" reality within the soul. 

When a *chossid* achieves a state of *munach*, it means that the *inyan* of *Elokus* is no longer an external concept that requires constant effort to grasp; rather, it has become the foundation upon which his entire *avodah* rests. This is the distinction between *hisorerus* (arousal) and *munach*. *Hisorerus* is like a flame that flickers and may be extinguished, whereas *munach* is the steady, enduring light that remains even when the initial *hispaalus* subsides. 

This is also the deeper meaning of the *taam* (cantillation note) known as *munach*. Just as the *munach* note serves as a bridge or a resting point that supports the subsequent notes, so too, the *avodah* of *munach* serves as the essential support for all further *dveikus* and *avodah* of the *neshamah*. It is the internalizing of the *inyan* to the point where it is no longer an "extra" addition to the person, but the very place where the person resides.

## 127

The second opinion is that the primary allusion of the Menorah is to the avodah of the emotions and the heart. Generally speaking, this involves seven levels, one above the other, even though there are many details within them. As it is stated regarding the attribute of humility: "Greater is what is said of Moshe and Aharon than what is said of Avraham" (Chullin 89a), and the same applies to the other middos. Furthermore, the manner of one’s avodah differs according to the nature, refinement, and holiness of his soul. This is why, according to this view, the Menorah is positioned east-to-west, such that one lamp is holier than another.

The foundation of all types of avodah is one’s hiskashrus to Hashem, for which sheflus serves as the preparation.

***

The second opinion regarding the Menorah—that it is positioned east-to-west—is that the primary allusion of the Menorah is to the avodah of the emotions and the heart.

By way of introduction: generally speaking, there are seven levels in this (the avodah of the emotions and the heart), corresponding to the seven middos, which are alluded to by the seven lamps. These seven levels are one above the other: the avodah in the attribute of sheflus (Malchus), the avodah in the attribute of hiskashrus (Yesod), and so on, up to the highest level, which is the avodah in the attribute of ahavah. This is the case even though there are many details within each of these seven levels themselves. As it is stated regarding the attribute of humility: "Greater is what is said of Moshe and Aharon than what is said of Avraham; for regarding Avraham it is written 'And I am dust and ashes,' whereas regarding Moshe and Aharon it is written 'And what are we?'" (Chullin 89a), and the same applies to the other middos.

The manner of one’s avodah also differs according to the nature, refinement, and holiness of his soul. That is to say, one whose soul is of a more refined and holy nature performs his avodah on a higher level (middah).
