This is because the concept of an oath, in its literal sense, is that in a situation where there is a concern that a person might lie and is not deterred by the prohibition involved, the imposition of an oath will cause him to confess the truth. He will not swear falsely, because an oath is a grave matter, as our Sages discuss at length in Shevuos (39a and following). Thus, through the oath, *yiras shamayim* (fear of Heaven) and *yiras cheit* (fear of sin) are awakened within him, and consequently, he is saved even from a minor transgression. This is why our Sages said (Nedarim 8a) that one may swear to fulfill a mitzvah, and it is explained in the Shulchan Aruch (Yoreh Deah, 203:6): "If he fears that his *yetzer* will overpower him and he will transgress a negative commandment, or be negligent in the fulfillment of a positive commandment, it is a mitzvah to swear or make a vow in order to spur himself on." For through an oath, deeper powers are awakened within the person—who is considered an entire world (Sanhedrin 37a)—just as we find (Shevuos 39a) that the entire world trembled when the verse "You shall not take the name of Hashem your G-d in vain" was spoken.

So too here, in the law of the Mishnah, the clarification is achieved through the swearing. For in order to know if the guidance or rebuke was effective in causing him to perform the mitzvah, it is done through an examination: whether the rebuke was given in the desired manner—meaning, that it came from the inner depths of the rebuker’s soul, and not out of hatred—as hinted by the juxtaposition of the verses (Vayikra 19:17-18), "You shall not hate... you shall surely rebuke... and you shall love your neighbor as yourself." Furthermore, that he did not rebuke him in public for the first time (Arachin 16b), and that in his rebuke there is no...

## 16

One must push him away with both hands (Sotah 47a), for if one is not careful in this, the rebuke will not achieve its purpose; on the contrary, it may cause him to add sin to his transgression, as we see tangibly.

However, if the person giving the rebuke claims, "It is all mine," and takes an oath to that effect—which is an indication that the matter touches him in the innermost depths of his soul—then the words that emerge from the heart of the one giving rebuke will surely enter the heart of the listener. As our Sages said (Berachos 6b), "Any person who possesses fear of Heaven, his words are heard." His rebuke will certainly have its effect, and he has a share in the *talis*.

Similarly, the second person, who performs the mitzvah in practice, must take an oath. This is an indication that his Torah study was not merely "from the lips outward," and the same applies to his mitzvos. For if it were otherwise, he would have no share in this *talis*, as the Torah and mitzvos would not protect him—which is the function of the "garments" as mentioned above. This is as stated in the Tosafos (Chagigah 15b) regarding Doeg and Achitophel, whose Torah did not protect them because they possessed no fear of Heaven at all. And it is said (Berachos 17a), "The ultimate goal of wisdom is teshuvah and good deeds"—the essence of Torah is that it be accompanied by teshuvah and good deeds (Rashi). The Gemara explains the outcome of learning: if it is not in such a manner—that a person does not study and review only to then rebel against his father, his mother, and his teacher, etc. But if he *takes an oath* that he has a share in the *talis*, meaning that the study of Torah and the performance of mitzvos touched him in the innermost depths of his soul, then not only is his avodah a complete avodah, but this serves as proof that he did not perform the mitzvah merely due to an arousal coming from the outside. Therefore, *they shall divide it*, because there is a participation of both of them here, and both this and that are the cause.

* * *

*One says, "It is all mine," and the other says, "Half of it is mine." The one who says, "It is all mine," shall take an oath, etc.*

The Mishnah explains a second type of person: the *beinonim*. Regarding them, our Sages said (Berachos 61b), "The *beinonim* are judged by both—the yetzer tov and the yetzer hara."

In the context of our Mishnah, this means that regarding the matter of Torah and mitzvos with which we are dealing, he is a *beinoni*. When he comes to perform them, a battle ignites in his soul, and both the yetzer tov and the yetzer hara express their opinions: whether to do it or not. Therefore, when he finally performs the mitzvah, and they come to adjudicate regarding the *talis*—the performer of the mitzvah or its reward, as discussed at length above—the guide and the one giving rebuke makes his claim with even greater intensity: *It is all mine.*

17

"Mine," and the one performing the mitzvah, who is a beinoni, argues that at the very least "half is mine," for even without the one who reproved him, his half is good.

The clarification is achieved through the taking of an oath, as mentioned above. If both take an oath, it is proof that both participated in this mitzvah, and that both have a share even in the "good half" of the beinoni. For although this half is indeed good, as stated above regarding the tzaddik, if his fellow had not reproved and aroused him, it is possible that even his good part would have remained dormant within him. Therefore, the one who reproved takes three parts, and the beinoni takes only a quarter.

* * *

There is one final category: the resha'im. Regarding them, our Sages of blessed memory said (Berachos, ibid.) that the yetzer hara judges them—meaning the yetzer hara is the sole authority for them, and it rules over them. As it is written (Sukkah 52b), at first he—the yetzer hara—is called a "wayfarer," passing by and not lodging with him (Rashi), and in the end he is called a "man," which Rashi explains as "the master of the house."

In the Mishnah, their ruling is not explicitly written, because it is learned from the ruling of the tzaddikim and beinonim. Consequently, we arrive at the principle that "we are not dealing with resha'im," since the essence of the law is known even without it (see Yevamos 33b; and see Rashi, s.v. *b'reshi'ei*, Kiddushin 33a). However, the Tosefta comes to explain the ruling for this category of people as well.

The reason for this can be understood by first addressing the nature of the Tosefta.

For every matter stated in the Tosefta, one must derive its explanation from the words of the Mishnah, as Ilfa said (Taanis 21a and Kesuvos 69b), though this requires a broad understanding. As the Rambam explains in his introduction to the Commentary on the Mishnah: "The words of our holy Rebbe in the Mishnah were concise and encompassed many matters; everything was clear to him due to the sharpness of his intellect, but to one who is lesser than him, that matter appeared deep... Therefore, one of his students, Rabbi Chiya, saw fit to compose a book... and that is the Tosefta... and it is possible to derive the reason for the Tosefta from the Mishnah after great exertion... and Rabbi Oshaya did the same."

This descent from the generation before the Mishnah to the generation of the Tosefta—which is why the Tosefta was necessary—was not only regarding the level of understanding, about which they said (Eruvin 53a) that the hearts of the early ones were like the opening of a hall, and the later ones like the opening of a sanctuary—and it concludes—"some say the early ones were like Rabbi Elazar ben Shamua"—that is, the generation before the redaction of the Mishnah, for Rabbi Elazar ben Shamua is...

## 18

Regarding the statement that Rabbi Oshaya Rabba (Yevamos 84a) was one of the compilers of the Mishnah—and later, Rabbi Oshaya Rabba, who was one of the compilers of the Tosefta (Yevamos 46b)—it is clear that in terms of the spiritual caliber of the generations, hearts diminished from generation to generation, as mentioned above. As they said—in a tone of wonder—"Are the generations fit?" And it is explained there (Chullin 93b)—according to the first interpretation—that this refers to the generation of Rabbi Meir, who was a colleague of Rabbi Elazar ben Shamua.

(Although Rabbi Meir was not the teacher of Rabbi, as stated in the Yerushalmi, Beitzah 4:2, that Rabbi only saw Rabbi Meir when he was fleeing. And Rashi—in his commentary on Eruvin 13b, s.v. *Dechazisei*—who explains it as "when I studied before him," must be interpreted this way so as not to contradict the Yerushalmi, for the text in Eruvin reads "Rav" and not "Rabbi." See also Tosafos, s.v. *Morayna*, Menachos 104a.)

This refers to the generation of Rabbi Yochanan, who was a student of Rabbi (Pesachim 3b) and of Rabbi Oshaya Rabba (Eruvin 53a). See also Shabbos (112b), "If the earlier ones..."

Therefore, the Tosefta comes to explain also the law regarding the third category—the wicked—because by the time of the Tosefta, this had become a more common occurrence due to the decline in the generation's *yiras shamayim*. Just as the general purpose of the Tosefta is to detail the words of the Mishnah because of the generation's decline in understanding.

It is worth noting the resolution of Rabbi Chaim Cohen (Tosafos, s.v. *Halacha*, Yoma 13a) that we do not raise the objection "is this a law for the Messianic era?" unless two conditions are met, etc.; see there.

The arguments in this category are as follows: The argument of the person providing guidance and rebuke is, with greater intensity and force, "It is entirely mine." And the one performing the mitzvah argues: "It is true that without the guidance and rebuke, I, on my own—the *yetzer tov* has no control over me, and the *yetzer hara* tells me, 'There is no fear of Elokus,' etc., as it is in Berachos there. But surely the purpose of the rebuke was that I specifically—and not the one rebuking—should perform the mitzvah. If so, at the very least, the portion of the *tallis* (garment) that is due for the *actual* performance of the mitzvah is mine." That is to say, of the three partners involved in every mitzvah—the *yetzer tov*, the *yetzer hara*, and the physical body of the performer—the *third*—the physical body of the performer—is *mine*. This will be explained further below.

***

—And now, regarding the category of *beinonim*, the body is not taken into account, for, in truth...

## 19

The body, in and of itself, is like a corpse; it is *batel* to the *nefesh* that animates it. Therefore, when it acts according to the will of the *nefesh* clothed within it—whether following the counsel of the *yetzer tov* in its *nefesh* or the counsel of the *yetzer hara* in its *nefesh*—the body does not count as an independent entity. This is why the *beinoni*’s claim is that "half is mine, and not two-thirds mine"—referring to the *yetzer tov* and the body. Because the *mochiach* (the one who reproved him), through his rebuke, removed the opposition of the *beinoni*’s *yetzer hara*, the body automatically acted according to the will of the *beinoni*’s *yetzer tov*. Consequently, the body does not count as an independent entity.

However, regarding *resha'im*, where the portion of the *yetzer tov* in their *nefesh* had no control over their body, and the *yetzer hara* portion incited them to sin—yet they still performed the *mitzvah* in practice due to coercion or an *hisorerus* coming from the *yetzer tov* of *another person’s nefesh*—then the body does count as an independent entity. This is because the body is only considered "nothing" in relation to the *nefesh* clothed within it, not in relation to the *nefesh* of another person, as is understood.

We can bring an example of this from the laws of the Torah:
(*Berachos* 23a) "They only taught this regarding a vessel that is their vessel"—for a vessel is close to being *batel* to them, as Rashi explains—"but regarding a vessel that is not their vessel..." And similarly there (*Berachos* 25b).
(*Shabbos* 93b) One who carries out food less than the required measure in a vessel is exempt even for the vessel, because the vessel is secondary to it.
(*Negaim* 13:9) One who enters a house afflicted with *tzara'as* with his garments on his shoulder... they become impure immediately. If he was wearing his garments... they remain pure until he stays for the time it takes to eat a *p'ras*.

***

We will explain these claims a bit further, and through this, additional clarity will be gained regarding the categories of *beinonim* and *tzaddikim*.

The *mochiach*’s claim is that the entire *tallis* is his, because the *mitzvah* was performed only through his rebuke. Even though the actual performance was done by the other person’s body, that body, in relation to the *mochiach*, is like an axe in the hand of the one who hews with it. For without the *mochiach*, he would not have been stirred to this action at all. Just as there is no room for punishment for one who is completely coerced, so too there is no room for receiving reward for such coercion, even though the *mitzvah* was performed. The Rambam has already explained at length (*Hilchos Teshuvah* ch. 5 and *Shemonah Perakim* ch. 8) that reward and punishment apply specifically where there is *bechirah* (free choice).

## 20

And naturally, there is no contradiction here from the laws of the *sugya* regarding "a person is always liable" (*Bava Kamma* 26a-b), nor from the passage in *Bava Basra* (9a) that one may take [charity] by force and it is still considered *tzedakah*, and so on.

Furthermore, regarding the *sugya* of the *beinoni* and the *tzaddik* who argues that were it not for his own *hisorerus*, he would have occupied himself only with the needs of his body, as discussed at length above, and would not have come to perform this *mitzvah*.

The *rasha* argues that, in any event, one-third of the *tallis*—the reward for the action—is his. The explanation of this claim is as follows:

The perfection of performing a *mitzvah*, whereby the reward is also a complete reward, occurs when both the *yetzer tov* and the *yetzer hara* (when one has "two hearts") agree to the action of the body, as our Sages of blessed memory said (*Berachos* 54a), "With all your heart"—with both your inclinations, with your *yetzer tov* and with your *yetzer hara*.

It is true that in *resha'im*, the *yetzer tov* has no control over their bodies, and the *yetzer hara* is in its full strength. But in absolute truth, even a *rasha*, although he has sinned, is still a Jew (*Sanhedrin* 44a). If he performs a *mitzvah* through coercion, then even though the beginning of his action was through external compulsion, it is still possible that the action itself aligns with the revelation of his will in the depths of his soul.

As is well known, this is the reason why a *get* (divorce document) coerced from a Jew is valid (*Gittin* 88b). As the *Rambam* explains in *Hilchos Geirushin* (Chapter 2, Halacha 20), stating: "Why is this *get* not nullified—a coerced *get*—since he is under duress? ... For we only say one is under duress if he is pressured and pushed to do something he is not obligated to do by the Torah. ... But one whose *yetzer hara* has overpowered him to nullify a *mitzvah* or to commit a transgression ... he has coerced himself through his own evil intent. Therefore ... since he wants to be part of Israel and wants to perform all the *mitzvos* and distance himself from transgressions, and it is his [evil] inclination that has overpowered him, once he is struck until his inclination is weakened and he says 'I want to,' he has already divorced of his own free will," end quote. See there in the *Hagahos Maimoniyos* that this law applies even to *meshumadim*.

Now, it is obvious that for the fact that he is good in his inner essence, he is not entitled to reward, because this comes to him by the nature of his birth, and it is not something over which he has free choice. But in the case at hand, he argues that the one who rebuked him weakened the power of his *yetzer*—to use the language of the *Rambam*—but the fact that the body performed the *mitzvah* is only on account of the inner essence of his soul. Therefore, the portion of the reward due to the body—which is one of the three partners that must be present in the fulfillment of a *mitzvah* as mentioned above (though generally, it is not taken into account when the *yetzer tov* or *yetzer hara* are taken into account, because it is *batel* relative to the soul clothed within it; however, this is not the case here, as mentioned above)—is due to him.

## 21

It is understood that in all categories, the oath serves as a *birur* that the *avodah* of the *yetzer hatov*, the agreement of the *yetzer hara*, and the action of the body were indeed *be'emes la'amito*. However, the primary reward is granted for the actions of the *yetzer hatov*, the *yetzer hara*, and the body, rather than for the fact that, in his essence, he is good.

* * *

The ruling is: "This one shall swear, and this one shall swear."

For the concept of an oath applies even to a *rasha*. As mentioned above, every Jew—regardless of his state—possesses an inner soul that is only good.

If both swear, the one who performed the mitzvah takes two-thirds. Through his oath, it is clarified that the strength of his *yetzer* was weakened to the point that his inner soul could be revealed. And because the other also swears regarding the remaining third—which corresponds to the body—it is clarified that the *hisorerus* and the rebuke brought about not only the weakening of the *yetzer* but also the action of the body. Thus, through the participation of both, the body arrived at the performance of the mitzvah.

* * *

One could explain that just as in the case at hand—literally—where each claims "I grabbed it all," it is also possible for one to claim, "I grabbed it, and I transferred half to my colleague, and half is mine." In that case, his status is like one who claims, "We both grabbed it together." Similarly, based on what was explained above, it is possible that even one who is in the category of a *tzaddik* might claim that the mitzvah was performed through the participation of both, and that half is his; in that case, his status is like that of the *beinoni* mentioned above. Likewise, it is possible for a *beinoni* to claim that he would have performed the mitzvah even without the help of the one who rebuked him, because his own *yetzer hatov* defeated the *yetzer hara*, placing him in the category of a *tzaddik* regarding that specific act, and therefore it is entirely his—in which case his status is like that of the first case in the *Mishnah*.

Furthermore: just as in the case at hand—literally—there are other types of claims, as stated in the *Tosefta*, "The general rule is..."—similarly, based on what was explained above, the *beinoni* could, for example, claim: "The portion of the *yetzer hatov* is entirely mine, and in the victory over the *yetzer hara* we both participated; therefore, three-quarters is mine," and other such claims.

However, the *Mishnah* and *Tosefta* only specified the primary modes: "It is all good," which corresponds generally to the category of the *tzaddik* in that matter; and "Half is good and half is bad."

## 22

And this is appropriate for the category of the *beinoni*. It is entirely evil, and only the action is performed, and it is appropriate for the category of the *rasha*.

* * *

Now, as a preface to all the categories of *tzaddik*, *beinoni*, and *rasha*, the Mishnah states: "One says, 'I found it,' and the other says, 'I found it.'"

This can be understood by first explaining the concept of a *metzi'ah* (a "find"). It refers to something that comes to a person without them having labored or exerted effort for it. In *Sanhedrin* (97a), it is stated that three things come when one is not expecting them: Mashiach, a *metzi'ah*, and a scorpion. Since this is the case, it is possible for the item to be completely beyond the person’s own level. Furthermore, it requires excellent guarding, because just as it came into their possession without effort, it is also liable to be lost easily. It is human nature to guard more zealously that which was acquired through toil. This is not the case with a *metzi'ah*, especially if it is beyond the person’s level—meaning they are not accustomed to such a thing—in which case there is an additional difficulty in guarding it.

This entire vision applies as well to the spiritual life of a person, where in their fulfillment of *Torah* and *mitzvos*, there is a portion that comes from Above by way of a *metzi'ah*.

This is in addition to the fact that regarding every *mitzvah*, our Sages have said (*Sukkah* 52b) that if Hashem did not help a person, they would not be able to overcome their inclination. We see that sometimes, suddenly and unexpectedly, thoughts of *teshuvah* fall upon a person without any preparation on their part at all, or they happen upon an opportunity for a *mitzvah* without having exerted any effort toward it. It is possible that these thoughts of *teshuvah* or this *mitzvah* are completely beyond the person’s level, and the absolute opposite of the course of their soul and their state until that moment. This is similar to what they said (*Avodah Zarah* 17a) regarding Rabbi Elazar ben Durdaya, who did not leave a single harlot in the world... One time... the harlot said, "Elazar ben Durdaya will not be accepted in *teshuvah*." He went... he placed his head between his knees and wept bitterly... Rabbi wept and said... "There is one who acquires his world in a single hour." And in the *Yerushalmi Taanis* (Chapter 1, Halacha 4), it is related that rain fell specifically through the prayer of Pentaka, who would commit five sins every day in the matter of harlotry, as he would hire harlots, etc. Yet one time it happened that in order to save a woman from that very sin, he sold his bed, etc. There are many other such examples in the *Shas*. See also *Gittin* (56a and 57b) and *Avodah Zarah* (10b and 18a) regarding non-Jews as well in this same vein.

Now, regarding opportunities and *hisorerus* that come from Above—there is no impediment. These opportunities and *hisorerus* can occur for the *tzaddik*, the *beinoni*, and the *rasha*.

## 23

And as mentioned above. Furthermore, in this regard, the guide and the one being guided, the one who rebukes and the one receiving the rebuke, are equal.

Therefore, the Mishnah does not phrase it in the context of a *metzi'ah* (found object) simply as "this one says, 'I found it,' and this one says, 'I found it.'" Rather, in the second clause, it employs the language used in the first clause regarding the laws of buying and selling (see Gemara 8a and Tosfos s.v. *Ella*; see also Chiddushei HaRashba. Perhaps it can be said that the language "it is all mine" includes both *metzi'ah* and buying and selling equally). This is because even the category of the *beinoni*—and even the category of the *rasha*—can, in the context of a *metzi'ah*, also claim: "I—and I alone—found it."

* * *

We shall now turn to the Gemara’s proposal, which requires further explanation beyond what was said above regarding the Mishnah: 

*"Why must it teach [both cases]... If it had taught [only] 'I found it,' I would have thought that 'I found it' means 'I saw it,' and even though it did not come into my possession, one acquires it through mere seeing..."*

For the matter of "seeing" (*re'iyah*) is that through it, one becomes aware of the existence of something, and one also recognizes some of the details of that thing. It is possible for this seeing to make a strong impression on the soul of the one who sees, as our Sages said (Kerisus 6a; see also Eiruvin 13b): "When you sit before your Rebbe, look at the mouth of your Rebbe." And they said (Yoma 74b): "The blind are not satisfied."

Therefore, our Sages warned so strongly about guarding against seeing evil. 

And this applies not only to intense staring, but they warned even against mere seeing. As the verse states (Yeshayahu 33:15): "And shuts his eyes from seeing evil." This is also implied in Bava Basra (57b), which states there that if there is no other path, he is considered coerced (*anus*). To the point that sometimes, seeing can have an effect even if the power of thought is not involved, as it is stated (Zavim 2:2): "A *zav* is examined in seven ways... did he have a *hirhur* (thought) before he saw, or did he see before he had a *hirhur*?"

—And see Chiddushei Aggados to Berachos (58a) and the Aruch, entry *bet-tet*, regarding whether the common expression "he set his eyes upon him and he became..." refers to actual physical sight—

However, despite all this, seeing is only a *makif* (encompassing) power; it is not an action that affects the essence of the thing itself. That is why they said that one does not acquire through seeing.

## 24

Regarding the concept of a "find"—which, as mentioned above, is an opportunity or an awakening that comes from Above—there are several ways this manifests:

Sometimes a person pays no attention, or does not even feel or perceive this awakening. This is similar to the teaching of our Sages (Sanhedrin 94a), "They do not see, but their *mazal* sees"—meaning that their soul, as it is enclothed within their body, does not perceive this awakening; only their *mazal* perceives it. At other times, a person does feel the awakening, such as when thoughts of *teshuvah* occur to them, as mentioned above.

Within this latter category itself, there are two paths: Either the awakening is effective, causing the person to turn back from their evil path and return to Hashem and His Torah—this is like someone who saw a "find" and took hold of it—or the person is moved only temporarily. Once the awakening from Above passes, the spirit of purity within them also fades away. This is seen in people who are awakened during the *Aseres Yemei Teshuvah*, a time when a person is awakened from Above, as our Sages teach (Yevamos 49b) regarding the verse, "Seek Hashem when He is to be found"—these are the ten days between *Rosh Hashanah* and *Yom Kippur*. Yet, once these days pass, they return to their old ways.

Let us explain this in a bit more detail: Suppose a person committed a sin with a woman, and later—perhaps through the blowing of the *shofar*, regarding which it is said (Amos 3:6), "If a *shofar* is blown in a city, will the people not tremble?" (see also *Yad HaChazakah*, *Hilchos Teshuvah* 3:4)—they suddenly remember this sin, feel regret, and resolve in their heart never to do it again.

Now, based on their current state, it appears to the eye that this is a complete *teshuvah*, where their willful sins have become like merits, and they are already in the category of one who "sat and did not commit the aforementioned sin," for which they are given a reward—the *tallis*—as if they had performed a *mitzvah*. However, the test of this is when they happen to be in that same time, in that same place, with that same woman. As our Sages teach in *Yoma* (86b), if they then completely forget about the awakening they had experienced and stumble again, this is proof that it was merely a fleeting glimpse; they did not take hold of the "find," and the *tallis* is not theirs.

Nevertheless, just as a visual sight can leave an impression on the soul even if it was not a deep, focused observation, and even if it occurred without the participation of the faculties of thought and intellect as mentioned above, it is possible that even with this glimpse, after some time has passed since stumbling the second time, they will remember their standing and state during the previous *Aseres Yemei Teshuvah*. This memory may then restrain them from committing that sin yet again. But even so,

## 25

At the moment of seeing, it is not acquired as a possession in one’s soul, and it is not a true matter for him, since the *hisorerus* passes and vanishes immediately. It is like someone who sees a lost object but does not take hold of it to perform an actual deed.

This is the explanation of the *sugya*: If the *Tanna* had said "I found it," I would have thought that one acquires it through mere seeing, since at the moment of *hisorerus* he stands in a state of excitement and weeps bitterly. But the *Mishnah* teaches us that he does not acquire it, for the purpose of *hisorerus* is that he should take hold of it and bring it into actuality, in *sur meira* (turning from evil) or *asei tov* (doing good).

And it concludes that "found" in the verse—referring to Torah—implies that "it came into his hand." For Torah is the Torah of truth, and "a truthful lip shall be established forever," meaning that the *hisorerus* should be a lasting impression even after the *eis ratzon* from Above has passed. This is achieved by taking hold of it, and only then is it called "finding." But in common parlance, where a person sees with his eyes, because he sees it, he says, "I found it," since at that moment he is aroused to *teshuvah* and *Torah u’mitzvos*.

* * *

And the conclusion of the *Gemara*: "The first clause refers to a lost object, and the final clause refers to a business transaction. And both are necessary; for if it had taught [only] a lost object, I would have thought that it is only regarding a lost object that the Sages imposed an oath upon him"—in order to clarify that he performed the *mitzvah* from the depths of his soul, and not merely with his lips—"because he might argue and say, 'My colleague lacks nothing in it'"—my colleague did not come to the fulfillment of the *mitzvah*, or to the *mitzvah* of *hocheiach tochiach* (rebuking), through a battle with his *yetzer* and through the exertion of soul and body, for this is for him only through an *hisorerus* coming from Above, without effort on his part. And even though for me, too, it is through an *hisorerus* from Above, I am no less than he, and therefore there is room to say, "Go and seize it," and at least, "And share it with him." And since it is possible that the *hisorerus* did not touch the inner soul of the claimant, therefore they imposed an oath upon him. "But regarding a business transaction..."

"And if it had taught [only] a business transaction, I would have thought that it is there that the Sages imposed an oath upon him"—to clarify that his exertion and effort were through his inner powers—"because he might argue and say, 'My colleague is paying a price'"—he wants to come to the fulfillment of this *mitzvah* through *avodah* out of love and fear of Hashem, with the depletion of his fat and blood—"and I am paying a price." And as it is the nature of man to magnify the value of his own exertion and *avodah*, and to diminish that of his colleague, he says, "Now that it is necessary for me"—that the *mitzvah* belongs to me because of the greatness of my exertion and the magnitude of my *avodah*—"I shall take it," entirely, because "my colleague can go and toil and buy."

## 26

He added the word "and he exerts himself" (*v'litrach*), for this is the crux of the argument: that his fellow did not exert himself as required in the performance of the mitzvah. If he had not paid the money—meaning, if his fellow had been unwilling, and it was not possible for him to perform the service fully out of love or fear—then perhaps the exertion he did put forth would suffice, for he is not within the capacity or ability to do more. But since he did pay the money, and he is considered one of those who serve Hashem, his exertion is considered as nothing, for he did not do as he was required to do. As the saying goes, "According to the camel is the load." Regarding one who performs a mitzvah but does not do so with all his strength, our Sages of blessed memory said that he is called one who "does not do the will of the Omnipresent" (*Kesuvos 66b*). If so, the tallis is not his until he goes *and exerts himself*, and then he may purchase it—
*But a lost object, etc., is necessary.*

* * *

Based on the above, we can also understand the precision in the language of the Gemara, *now that it is necessary for me* (*d'tzricha l'didi*). Seemingly, it should have said "that I need it" (*d'tzrichna lah*), as in *Taanis 9a*, "I do not need you" (*la tzrichna lach*), and as Rashi says here, s.v. *Aval*, "he needs it" (*tzrich lah*). This is unlike the phrasing "that it is necessary for me" (*d'tzricha l'didi*), which *also* admits the interpretation that the tallis is in need of me, just as "many are in need of him" (*Moed Katan 21a*).
— (And it requires further study whether there is a distinction between "it is necessary for me" (*tzricha li*) and "it is necessary for me" (*tzricha l'didi*).) —

Based on the above, that this refers to Torah and mitzvos, it works out well. For the relationship between Torah and mitzvos and the person who performs them functions in two ways: Just as a person is elevated through Torah and mitzvos—and this is the purpose of his creation, for he was created only to serve his Maker (*Kiddushin 82a*)—so too, the Torah and mitzvos, and the objects with which the mitzvos are performed, are elevated by the fact that a person performs and fulfills them.

This is why they said (*Menachos 34b*) that tefillin that have already been completed, but have not yet been worn, may be changed from tefillin shel rosh to tefillin shel yad, and it is not considered "descending from a higher level of holiness to a lower level of holiness." The same applies to other mitzvos. And regarding Torah, they said (*Kiddushin 32b* and *Avodah Zarah 19a*) that it is called by the name of the student who labored in it, and it becomes "his Torah." This is also a hint to the precision of the language in *Kiddushin 61a*, "What will become of the Torah?"—if the Torah scholar is killed—and it does not say "the study of Torah," because the Torah itself will also suffer as a result.

It is understood that the holiness and elevation that Torah and mitzvos attain is not uniform.

## 27

In every single person. This is precisely why it was said regarding Yonasan ben Uziel (Bava Basra 134a)—and similarly in Chagigah 14b—that when he engaged in Torah, any bird that flew over him was incinerated. And the Sages said regarding the breath of children (Shabbos 119b) that the breath of one who has no sin is not comparable to the breath of one who has sin. The same applies to tefillin (Shabbos 49a; see there in Tosafos, s.v. *k'Elisha*). As is known, regarding the performance of Torah and mitzvos, which must be done *lishmah*, there are many levels to this, as explained by the Rambam in *Hilchos Teshuvah*, chapter 10, halachah 5.

This is what is being discussed in our *sugya*: that by permitting himself to act, he justifies himself with the claim, "Now that it is necessary for me." On the face of it, the fact that he needs the *tallis* is the *cause* that leads him to permit it, because he is personally involved—he needs the *tallis*. However, this cannot serve as a *reason or justification* that he is permitted to take the *tallis*. But based on the aforementioned premise, this too is part of his argument: he claims that it—the *tallis*—is necessary *for me*, because specifically through my *avodah*, and not that of my colleague, it will be elevated and the reward will be greater. If so, this itself is proof that it must be—*I am the one who takes it*.

With this, it is also resolved why, regarding the permission to keep a lost object, he does not say, "Now that it is necessary for me."

* * *

And he raises the difficulty: "Let us examine the buying and selling; let us see from whom he took the money."

Explanation: The manner of buying and selling was explained above—that is, he performs the mitzvah (for which he receives the exalted reward called in the *Mishnah* a "tallis") with great toil and effort on his part. The mitzvah, or the preparation for the mitzvah, and his *ahavah* and *yirah* are like the money, through which he acquires the *tallis*. The difficulty is: the clarification of to whom the *tallis* belongs—that is, the exalted level of the reward, the principal rather than the interest—is simple: by looking at the money—the performance of the mitzvah—to see who received it.

For our Sages said (Pesachim 50b) that for those who perform Torah and mitzvos *lishmah*, "Your kindness is greater than the heavens," but for those who perform them *shelo lishmah*, it is only "up to the heavens," and the money—the performance of the mitzvah—does not reach "above the heavens," where the essence of the reward for mitzvos is found. And it has already been explained in *Hilchos Teshuvah*, chapter 10, halachah 5: "Anyone who engages in Torah in order to receive reward... is engaging in it *shelo lishmah*. And anyone who engages in it... out of love for the Master of all the earth who commanded it, is engaging in it *lishmah*." And in halachah 6 there, he writes: "And what is the appropriate love? It is that one should love Hashem with a great, exceeding, and very strong love... as He commanded us, 'with all your heart and with all your soul.'" And see there.

## 28

[reshimo]
This is also the case here. If so, by seeing which of the two is holding the tallis, we can know whose tallis it is, based on whether his avodah is from such love.

— In truth, even in the manner of performing the mitzvah, it is evident if one is doing it from love, for then he is meticulous in its performance, down to all its details and nuances, and he also beautifies the mitzvah. This is not the case when performing it to receive a reward or out of fear of punishment, where his performance is merely like paying off a debt. —

— Based on the above, we can also understand why the Gemara asks here, quite simply, "Let us see from whom he took the money," even though it is not identical to the sugya in Kiddushin (73b). This has been discussed by Rashi, Tosafos, and others. See also the *Meah She'arim* printed in the Vilna Shas. One should also note the Tosafos *d.h. V'chi* (Kesuvos 25a) and Tosafos *d.h. V'i* (Bava Kamma 57b). —

The Gemara answers: *No, it is necessary [to ask this] because he took from both of them* — (meaning that the performance of both is "above the heavens," for both are doing it *lishmah*, yet there is a distinction between them, for he took) — *one from his own volition and one against his will.*

For in avodah *lishmah* out of love, there are two general modes, similar to the distinction between the *chossid* and the *gibor* who conquers his inclination, which the Rambam explains in *Shemonah Perakim*, Chapter 6. The *chossid* is one who performs good deeds while desiring and yearning for them, whereas the one who conquers his inclination struggles with his *yetzer* and suppresses his desire.

There are also levels within avodah from love corresponding to this. As the Rambam writes in *Hilchos Teshuvah*, Chapter 10, Halacha 6: One does not love the Holy One, Blessed be He, except through the *da'as* with which he knows Him, and the love is according to the *da'as*. And in *Hilchos Yesodei HaTorah*, Chapter 2, Halacha 20: "And what is the way to love and fear Him? At the time that a person contemplates His deeds... he immediately loves." See also there, Chapter 4, Halacha 12. If so, the contemplation comes first in his intellect, until he decides in his mind that only the Holy One, Blessed be He, is worthy of being loved. And since the mind naturally rules over the heart, even if the love of Hashem is not yet revealed in his heart, the mind rules over the heart, and it is considered avodah from love, because the cause of the avodah is the love for Hashem that was born in his mind, at the very least.

However, there is still an element of *ba'al korcho* (against his will) here, both on the part of the heart of the doer—the one giving the money—and on the part of the ascent of the mitzvah and its reward "above the heavens," since the love is not revealed in his heart. This is especially true in comparison to the second level, where the contemplation acts in such a way that his heart also burns like coals of fire, and he desires with a felt yearning and longing in his heart to cleave to Him—and the giving and receiving of such money is *l'da'ato* (according to his own volition).

## 29

Entirely, and there is no element of *be’al korcho* (compulsion) here at all. However, since even in the first case the performance of the mitzvah is *lishmah*—not for the sake of receiving a reward or out of fear of punishment—it also transcends the heavens. Yet, because of the principle of *middah k’neged middah* (measure for measure), just as the performance of the mitzvah involved an element of *be’al korcho*, so too in its reward, there is, as it were, an element of *be’al korcho* on the part of the seller—the one giving the tallis—in accepting the coins. This is similar to what our Sages said regarding the acceptance of teshuvah (*Chagigah 15a*): it was said from behind the *pargod* (curtain), "Return, wayward children," except for Acher. Nevertheless, Rabbi Meir said to him, "Even after this, repent," and ultimately, he will come to the *olam haba*. As is known, this is the precision in the language of our Sages (*Yoma 85b*): "They do not provide him the opportunity to do teshuvah"—they do not *provide the opportunity* specifically, but if he pushes himself and enters, there is nothing that stands before teshuvah, as the Rambam writes in *Yad, Hilchos Teshuvah, Chapter 4, Halacha 6*.

And here, since ultimately both are above the heavens, therefore, "I do not know who is doing it of his own volition and who is doing it *be’al korcho*."

Consequently, it is impossible to clarify the matter except through the oath that the Sages imposed, and an oath is capable of clarification, for it reaches the soul more deeply than the initial *avodah* mentioned above. This is hinted at in the order of ascents from strength to strength and from one level to a higher one, in the verse (*Devarim 10:20*): "You shall fear Hashem your G-d, Him shall you serve, and to Him shall you cleave, and by His name shall you swear."

If only one of them takes the oath, it is proof that the other is the one acting *be’al korcho*, and the tallis does not belong to him, for he only performed the mitzvah due to the arousal of the one who challenged him. Or, if the one who takes the oath is the one who performed the mitzvah, it is proof that he would have performed the mitzvah regardless, for his *avodah* is higher than the *avodah* of the challenger who did not take the oath. And if both take the oath, it is proof that both are acting of their own volition—for in the *Mishnah*, the division can be true, and there is no definite liar here, etc. (see *Tosafos, s.v. Leima, 2b*).

Furthermore, the oath indicates an *avodah* from the depths of the inner soul, and in particular, it is an *avodah* through *mesiras nefesh*, whether in actuality or in potential. As our Sages said (*Berachos 61b*) in explanation of "And you shall love... with all your soul"—even if He takes your soul. Through this, one merits the most exalted reward, which is the promise stated (*Yeshayahu 64:3*; see *Berachos 34b*): "No eye has seen a G-d besides You, who will act for those who wait for Him."

## Addendum from the First Edition

## 31

[reshimo]
[/reshimo]

## 32

**Reshimo**
*The beginning of the chapter and the outline*:
Presentation of the *sugya*.
A question based on logic and *sevara*, and a precise analysis of the language.
Everything in the Torah is expounded through *Pardes*, with multiple facets in each and every part.
Proofs that even the *halachos* in *Nigleh* are expounded according to the way of *Derush* and *Remez*.
Clarification of certain concepts in this *sugya* according to their literal meaning, and their essence according to the approach of Chassidus. These are: *tallis* (internal and *makif* as the reward for *Torah* and *mitzvos*), finding a lost object (*metzi'ah*), buying and selling, an oath (*shevuah*), evidence/seeing (*re'iyah*?), "all of it," "all of it, all of it," "half of it, all of it," and "a third, all of it" — *tzricha iyun*.
Presentation of the *sugya* according to the above.

## 33

The concept of *Tzimtzum* is that it is not a literal removal of *Ohr Ein Sof*, for *Hashem* is omnipresent, as it is written, "Do I not fill the heavens and the earth?" Rather, the *Tzimtzum* is only in the perspective of the created beings. This is similar to a person who hides his face from his friend; the friend is still present, but he is unable to perceive the person's face. 

However, this analogy is limited, for in the case of a human, the hiding is external to the person. In the case of *Elokus*, the *Tzimtzum* itself is also an expression of His will. Thus, the *Tzimtzum* is not a limitation on *Hashem*, but a manifestation of His power to conceal His light so that the worlds can come into existence as distinct entities. This is the meaning of the *Zohar*'s statement that *Hashem* "contracted Himself into a point," which refers to the transition from the infinite to the finite, a process that is entirely within the realm of *Seder Hishtalshelus* yet originates from the absolute *bitul* of the *Ein Sof*.

## Beginning of Winter, Shabbos, Brooklyn.

(Beginning of Bava Metzia) "Two are holding a tallis; one says, 'I found it,' and the other says, 'I found it.' One says, 'It is all mine,' and the other says, 'It is all mine.' One says, 'Half is mine,' and the other says, 'A quarter is mine.' The one who says, 'It is all mine,' shall swear that he has no less than half of it, and the one who says, 'Half is mine,' shall swear that he has no less than a quarter of it. This one takes three-quarters, and that one takes a quarter. If two were riding upon an animal..."

And in the Tosefta—and in the Yerushalmi, end of the first chapter—"Two are holding a tallis; one says, 'It is all mine,' and the other says, 'It is all mine.' When does this apply? When both are holding it. But if it was in the hand of one of them, the burden of proof is upon the other." One says, "It is all mine," and the other says, "A third is mine." The one who says, "It is all mine," shall swear that he has no less than two-thirds, and the one who says, "A third is mine," shall swear that he has no less than a sixth. The general rule is: one only swears regarding the half that the other claims against him.

And in the Gemara: Why is it necessary to teach...? "I found it..." The entire [Mishnah] is one [case]. One says, "It is all mine," and the other says, "It is all mine." Let it teach "It is all mine," and I would know that "half is mine" [is implied]... I might have thought that "I found it" means "I saw it," and even if it did not come into my possession, I acquire it through mere sight. But the "finding" mentioned in the verse implies that it came into one's possession. However, the Tanna used common parlance... And how can you say...? The first clause deals with a lost object, and the final clause deals with business transactions. And it is necessary [to teach both], for in the case of a lost object, one might permit himself [to keep it] by saying, "My fellow is not losing anything," and in the case of business, one might permit himself [to keep it] by saying, "My fellow can go and work and buy [another]." And [the Tanna] took [examples] from both: one [where the acquisition is] by one's own volition, and one [where it is] against the other's will, and I do not know which is which.

Now, there are several points of inquiry in this sugya, and we shall mention only two of them.

First, regarding the logic:
For behold, regarding the difficulty in the case of one who says, "Half is mine," and the other who says, "A third is mine"—one should have been able to take the half by virtue of the *miggo* that, if he wanted, he could have said, "It is all mine," and taken half [of the whole] (see Tosafos, s.v. *v'zeh notel*)—they answered (Nimukei Yosef)...

[ftn_7_א] In Bava, etc.: Version 2, which is closer to the final edition.

## 34

Since, in the end, he will take half, he is content to claim in a manner that he takes his entire claim, rather than saying "the whole thing," for then he would take only half his claim. A difficulty was raised against this: the Tosefta teaches "this one says 'the whole thing' and this one says 'a third,'" where each takes half his claim. In this case, the difficulty returns to its place: he should be trusted regarding his "third," for if he wanted, he could have claimed "the whole thing" and taken more than a third. One could say that for this reason, Tosafos (and also the Nimukei Yosef, who offers another explanation) resolved the aforementioned difficulty in a different way: that he does not take half because we do not apply *miggo* to extract [property from another]. However, based on this, it is difficult: the Mishnah should also have taught the ruling of "this one says 'a third' and this one says 'a third'" to inform us that we do not apply *miggo* to extract, whereas now, were it not for the Tosefta, we would not know this, as in the Mishnah one could suggest the reason provided by the Nimukei Yosef—*miggo* that if he wanted he could have said "the whole thing" and taken more than a third. 

One could say that for this reason, the Tosafos (in the *dihur hamaschil* "v'zeh notel," 2a) were not satisfied with the explanation of the Nimukei Yosef, and they resolved the aforementioned difficulty in the Mishnah: that the reason he does not take half is because we do not apply *miggo* to extract (and see also the Nimukei Yosef for another explanation). But this remains difficult: if so, in the case of the clause "this one says 'the whole thing' and this one says 'half,'" which teaches us the logic that "they are engaging in trickery" (8a), the Mishnah should have taught the ruling of "this one says 'the whole thing' and this one says 'a third,'" so that we would know both. From the fact that he takes only a sixth—half of his claim—we would know that we do not apply *miggo* to extract (unlike in the clause of "half," where one could suggest the explanation of the Nimukei Yosef), and from the fact that he takes the sixth only with an oath, we would infer the logic that "they are engaging in trickery." 

And even if you say that the redactor of the Mishnah did not want to skip from "the whole thing" and "the whole thing" to "the whole thing" and "a third," since the claim of "the whole thing" and "half" comes after the claims of "the whole thing" and "the whole thing" in the order of the Mishnah, it is still very difficult that the Mishnah should have included the third clause of "this one says 'the whole thing' and this one says 'a third'" to inform us that we do not apply *miggo* to extract. 

One could suggest an answer by introducing the difficulty of the Gilyon Maharsha on the Gemara, which explains that the clause of "half" teaches us that he takes only with an oath because "they are engaging in trickery." He questioned this, noting that this logic is not such a great novelty, and it would have been better to say that it teaches us he takes only a fourth because we do not apply *miggo* to extract—a point he left as requiring further study. Seemingly, this difficulty can be answered based on what is written in the Mordechai at the beginning of this tractate: that in order to exempt him with an oath—especially the oath of the Mishnah, which is only a rabbinic enactment—any *miggo* is sufficient. And the fact that "they are engaging in trickery" and also that it is not a *miggo* of "if he wanted" is a greater novelty for the one who is being sued than the *miggo* of "if he wanted" to extract. Especially if we say that he holds like Rav Nachman (Kiddushin 64b) that "what reason would I have to lie" is considered like a presumption, and not like witnesses.

Another way to resolve the two aforementioned difficulties is to say that our Mishnah holds that the principle that we do not apply *miggo* to extract is an external logic and does not need to be taught. Therefore, it does not record the clause of "this one says 'a third' and this one says 'a third.'" And from the very fact that it does not teach the ruling of "this one says 'a third' and this one says 'a third' in the Mishnah, it is also necessary for the one being sued in our *sugya* (8a) that it does not stand in the Mishnah on the ruling of *miggo* to extract, for if...

## 35

It could be said that the Mishnah holds that *miggo l'hotzi* is not a valid argument, and that it is an external, superficial reasoning; therefore, it was unnecessary to teach us an extra clause for this. However, it is difficult to understand why it is taught in the Tosefta. It is forced to say that the Mishnah and the Tosefta are in dispute as to whether *miggo l'hotzi* is an external reasoning or not.

Secondly, there is a minor nuance in the language of the Mishnah, "Two are holding onto a tallis." It sounds as if it is a clear, established fact that two are holding the tallis, or at the very least, that it is a highly common occurrence. It does not say, "If two were holding onto a tallis," which would imply a scenario where it happens that two are holding it—as is concluded later in this very Mishnah: "If two were riding..." It should have either said, "Two who were holding onto a tallis," or "Two that are holding onto a tallis." The addition of the word "were" (*hayu*), or the prefix "that" (*she-*), is found frequently in our Mishnah, unlike the omission of "were" (except where there is a parallel or contrast, such as "Two say... and two say...").

We will list here only the instances where it appears with the word "two," as marked in the concordance for the Mishnah:
"There were two": Eruvin 4b, Bava Metzia 1b.
"Two that...": Peah 3:5, 5:5; Demai 6:7, 6:8; Pesachim 9:11; Yevamos 2:7; Kesuvos 13:9; Gittin 9:5; Bava Kamma 3:6; Bava Metzia 3:4; Bava Basra 10:7; Avos 3:2; Zevachim 13:3; Chullin 5:3; Negaim 5:4, 5:5; Parah 7:11, 8:1; Mikvaos 3:3.
Like here: Sanhedrin 7:11; Chullin 2:2 (and one more; to be found in the concordance).

3) The Bartenura explains that when it is a matter of a *metzi'ah* (found object), one says, "I will go and seize it and divide it with him," which implies only half. But in a business transaction, one says, "I will take it," which implies the whole.

We will present the entire *sugya* and its content in terms of the paths of *avodas Hashem*, and the aforementioned nuances—along with many other points—will be resolved, as explained further on.

*Two are holding onto a tallis.* Just as there is a *tallis* and a garment in the life of the human body...

[The Rebbe's note:]
...comes to teach us—as per the question of the Rav Akiva Eiger—it should have taught the law of *zeh omer shekulo sheli* (this one says it is all mine). For in the clause of *chatzya sheli* (half is mine), one could say as the Nimukei Yosef explains; therefore, he says that it teaches us the reasoning that "he is being cunning." This is not the case in the Tosafos (s.v. *v'zeh*), for since we already know from the Tosefta that the reasoning of the Nimukei Yosef does not apply, it explains the Mishnah as being because *miggo l'hotzi* is not a valid argument.

In any event, the law of *zeh omer shekulo sheli* is not explained in the Mishnah, nor can it be learned from the other clauses as mentioned above; whereas the Tosefta does address this law as well.

## 36

An example of this is also found in the spiritual life of every individual. This is especially true regarding the Torah and mitzvos that one performs, and their reward, as will be explained later.

Our Mishnah deals with the law of two people holding onto one object of Torah or mitzvah—meaning, there is room to say that it was accomplished through the participation of both of them together, yet each one claims that it belongs to him alone. And in what context is this dispute most common? In a *tallis*—which represents the matter of Torah and mitzvos, whose role in a person’s spiritual life is like the role of clothing in a person’s physical life.

For all of a person’s essential needs are generally divided into two categories: food and clothing. As in Yaakov’s request, "And give me bread to eat and clothing to wear" (Bereishis 28:20), and it is stated (Devarim 10:18), "And He loves the convert, to give him bread and clothing," regarding which the Ramban explains there, "These are all of man's needs."

Now, just as it is with a person in his physical life, where he requires food and clothing, so too is it in his spiritual life [Taanis 22b: "For a living soul, the soul that I have given you is living." However, that is in the context of the prohibition against fasting], he requires spiritual food and clothing in order to reach perfection and ascend higher and higher, similar to what our Sages said (end of Berachos), "Torah scholars have no rest, not in this world, etc."

The concept of food and clothing in spiritual life can be understood by their nature in physical life. Food provides vitality and strength to a person, because he eats or drinks, and the food and drink are transformed within him to become blood and flesh of his own flesh, becoming one with him. This is not the case with clothing, which merely surrounds the person’s body and protects him from cold and heat, but is a separate entity and does not unite with the person.

And in all these matters lies the primary distinction between Torah and mitzvos. The nature of Torah study is to understand and grasp the *sugya* one is learning until it is apprehended by his intellect and unites with him, becoming one. This is what is said regarding Torah (Tehillim 40:9), "And Your Torah is within my innards," and it is compared to water, wine, and milk (Yeshayahu 55; Taanis 7a), bread (Shabbos 120a; and there it is also compared to a garment, and this is because Torah study is a mitzvah, and the perfection of its fulfillment is its study because the King so commanded, and not because the intellect within it is pleasant to him, as will be explained later), and meat (Eruvin 21b; Bava Basra 22a). This is not the case with mitzvos, where none of this applies. Furthermore, the perfection of their fulfillment is because it is a royal decree, and not because the reason for the mitzvah is upright in his eyes. This is hinted at in the Mishnah (Berachos 2:2), "He should accept upon himself the yoke of the Kingdom of Heaven first, and afterwards he should accept upon himself the yoke of the mitzvos." See *Yad Melachim* at the end of Chapter 8 regarding the *z'man* of *Bnei Noach* in this regard. [And in the Midrash (Yalkut Shimoni, Parshas Re'eh) it is compared to a flesh-and-blood king who entered a province; his servants said to him, "Issue decrees..."]

## 37

[reshimo]
"Upon them I decree decrees; when they accept My sovereignty, I will decree decrees upon them, for if they do not accept My sovereignty, how can they fulfill My decrees?" (Search in Mechilta). However, mitzvos protect a person and save him from all evil, as it is written (Bamidbar 15:39), "And you shall see it... and you shall not stray..." See Sotah 21a. This is the opposite of an aveirah, regarding which our Sages said (Sotah 3a) that it "wraps itself around him." And in Zechariah (3:3-4), "Now Yehoshua was wearing filthy garments... 'Remove the filthy garments from him,' and He said to him, 'See, I have removed your iniquity from you, and I will clothe you with festive garments.'" Rashi explains that because he likened the iniquity to filthy garments, he likened the merits to festive garments—fine, white clothing (this is based on Sanhedrin 93a, and not like the commentary of the Ibn Ezra). See Shabbos 153a. [In Midrash Rabbah Koheles (9:8), "At all times let your garments be white..."—these are the mitzvos, good deeds, and Torah. (Note: There is a difficulty regarding Rashi in Shabbos 153a, who explains it as referring to the neshamah). This is also the precision in the language of the Shas (Shabbos 14a), "naked without mitzvos," as is written in Bereishis Rabbah ch. 19 regarding the verse "and they knew that they were naked"—cited in Rashi on the Torah (Bereishis 3:7)—even the one mitzvah they possessed, they were stripped of it; see there in Yefeh Toar. Note the Tanna D’bei Eliyahu Rabbah (ch. 27) on the verse (Yeshayahu 58:7), "Is it not to share your bread with the hungry... when you see the naked, cover him?"]

Furthermore, regarding Torah: because Torah study is a mitzvah, like all other mitzvos, the perfection of its fulfillment is when one studies Torah because the King of Kings so commanded, and not because the logic and reasoning within it are pleasant to him. One who says so is punished measure for measure, as our Sages said (Eruvin 64a), "Whoever says, 'This teaching is pleasant and this one is not,' destroys the wealth of Torah," for eventually even the teaching that is pleasant in his eyes will be forgotten by him. Therefore, it is also compared to clothing, as it is stated (Shabbos 120a), "And in my house there is no bread and no garment," meaning I have no mastery of Mikra, Mishnah, or Gemara.

Now, just as there is the aforementioned distinction between food and clothing regarding Torah and mitzvos—that the essence of Torah is that it unites with the person, whereas the mode of fulfilling mitzvos is because they are a royal decree (and even in Torah study, this detail exists, as mentioned above)—this same distinction is also found in the effect of Torah and mitzvos upon the soul and body of the person, and in the reward for Torah and mitzvos.

The effect of Torah study is that one’s intellect becomes more refined through it. Similarly, through the persistence of fulfilling mitzvos, many good character traits become a possession of the human soul. This is similar to what is written in the Commentary on the Mishnah (Avos 3:15), "Everything is according to the majority of the deed"—it does not say the "greatness" of the deed, for through persistence in action, a strong possession is formed in his soul. This is also understood from the statement of our Sages regarding the commission of an aveirah (Yoma 86b), that once one transgresses and repeats it, it becomes for him as if it were permitted. And they said (Avos 4:2), "A mitzvah leads to another mitzvah." And in Yoma (38b), "Once an opportunity for an aveirah comes to his hand a first time..."

## 38

"And a second time, and he does not sin again, he does not sin again." See also Rambam, Hilchos Deos 1:7: "How does a person habituate himself to these character traits until they are fixed within him? He should perform, repeat, and repeat a third time... and return to them constantly until their performance is easy for him and entails no burden, and the traits are fixed in his soul." See also there, Chapter 2, Halacha 2.

Similarly, this applies to the reward for Torah and mitzvos, which contains two aspects: analogous to food and analogous to clothing. There is a reward for Torah and mitzvos that a person who performs them attains and understands, but this is only a very small part; the essence and the principal cannot be attained except in the World to Come. As our Sages said (Pe'ah 1:1), "These are the things whose fruits a person eats in this world..."—honoring parents, etc., "and Torah study is equivalent to them all." This is not the case for most mitzvos, for "there is no reward for a mitzvah in this world." Nevertheless, even in this world, "a mitzvah protects" (Sotah 21a)—even though it is a *makif* (encompassing light) upon him, for it is not understood how the performance of a specific mitzvah protects one from sin—similar to a garment that protects a person.

Now, regarding the part analogous to food, there is not as much dispute, where one says, "It is all mine," and the other says, "It is all mine," because the unification with the intellect that one has grasped, or the acquisition of a good character trait in the soul, is within the person who actually performs the mitzvah, as is understood. Likewise, regarding those things "whose fruits a person eats in this world," where the reason given (Kiddushin 40a and Commentary on the Mishnah, Pe'ah 1:1) is that he is "good to others" and conducts himself with good behavior among people, this belongs to the one who initiated the conduct. For the second person, who educated him, rebuked him, or compelled him to perform this action—the second person himself did not conduct himself in this manner so that others would follow his conduct (as phrased in the Commentary on the Mishnah), and consequently, it is not as applicable to call him "good to others." However, regarding the part analogous to clothing, which does not unite with the person but only protects and encompasses him, and similarly regarding the reward for mitzvos that are between man and G-d, in this case, we have the scenario of "two holding onto a garment."

*And their claims are: "It is all mine," and "It is all mine."* For a person, whoever he may be—even if he is naturally diligent in his studies and naturally soft-tempered like a reed—is still (Iyov 11:12), "a wild ass of a man is born," and it is only his teacher who brings him to the life of the World to Come (Bava Metzia 33a), or one who guides him to walk in the path of the good. And even after he has grown in good deeds, the Sages said (Avos 4:14), "Exile yourself to a place of Torah... so that your colleagues may confirm it in your hands, and do not rely on your own understanding." And in Rambam, Hilchos Deos (6:1): "It is the way of man to be drawn in his character and actions after his friends and colleagues..." And just as this is true for every person in a general sense—that there is always another holding onto the garment, which is the language of "two holding onto a garment," meaning it is certainly so—so too, in specific instances, a person often performs a mitzvah or studies Torah due to an awakening that comes from another. In that case, "it is all mine" applies to both. And the claim of the second is also "all mine," and with even greater force regarding the one who educated him in his youth, since his rebuke was the proximate cause of that action.

Now, in general, there are three categories of people who perform Torah and mitzvos: the righteous, the intermediate, and the wicked. And the intention is not...

## 39

A person is not necessarily a *tzaddik* in all his matters and deeds, but rather a *tzaddik* regarding the specific matter under discussion—meaning he has no desire for this particular sin, or he is naturally drawn to perform this particular mitzvah, and he has no *yetzer hara* regarding it at all. This is similar to the Gemara in *Yoma* 69b and *Avodah Zarah* 17a: "One said to his colleague, 'Let us go to the entrance of the house of *Avodah Zarah* and kill our *yetzer*,' and [regarding] the *yetzer* of forbidden relations, it is not aroused by those who are close." See also *Rambam*, *Hilchos Deios*, Chapter 1, Halacha 4: "There is a person whose mind is settled and he does not get angry at all... there is one who is extremely humble... there is one who is very pure of heart and does not lust... Some of these traits are with a person from the beginning of his creation according to the nature of his body, and some of them... he learned from others... or he heard that this trait is good for him... and he accustomed himself to it until it was fixed in his heart." Regarding this, our Sages of blessed memory said (*Berachos* 61b): "The *tzaddikim*—the *yetzer hatov* judges them, as it is written, 'For He stands at the right hand of the needy'—because they have killed their *yetzer hara*," as is brought in the *Yerushalmi*, *Sotah* 5:5 (and Rashi’s commentary in *Berachos* requires study).

In such a case, the arguments of both sides are equal—that is, each has a strong claim. The one performing the mitzvah argues that in this matter, he did not require any guide or reprover, for he was naturally drawn to perform this mitzvah. This is similar to what is stated (*Chagigah* 9b) that one who reviews his lesson one hundred times and not one hundred and one is called "one who did not serve [Hashem]", and is also called a *tzaddik gamur*. This is because in their days, it was the custom to review every chapter one hundred times, and this was not considered "service." Alternatively, his soul is naturally repulsed by this sin; see *Bechoros* 37a: "And these are... [Makkos 23b regarding blood. *Bechoros* 37a: 'And these are things that the soul is repulsed by: carcasses, *treifos*, swarming things, and creeping things.' See also *Horiyos* 11a]." Even so, he receives reward—the *tallis* that is in his possession—as they said in *Chagigah* that also... and as it is said (*Makkos* 23b): "And if regarding blood, which a person’s soul is repulsed by, one who abstains from it receives reward, etc.—Rabbi Chananya ben Akashya says, 'He desired, etc.,'" as explained by the commentators on the Mishnah there. The one who arouses him, guides him, or reproves him also has a strong claim, for were it not for proper guidance or timely reproof, he surely would have occupied himself only with the needs of his body. For even if he is diligent in his learning by nature, he nevertheless loves his body more by nature. And our Sages of blessed memory said (*Berachos* 8a): "A person should always live in the place of his teacher, for as long as his teacher is alive, he does not forget his learning."

The ruling is [based on] the oath, etc. The concept of the oath, in its simple sense, is that in a place where there is a concern that one might lie [and according to many opinions, even if in addition to this he is suspected regarding money], and he is not deterred by the prohibition involved, then by requiring him to swear, he will admit the truth and will not swear falsely. [And according to the aforementioned opinion, even one who is suspected regarding money is not believed regarding an oath (*Bava Metzia* 5b; see there in *Tosafos* s.v. *d'chashid*, it appears, etc., whereas according to Rabbi Yehuda HaChassid there, it is different. And in *Tur Shulchan Aruch*, *Choshen Mishpat*, Siman 92, Seif 3: "And anyone who is suspected of taking..."]

## 40

Regarding the law that one who is suspected of misappropriating his fellow’s money is required to take an oath—specifically when there are witnesses that he took it, etc.—this is because an oath is a grave matter, as explained at length in Shevuos (39a and there). [It is stated (Yalkut Shimoni 825; Sifri?) that in the context of vows, it is like vowing by the life of the King, and in the context of oaths, it is like swearing by the King Himself.] The reason for this is that through an oath, more internal powers are awakened within a person. This is why it is stated (Yoreh Deah, sec. 203, 6) that if a person fears that his *yetzer* will overpower him and he will transgress a negative commandment or be negligent in fulfilling a positive commandment, it is a *mitzvah* to swear or vow in order to spur himself on.

Here, too, the clarification is achieved through the taking of an oath. For in order to know whether the guidance or rebuke successfully caused the person to perform the *mitzvah* or distance himself from the sin, one must examine whether the rebuke was delivered in the desired manner—meaning, whether it came from the depths of his soul and not out of hatred. This is hinted at by the juxtaposition of the verses (Vayikra 19:17-18): "Do not hate...," "You shall surely rebuke...," and "Love your neighbor as yourself." Furthermore, it must not be a case where he pushes him away with both hands (Sotah 47a) or rebukes him in public (Erchin 16b), for in such cases the rebuke does not achieve its purpose; on the contrary, it may even cause him to add sin to his transgression, as we see tangibly.

However, if he takes an oath, this is proof that the matter touched the innermost part of his soul. As it is said, "Words that come from the heart enter the heart." Furthermore, our Sages said (Berachos 6b), "Any person who possesses *yiras shamayim*, his words are heeded." Therefore, the rebuke has certainly taken effect, and he has a share in the *tallis*.

Similarly, the second person must also take an oath, for this serves as an indication that his Torah study was not merely lip service. The same applies to *mitzvos*, for if it were only lip service, he would have no share in this *tallis*, as the Torah and *mitzvos* would not protect him—which is the function of the "garments" mentioned above. This is as explained in Tosafos (Chagigah 15b, s.v. *Kol*): Doeg and Achitophel were not protected by their Torah study because they had no *yirah* at all. Our Sages said (Berachos 17a), "The purpose of wisdom is *teshuva* and good deeds"—the essence of Torah is that *teshuva* and good deeds accompany it, as Rashi explains. The Gemara there explains the consequences: if this is not the case, a person might read and study yet still kick at his father, mother, and teacher, etc. But when he takes an oath that he has a share in the *tallis*, it means that the study of Torah and the performance of *mitzvos* touched his inner soul, and thus he has a share in it. Consequently, they shall divide it, for there is a contribution from both of them here.

There is a second type of person: the *beinoni*. Regarding them, our Sages said (Berachos 61b) that the *beinoni* is judged by two—the *yetzer tov* and the *yetzer hara*. This means that regarding the matter of Torah and *mitzvos* with which we are dealing, a battle rages within his soul, and both the *yetzer tov* and the *yetzer hara* express their opinions in the judgment of how he should act. Therefore, when he ultimately performs the *mitzvah* and they come to adjudicate regarding the *tallis*, the one who guided and rebuked him claims, "It is entirely mine," as explained above. But the one who performed the *mitzvah*—who is a *beinoni*—argues that even without the rebuker, his "good half" would have acted, for he is a *beinoni*, and therefore half belongs to him. The clarification is, as mentioned above, through the taking of oaths.

## 41

There is one final category: the *resha'im*. Regarding them, our Sages of blessed memory stated (*Berachos* 61a) that the *yetzer hara* rules over them, meaning the *yetzer hara* is their sole authority. As our Sages of blessed memory stated (*Sukkah* 52b), the *yetzer hara* is ultimately called "man," which Rashi explains as the "master of the house."

In the *Mishnah*, their status is not explicitly written, as it is understood from the explicit principle that "we do not deal with *resha'im*," since the essence of the law is known even without this (see *Yevamos* 33b and Rashi s.v. *b'resha'im*; *Kiddushin* 33a). However, the *Tosefta* comes to explain the status of this category of people as well.

For every matter taught in the *Tosefta* can be resolved from the *Mishnah*, as Ilfa said (*Ta'anis* 21a and *Kesuvos* 69b). The Rambam writes in his introduction to the *Commentary on the Mishnah*: "The words of the *Mishnah* were brief and encompassed many matters; everything was clear to [Rabbi Yehuda HaNasi] due to the sharpness of his intellect, but to one of lesser stature, that matter appeared deep... Therefore, one of his students, Rabbi Chiya, saw fit to compose a book... and that is the *Tosefta*. It is possible to derive the reasoning of the *Tosefta* from the *Mishnah* after great exertion... and Rabbi Oshaya did the same," end quote.

This descent from generation to generation was not only regarding the level of understanding—as they said (*Eruvin* 53a), "The hearts of the early ones were like the opening of a hall, and those of the later ones like the opening of a palace," and they said there that the "early ones" refers to Rabbi Elazar ben Shamua—who was the teacher of Rebbe (*Yevamos* 84a), the compiler of the *Mishnah*—and the "later ones" refers to Rabbi Oshaya *b'Ribi*, who was one of the compilers of the *Tosefta* (*Yevamos* 46b). Rather, it also concerned the spiritual integrity of the generation; hearts diminished. As they said (*Chullin* 93b) regarding the generation of Rabbi Meir—who was a contemporary of Rabbi Elazar ben Shamua (*Sanhedrin* 14a) [even though he was not Rebbe’s teacher, as is found in the *Yerushalmi*, *Beitzah* 4:2, that Rebbe only saw him when Rabbi Meir was fleeing. And Rashi, who explains (*Eruvin* 13b s.v. *d'chazisei*) that he studied before him, must be interpreted this way so that the *Yerushalmi* does not pose a difficulty for him, as he reads "Rav" in *Eruvin*. See also *Tosafos* s.v. *mureina*, *Menachos* 104a]—compared to the generation of Rabbi Yochanan—who was a student of Rebbe (*Pesachim* 3a) and of Rabbi Elazar *b'Ribi* (*Eruvin* 53a), the compiler of the *Tosefta*—"the generations were worthy" (in astonishment). See also *Shabbos* 112b, "If the early ones..."

Therefore, the *Tosefta* comes to explain the third category as well—the explicit *rasha*—because this became a more frequent occurrence due to the decline of the generation in *yiras shamayim*. This parallels the fact that the general purpose of the *Tosefta* is to detail the words of the *Mishnah* because hearts had diminished in understanding.

The arguments are as follows: The argument of the person providing guidance and rebuke is with added intensity and strength: "How much more so!" The one being rebuked argues in response: "It is true that without the guidance and rebuke, I am as I am—the *yetzer tov* has no control, and the *yetzer hara* tells me, 'There is no fear of Elokus,' etc. But surely the action and purpose of the rebuke they gave me is that it is specifically [the rebuke]—and not the one who rebuked..."

## 42

He performs the mitzvah or studies Torah; if so, the portion that comes as a result of the actual deed belongs to him. This refers to the division between the *yetzer tov*, the *yetzer hara*, and the body that performs the act. The division of two-thirds, corresponding to the *yetzer tov* and the *yetzer hara*, belongs to the person who provides the rebuke, and the remaining third, which corresponds to the body that performs the act, belongs to the performer.

Now, regarding the category of *beinonim*, the body does not enter into the calculation, because in truth, the body in and of itself is like a corpse and is *batel* to the soul that gives it life. Therefore, when he acts according to the will of his soul—whether based on the counsel of the *yetzer tov* within his soul or the counsel of the *yetzer hara* within his soul—the body is not considered a separate entity. Consequently, the *beinoni’s* claim is for a half-share, not for two-thirds against the *yetzer tov* and the body that performs the act. However, in the case of *resha'im*, where the *yetzer hara* has incited them to sin, the *yetzer tov* within them has no control over their body. Yet, if they perform a mitzvah in practice due to an arousal and coercion coming from the *yetzer tov* of *another person*, the body does enter into the calculation. This is because the body is *batel* only to the soul that gives it life, not to the soul of another person.

We find examples of this in the laws of the Torah. For instance: (*Berachos* 23a) "They only taught this regarding a vessel that is their vessel"—Rashi explains that the vessel is close to being *batel* to them—"but regarding a vessel that is not their vessel..." and similarly there (25b). (*Shabbos* 93b) One who carries out food less than the required measure in a vessel is exempt, even regarding the vessel, because the vessel is secondary to it.
