(*Negaim* 13:11) One who enters a house afflicted with *tzaraas* with his garments on his shoulder, etc., they become tamei immediately. If he was wearing his garments, etc., they remain tahor until he stays for the duration of "the time it takes to eat a half-loaf."

The ruling is that he takes an oath, etc., because in ultimate truth, even the *rasha*, although he has sinned, is a Yisrael (*Sanhedrin* 44a), and it is within his ability to take an oath. That is to say, he can demonstrate that when he performed the Torah and mitzvos, it was in accordance with the desire of his inner soul, even though he was coerced into this performance, for in his inner essence he is good. As is known, this is the reason why a *get* (divorce document) that is coerced from a Yisrael is valid (*Gittin* 88b), as is written in the *Yad*, *Hilchos Geirushin* (Chapter 2, Law 20). See there in *Hagahos Maimoniyos* that this law applies even to *meshumadim*. Therefore, he takes an oath, etc.

Now, as a preface to all the categories of *zeh b’zeh*, the Mishnah states: "This one says, 'It is all mine,' and this one says, 'It is all mine.'"

This can be understood by first explaining the concept of *metzi'ah*, which is when something comes to a person without them having labored for it.

*Metzi'ah* and *masa u’matan* (business dealings). In *masa u’matan*, one gives payment—meaning he has labored and exerted effort to acquire it—and consequently...

## 43

"It comes to him without effort." In Sanhedrin (97a), it is stated: "Three things come unexpectedly: Mashiach, a lost object, and a scorpion." Consequently, it is possible for the matter to be entirely beyond his level. Furthermore, it requires exceptional guarding; for just as it came into his possession without toil, so too is it liable to be lost easily. It is human nature to guard with greater intensity that which one has acquired through effort. This is not the case with a "lost object" (a windfall), especially if it is beyond his level, as he is not accustomed to such a thing, and there is the additional difficulty of guarding it.

This entire phenomenon is mirrored in a person’s spiritual life. In his fulfillment of Torah and mitzvos, there is a portion that comes from Above. Beyond the fact that regarding every single mitzvah, our Sages have said (Sukkah 52b), "If the Holy One, Blessed be He, did not help him, he could not overcome it," we see tangibly that sometimes, suddenly and unexpectedly, a person is struck by thoughts of teshuvah without any preparation on his part, or he happens upon an opportunity to fulfill a mitzvah without having made any effort toward it. It is possible that these thoughts of teshuvah or this mitzvah are entirely beyond his level, and the opposite of the trajectory of his soul and his spiritual state until now. As it is related (Avodah Zarah 17a) regarding Rabbi Elazar ben Dordia, that there was no harlot he did not visit... she said to him, "Just as this [harlot's] signet will not return to its seal, so too will Rabbi Elazar ben Dordia not be accepted in teshuvah." He went and sat between two mountains and hills and wept, until his soul departed... a Heavenly Voice proclaimed, "Rabbi Elazar ben Dordia is invited to the Life of the World to Come." Rabbi [Yehudah HaNasi] wept and said, "There are those who acquire their world in one hour." And in the Yerushalmi, Taanis (Chapter 1, Halachah 4), it is related that it was demonstrated to Rabbi Avahu that Pentaka (who was notorious as a sinner) should pray and rain would fall. They asked him, "What is your profession?" He replied, "I commit five sins every day," and he described how he would hire harlots, etc. But once he saw a woman weeping, and to save her from the sin of harlotry, he sold his bed and the cloth spread over the bed [to give her the money]. And there are other such examples. See also Gittin (56a and 57b) and Avodah Zarah (10b and 18a) regarding non-Jews in a similar vein.

Now, regarding an awakening or opportunity that comes from Above—there is no impediment. It can occur for a tzaddik, a beinoni, or a rasha, as in the aforementioned Gemara. In this, the guide and the guided, the one who rebukes and the one who receives the rebuke, are equal. This is why the Mishnah does not phrase the case of a "lost object" simply as "this one says, 'I found it,' and this one says, 'I found it.'" Rather, in the second case, it does not say "this one says, 'I found it,' and this one says, 'We found it,'" but rather "this one says, 'It is all mine,' and this one says, 'It is all mine'" (and according to the simple meaning, this is because it uses language that includes negotiation as well as finding). This is because even for the category of beinonim (and even a rasha), in the case of a "lost object"—an awakening from Above—their claim is, "I—alone—found it."

***

Something acquired through effort is within one's level and is not a matter beyond one's grasp, but it is difficult to lose because one has labored for it—this is something that comes through *is'arusa d'lesata* (awakening from below). A "lost object" is something for which one has not labored; it may be an exalted matter that is not within one's level, but it is easy to lose, since of his own accord he would not have attained it—this is *is'arusa d'le'eila* (awakening from above) on the part of the person.

## 44

We now turn to the Gemara’s proposition regarding the additional *yud* [in the word *metzi’asi*], based on the aforementioned interpretation of the Mishnah:
"Why must it teach [the word *metzi’asi*]? If it had taught *ani matzasi*, I would have thought that *matzasi* means 'I saw,' and even if it did not come into my possession, I would acquire it through mere seeing. Therefore, it teaches *kullah sheli*, to indicate that through seeing alone, one does not acquire."

For the concept of *re’iyah* (seeing) is that through it, one knows of the existence of something, and also knows some of its details: its appearance, etc. And *re’iyah* can make a strong impression on the soul of the one who sees. As our Sages said (*Kerisus* 6a; see also *Eruvin* 13b): "When you sit before your teacher, look at your teacher’s mouth." And they said (*Yoma* 74b): "The blind are never satisfied." Therefore, they warned so strongly against guarding oneself from seeing evil. And this applies not only to the manner of *histaklus* (staring/gazing), regarding which our Sages said it is forbidden to gaze (*Bava Basra* 57b; *Avodah Zarah* 20a; and in the *Yad*, *Hilchos Issurei Biah* 21:21, it is forbidden to gaze, etc.; see also *Megillah* 28a), but even to mere *re’iyah*. And more than this: even if there is no participation of the power of thought, *re’iyah* can cause harm. As it is stated in the Mishnah in *Zavim* (2:2): "A *zav* is examined in seven ways... [including] whether he had a thought before he saw, or saw before he had a thought." It should be noted that the expression common in the *Shas*, "he set his eyes upon him," is interpreted by some as literal visual sight; see *Chiddushei Agados* (*Berachos* 58a) and *Aruch*, entry *bet-tes-tes*. Nevertheless, *re’iyah* is only a *koach makif* (encompassing power), and is not an action upon the essence of the thing; therefore, they said that through *re’iyah* one does not acquire.

*Re’iyah*. In *re’iyah*, we find two opposites: It is a most external power [it should be noted that the Sages ruled (*Pesachim* 26a) that sound and sight do not carry the prohibition of *me'ilah* because they have no substance—referring to the sound and the sight of the object being looked at], in that the person looking does not influence the object with his own power through this, as is the case with speech, and certainly with action, walking, and the like. On the other hand, they said (*Eruvin* 13b): "The reason I am sharper than my colleagues is that I saw Rabbi Meir from behind," and they said (*Megillah* 28a) that it is forbidden for a person to look at the image of a wicked person, and they said (*Yoma* 74b) that the blind are never satisfied. And more. [Seemingly, the passage (*Shabbos* 34a) regarding "he set his eyes upon him" is also a matter of literal *re’iyah*. But this is not the case in *Berachos* (58a) regarding Rav Sheshes "setting his eyes upon him," for Rav Sheshes was blind. And in *Chiddushei Agados* it says that the eyes of Rav Sheshes were opened. And in the *Aruch*, entry *bet-tes-tes*, there is a different version of the *Shas*; see *Beis HaShem*]. And they said (*Taanis* 24a): "A bride whose eyes are beautiful, her entire body does not require inspection." [Perhaps it should be noted...

## What

Now, regarding the matter at hand concerning the "finding"—which refers to the opportunity or the *hisorerus* (awakening) that comes from Above—there are several modes. Sometimes a person does not pay attention, or does not even feel or perceive this awakening [as our Sages of blessed memory stated (Avos 6:2) in a similar vein: "A Bas Kol goes forth from Chorev and proclaims, saying, 'Woe to the creatures for the insult to the Torah'"], and it is only in the manner of what our Sages stated (Sanhedrin 94a): "They do not see anything, but their *mazal* sees." That is, his *neshamah*, which is clothed within his body, does not see this awakening; only his *mazal* sees it.

At other times, he does see and feel the awakening, as thoughts of *teshuvah* suddenly fall into his mind without any preparation on his part, as mentioned above. Regarding this, there are two modes: Either it acts upon him so that he turns back from his evil path and returns to Hashem and His Torah—this is like someone who saw the "finding" and took hold of it. Or, the second mode: he stands in a state of *hispaalus* (emotional arousal) for the moment, but once the awakening from Above passes, the spirit of purity within him also passes and fades away. 

This is like what we see with people who are awakened during the *Aseres Yemei Teshuvah*, which is a time of awakening from Above, as our Sages stated (Yevamos 49b): "Seek Hashem when He is found..." and "When is this? Rav Nachman said in the name of Rabbah bar Avuha: These are the ten days between Rosh Hashanah and Yom Kippur." Yet, once these days pass, they return to their old ways. 

Nevertheless, similar to the "seeing of the eye," where it is possible that it makes an impression on the *nefesh* even if there was no focused contemplation and even without the participation of the power of the mind, as mentioned above—so too, in this "seeing," we have seen that it happens that in the middle of the year he will remember his standing and state during the past *Aseres Yemei Teshuvah*, and this memory will restrain him from committing a sin or lead him to perform a *mitzvah*. But even so, at the time of the "seeing," it is not acquired as a possession in his *nefesh*, and it is not a true matter for him, since the awakening passes and fades immediately. He is like a person who sees the "finding" but does not take hold of it to perform an actual deed. 

This is the explanation of the *sugya* (Talmudic discussion): "If the *Tanna* had taught 'it is found,' I might have thought..." that mere seeing constitutes acquisition, since at the time of the awakening from Above, he stands in *hispaalus* and weeps in the bitterness of his soul. Therefore, the *Tanna* taught in the *Mishnah* "all the more so," that seeing does not constitute acquisition, because the awakening comes so that he should take hold of it and bring it into actuality through *sur meira* (turning away from evil) or *asei tov* (doing good).

The Gemara then asks: "But didn't Ravnai say, 'And you have found it'—that it came into his possession?" And it answers: "Yes, 'And you have found it' in the verse—the Torah—that it came into his possession," because the Torah is the Torah of...

## 46

Truth, and a truthful lip shall be established forever. The intent of the *hisorerus*—the *metzi'ah* (finding)—that is summoned for him from Above, is that it should come into his hand. If he does not seize it, then once the *eis ratzon* passes, he remains in his original state and nature, and it is as if it never was. However, the *Tanna* employs common parlance, and because he sees it, he says, "I found it," since at that moment he is in a state of *teshuvah hisorerus*, and "man looks at the eyes" [and therefore, the common parlance—for then we were like dreamers —is that because he sees it, he says, etc.], and even though, etc.

The first clause deals with a *metzi'ah* (finding), and the final clause deals with *mako"m* (a garment of mixed fibers). And both are necessary: For if the *Tanna* had only taught the case of a *metzi'ah*, I might have thought that it is only regarding a *metzi'ah* that the Sages imposed an oath—to clarify that he performed the *mitzvah* with *kulo v'chol* (his entire being) and not merely with his lips—because he might argue and say, "My fellow lacks nothing in this"—my fellow did not come to the fulfillment of the *mitzvah* or to the guidance and rebuke through a war with his *yetzer*, through the reduction of his fat and blood, and through the exertion of soul and flesh; for he arrived at this through an *hisorerus* from Above without any effort on his part. If so, who knows if he truly grasps this *mitzvah*, if he is connected to it, and if he understands it? And even though for me, too, it is through an *hisorerus* from Above, etc., nevertheless, I am certainly no less than him. Therefore, there is room to say, "I will go and seize it and share in it"—even though the matter does not touch the claimant so deeply, for this *hisorerus* did not touch the inwardness of his soul—and therefore they imposed an oath upon him. But regarding *mako"m*, where one cannot say this, I might say [it is] not [so].

And if the *Tanna* had only taught the case of *mako"m*, I might have thought that it is only there that the Sages imposed an oath upon him—to clarify that his exertion and effort were with his internal powers—because he might argue and say, "My fellow is giving money"—that he came to this *mitzvah* through *avodah* out of *ahavas Hashem* and *yiras Hashem*—"but I, too, am giving money." And since it is human nature to magnify the value of one's own exertion and *avodah* and to diminish that of his fellow, he says, "Now that it is necessary for me"—seemingly, it should have said "that I need it" (as in *Taanis 9a* and *Rashi* here, s.v. *Aval*), and not "that it is necessary for me" (*l'didi*), which also allows for the interpretation that the *tallis* needs him. Perhaps it can be said that in that case it would have said "for it" (*lei*) and not "for me" (*l'didi*). But according to the aforementioned, that he stands within *Torah u'mitzvos*, it works out well. For just as man is in need of *Torah u'mitzvos*, and they are the purpose of his creation, for he was created only to serve his Creator (*Kiddushin 82a*), so too, the *Torah u'mitzvos* themselves are elevated by the person who performs and fulfills them. For example, the holiness of the *mitzvah* of *tefillin* rests upon the *tefillin* when a Jewish man fulfills the *mitzvah* of *tefillin* with them, as it is stated (*Menachos 34b*) that with *tefillin* that have not yet been worn, it is permitted to change from *shel rosh* to *shel yad*, and it is not considered descending from a higher holiness to a lower holiness. And similarly regarding other *mitzvos*. And regarding *Torah*, they said...

## 47

(Kiddushin 32b and Avodah Zarah 19a) In the end—after he has studied—it is called by the name of that student who labored in it. And in Kiddushin (66a), there is an analysis of the expression, "What will become of the Torah?" (if the Torah scholar is killed). This is the meaning of the expression, "It is necessary for me," to hint also at this: that the mitzvah is also "necessary" for the person. And similarly regarding many mitzvos... that they should all be fulfilled by me (Sotah 14a), "Why should I not fulfill them?" as explained in Likutei Torah, Parshas Va’eschanan—

That it belongs more to me because of my great exertion and the magnitude of my avodah—"I will weigh it"—which implies the entirety of it. This is unlike a "finding" (metzi'ah), where it says, "And he disagreed," which is as stated above. And "let my colleague go and labor"—the word "and let him labor" seems superfluous. But in truth, this is the essence of the argument that misleads him, for in his eyes, his colleague’s avodah does not contain enough exertion, and he did not labor as is required. And it would be fine if he were not "giving payment"—meaning, if he were not connected to a complete avodah out of love for Hashem—then the exertion he put in would be sufficient, for there is nothing more within his capacity. But since he *is* "giving payment," and is considered one of those who serve Hashem with love, then his exertion is considered as nothing, for he did not do as he was required to do, and "according to the camel is the burden." One could call him "one who does not do His will," as is found regarding this in Kesuvos there (66b). Only when his colleague adds exertion upon his exertion can he—"buy it." But regarding a "finding," where this does not apply—for it is an arousal coming from Above, etc.—I would say no, it is necessary.

And he raises a difficulty from the place of the source: "Let us see from whom he took the money."

Explanation: The manner of the "place of the source" was explained above, namely, that he performs the mitzvah—for which he receives the elevated reward, which is called in the Mishnah "a tallis"—through great exertion and effort on his part. The mitzvah and the preparation for the mitzvah are like the "money" through which he acquires the tallis. The difficulty is: the clarification of to whom the reward belongs—the tallis—which is, as mentioned above, the elevated aspect of the reward of the mitzvah, the "principal" and not the "interest"—is simple: to see from whom the money for performing this mitzvah was received.

For our Sages have said (Pesachim 50b): Rava raised a contradiction: It is written, "For Your kindness is great unto the heavens," and it is written, "For Your kindness is great above the heavens." (And he resolves it:) How so? Here it is regarding those who perform [mitzvos] *lishmah* (above the heavens), and here it is regarding those who perform them *shelo lishmah* (only up to the heavens, for he is at least performing the mitzvah, as Rashi explains). And "above the heavens," where the primary reward for mitzvos is, such "money" does not reach. And the Rambam has already explained this in Hilchos Teshuvah, Chapter 10, Halachah 5: Anyone who engages in Torah in order to receive a reward... this is engaging in it *shelo lishmah*, and anyone who engages in it...

## 48

One who does not act out of fear, nor to receive a reward, but rather out of love for the Master of all the earth who commanded it—this person is engaged in it *lishmah* (for its own sake). And there, in Halacha 3: "And what is the proper love? It is that one should love Hashem with a love so great, exceeding, and very intense... as He commanded us, 'with all your heart and with all your soul'..." See also there, Halacha 5. If so, by clarifying which of the two holding the tallis performs his service with such love, the tallis would also be identified as belonging to him. In truth, even in the manner of performing a mitzvah, it is evident whether the performance is out of love—in which case one is meticulous in its performance, down to all its details and particulars, and even in *hiddur mitzvah*—or if one is doing it only to receive a reward or out of fear of punishment, in which case it is evident that he is merely paying off a debt.

In any case, the difficulty regarding the *shinuya* (the change) that is not before us—that it is *nishlei* (lost/unidentifiable)—is removed. Note the Tosafot *dibur hamaschil* "V'chi" (Kesuvos 20a) and *dibur hamaschil* "V'i" (Bava Kamma 57b).

The Gemara answers: "It is not necessary, for it mentions both." This means that both are "above the heavens," because their service is *lishmah*, as mentioned above. Yet, there is a distinction between them, for it mentions one who acts "of his own volition" and one who acts "compulsorily." In service *lishmah*, out of love, there are two modes, similar to the distinction between the *Chassid* and the *Gibor* who conquers his inclination. As the Rambam writes in *Hilchos De'os*, Chapter 6, the *Chassid* is one who performs good deeds while desiring and yearning for them, whereas the one who conquers his inclination is one who fights his inclination and suppresses his desire. 

It is the same regarding service out of love. There are levels to this, as he writes in *Hilchos Teshuvah*, Chapter 10, Halacha 6, that one does not love the Holy One except through the *da'as* (intellect) with which he knows Him, and according to the *da'as*, so will be the love. And in *Yesodei HaTorah*, Chapter 2, Halacha 2, he writes: "And what is the path to His love and fear? At the time that a person contemplates His deeds... he immediately loves..." It is understood that the love will be according to the manner of the contemplation. See also there, Chapter 4, Halacha 12. 

If so, initially, the contemplation comes through his intellect, until he decides in his mind that only the Holy One is worthy of being loved, etc. And since the mind naturally rules over the heart, even if the love of Hashem is not yet revealed in his heart, the mind rules over it, and it is considered service out of love, because the cause of the service is the love for Hashem that was born in his mind, at the very least. 

However, there is still an element of "compulsion" here (both from the perspective of the heart of the one performing—the one giving the *zuzim*—and regarding the ascent of the mitzvah and its reward above the heavens, since the love is not revealed in his heart). This is especially true in comparison to the second level, where the contemplation has acted upon him such that his heart burns like fiery coals, and he desires with a palpable longing, craving, and yearning in his heart to cleave to Him, such that the giving and receiving of these *zuzim* is entirely of his own volition. But since, even in the first case, the fulfillment of the mitzvah is *lishmah* and ascends above the heavens, therefore—I do not know (by means of the *zuz* being taken) who is acting of his own volition and who is acting under compulsion. It is impossible to clarify except by the oath that the Sages imposed upon it, for through the oath, it reaches the soul more internally than the aforementioned service. And this is hinted at above.

## 50

To understand the entire matter in terms of spirituality and the life of the human soul according to Chassidus—which will, in turn, resolve several nuances and difficulties—we shall mention just two.

"Two are holding a garment: one says, 'I found it,' and the other says, 'I found it.' One says, 'It is all mine,' and the other says, 'It is all mine.' This one shall swear that he has no less than half of it, and that one shall swear that he has no less than half of it, and they divide it. If one says, 'It is all mine,' and the other says, 'Half of it is mine,' the one who says, 'It is all mine,' shall swear that he has no less than three-quarters of it, and the one who says, 'Half of it is mine,' shall swear that he has no less than a quarter of it. The former takes three-quarters, and the latter takes a quarter."

And in the Tosefta [and Yerushalmi]: "If one says, 'It is all mine,' and the other says, 'A third of it is mine,' the one who says, 'It is all mine,' shall swear that he has no less than five-sixths of it, and the one who says, 'A third of it is mine,' shall swear that he has no less than a sixth of it. The rule of the matter is: one only swears regarding half of what he claims."

Now, the Mishnah does not explain the law regarding one who says, "A third of it is mine." One cannot say that we derive it from the law in the Mishnah regarding "half is mine," for it is significantly different. The point is that regarding the one who says, "Half is mine," they asked [ ] why he should not be believed based on the *miggo* (argument of credibility) that he could have said, "It is all mine." The answer is that it is more comfortable for him to say "half is mine" and receive his entire claim, which is not the case if he says "it is all mine." If so, regarding one who says "a third is mine," the difficulty returns: why should he not be believed based on *miggo*? We must conclude that there is no *miggo* to extract [property] according to the Tosefta, whereas from the Mishnah itself we would not know this law, and it should have taught us the law of "a third is mine."

Furthermore, the nuance of the language "two are holding" (*shnayim ochzin*)—as a statement of a definite fact, not as a possible reality—implies it should have said "two who were holding" (*shnayim she-hayu ochzin*), just as it says "partners who wanted" (*ha-shutfin she-ratzu*), and not "partners want" (*ha-shutfin rotzim*). Also, in the Mishnah itself it says, "one was riding" (*echad rochev*), etc., and not as in the first clause, "one is riding" (*echad rochev*). And in the Yad [Rambam] ( ) and the Shulchan Aruch ( ) it says "who were holding" (*she-hayu ochzin*).

Before we come to explain the entire presentation of this law in terms of spirituality, we must explain the matters mentioned within it, one by one.

*Tallis* (garment) refers to clothing. In the Mishnah, it refers to a case where it has *karkashta*—that is, *tzitzis* and threads at the head of the *tallis*, and they are holding onto the *karkashta* (Bava Metzia 7a). See [ ] regarding why the Mishnah specifically uses the term "holding a *tallis*," which is unique in that it has *karkashta*. Its meaning in spirituality will be understood from its analogue in literal clothing. The function of clothing is to protect a person from cold and heat by surrounding and covering him. The general needs of a person are bread to eat and a garment to wear—sustenance and clothing. [Note: One should point out the teaching of our Sages on the verse, "And He loves the convert, giving him bread and a garment..." (Devarim 10:18): "Bread" is Torah; "garment" is *tallis*—Yalkut Shimoni 123]. And in Kesuvos ( ) regarding livelihood, and Rashi explains [ ]. The difference between them is that the person consumes the food.
